Transmittal Letter

The Hon Greg Combet

Minister for Industry and Innovation

Parliament House

CANBERRA ACT 2600

Dear Minister

I am pleased to present you with the Food Processing Industry Strategy Group (Strategy Group) report on the status and future of the Australian food processing industry. The report is the product of the inquiry commissioned by the former Minister for Innovation, Industry, Science and Research, Senator the Honourable Kim Carr, on 24 January 2011.

The Strategy Group, comprised of business, unions, research, academic and government representatives, is committed to building a more innovative, competitive and productive food processing industry. Members have engaged in highly productive dialogue and have provided you with a number of recommendations designed to assist and improve the health and sustainability of their industry.

I would like to thank my two Deputy Chairs Rebecca Dee-Bradbury from Kraft Foods and Jennifer Dowell from the Australian Manufacturing Workers Union for their constructive contributions during the course of the inquiry. I would also like to acknowledge the valuable contribution, advice and expertise of all the Strategy Group members, the former Chair, Michael Schwager and stakeholders.

In addition, I would like to thank the Departmental Secretariat team for their assistance in the preparation of the report and their services during the course of the inquiry.

Minister, I commend the Food Processing Industry Strategy Group’s report to you.

Yours sincerely

Mike Lawson

Chair, Food Processing Industry Strategy Group

Head of Manufacturing Division

Department of Industry, Innovation, Science, Research and Tertiary Education (DIISRTE)

Industry Foreword

On behalf of the Strategy Group we would like to acknowledge the foresight of the former Minister, Senator the Hon Kim Carr, in establishing the Food Processing Industry Strategy Group and thank the current Minister for Industry and Innovation, the Hon Greg Combet, AM MP for his subsequent support.

The process involved in the production of this report has demonstrated how well companies, unions, researchers, academics and governments can work together to help secure a more sustainable and robust food processing industry. Our industry makes a very important contribution to the Australian economy, particularly in regional areas through employment, business, investment and service opportunities.

Like many Australian manufacturers, the food processing industry has suffered recently from the effects of the high Australian dollar. This has compounded some systemic productivity and cost issues, as well as facilitating cheap imports that have affected some sectors significantly. These issues plus very difficult domestic market conditions have placed significant pressure on many processors, contributing to a number of factory closures and resultant job losses in Victoria, NSW, Queensland and Tasmania.

Continuing food protectionism in many countries still acts to prevent food from moving to where it is needed, and stops the market mechanism from sending appropriate signals to food producers. The current low levels of unemployment and the competition for skilled workers, exacerbated to some extent by the demands of the mining sector, are impacting on the food processing sector across Australia, particularly in some rural and regional areas. Competition between the mining sector and other manufacturing industries has made the search for scientists, chemists and engineers particularly challenging. Consultation with the food processing industry has also highlighted ready access to workers in some subsectors as a key challenge for some food processors.

Despite this, the food processing industry has consistently accounted for at least 23 per cent of employment in the Australian manufacturing sector. It is Australia’s largest manufacturing industry by gross value add, employing nearly 222 800 people currently (excluding tobacco). The industry also exported nearly $17 billion of processed food in 2010-11, or approximately 63 per cent of total Australian food exports.

The food processing industry is resilient but times are tough. There are a number of challenges and opportunities for the Australian food processing industry in the immediate future. The challenges include the impact of climate change; declining natural resources such as land and water; food security; a burgeoning human population, estimated by the United Nations to exceed more than nine billion in 2050; and questions regarding whether the growth in population will be accompanied by a necessary growth in incomes. The global decline in food processing research and development is also of concern.

The Strategy Group’s recommendations to the Australian Government aim to build long-term innovation, investment, productivity and international competitiveness in the food processing industry, and to assist it to respond to the challenges and opportunities ahead.

The recommendations are designed to:

  • Increase the level and quality of innovation
  • Improve access for Australian processed food manufacturers to new markets through a more effective use of Australia’s valued brand and a strategic alignment of assets, among other initiatives
  • Align Australia’s innovation and marketing assets behind the food processing sector so that it can take advantage of the Asian Century
  • Identify regulatory inefficiencies and program duplications that diminish the effectiveness of all stakeholders in the industry, and options to address those inefficiencies and
  • Provide options to address market place relation issues.

There is a cultural change needed in the industry here. We have no birthright to win on the world stage and we must invest in innovation, entrepreneurship and our people if we are to do so.

We would like to thank the Strategy Chair, Mr Mike Lawson and his predecessor Mr Michael Schwager, for the manner in which they directed the work of the Strategy Group and the production of the report. We are also grateful to the Strategy Group’s Secretariat for their assistance over the course of the inquiry.

We look forward to the Government’s response to our ideas and the future collaboration of business, unions, researchers, academics and government in ensuring a healthy and profitable food processing industry in the decades ahead.
Rebecca Dee-BradburyJennifer Dowell
Kraft Foods Limited ANZAustralian Manufacturing Workers Union

(From the Deputy Co-Chairs on behalf of the Food Processing Industry Strategy Group)

Terms of Reference

The Food Processing Industry Strategy Group (Strategy Group) will:

  1. Identify the current strengths and weaknesses of industry and any impediments to improving innovation, productivity and competitiveness;
  2. Report to the Minister on opportunities for enhancing/expanding the industry;
  3. Advise the Minister on strategies to overcome impediments including any changes to current programs and market failures not addressed; and
  4. Provide an industry report to the Minister within 15 months of its first meeting.

Vision

An innovative, growing, sustainable, responsive and globally competitive Australian food processing sector.

Mission

To provide expert advice and foster a common understanding of the issues facing the food processing sector and develop practical solutions lead by industry to improve innovation, increase growth, employment, productivity and competitiveness in the sector.

Executive Summary

Since the Global Financial Crisis and in particular, over the last 12 months, the food processing industry, like other Australian manufacturing industries that are trade-exposed, has been tested by an increasingly competitive environment. A sustained high dollar and a highly concentrated retail environment have placed additional pressures on already modest profit margins. Productivity has been stagnant in many parts of the food chain and there has been an insufficient focus on expanding markets - the result of an industry in survival mode.

The industry is by no means homogeneous with over
238 sub-sectors and 13 000 (mainly small) businesses. This means that issues vary, and there is not one magic bullet that can improve productivity or increase demand for each sub-sector.

Australia is a comparatively expensive place to do business - labour, land and transport costs are high, with a rigorous regulatory environment which among other things protects consumers. A flow-on effect of these factors is comparatively high food costs. This is a reflection of and a contributor to the relatively egalitarian and high quality of life we Australians enjoy. We have competitive advantages in good-quality, diverse and moderately priced beef, dairy, wheat, sugar and wine.

There are low barriers to business entry, so it is relatively easy to start a business. Australia’s stable political system and growing economy provides a good foundation for industry.

An educated and culturally diverse population means that we have the potential to be successful. Low unemployment and high wages being offered in the resource sector make attracting and retaining skilled staff difficult in some areas. A strong regional base contributes to generally positive labour relations overall. Food manufacturing companies are often major regional employers and they support the communities through employment. The Strategy Group benefited from its membership of both unions and industry, both of which felt that Australia cannot afford to be complacent in that relationship. There is a mutual benefit to companies and employees working together to improve productivity, and in skilling the work force both to maintain in and attract employees to these communities. A greater focus on the fitness for purpose of relevant courses at both certificate level and undergraduate level is needed. Management and business skills are sometimes lacking in smaller companies’ management; firm-level support provided by governments should be maintained.

There is sufficient domestic population growth to sustain the industry, and Australia’s population is growing faster than many advanced economies (expected population in 2050 is 33.9 million, although this has been informally revised downwards after the recent Census). Australians are generally proud of their food industry, but this is not enough in itself to guarantee their loyalty to Australian-made and produced food products. Australians demand value (which includes quality) for money, especially in a fragile world economic environment; their preferences are also changing to healthier, more convenient foods.

Despite these challenges, the Australian Food Industry has a unique opportunity to capitalise on opportunities in our region. It is critical that Australian manufactured foods ride the wave of the demand for Australian produce from Asia. Asia’s incomes as well as its populations are growing, which means that Asians’ desire for processed foods not just raw ingredients is growing. We export a significant amount of processed foods but the majority is not substantially transformed, reducing the potential benefit to Australians.

The phenomenon of working families and dietary changes mean that families of the burgeoning Asian middle-classes too are looking for convenient, safe and healthy food. The Australian brand is a trusted one and provides an ideal starting entry point into these growth markets.

Multinationals located in Asia should not be the only beneficiary of the ‘Asian Century’. Small and Medium Enterprises (SMEs) can collaborate with multinationals, learn from their success and follow in their slipstream. Retailers too can be a vehicle for penetrating growing Asian markets.

The Australian brand is a quality hallmark and more can be made of the opportunity to use it both in Australia and overseas. The Strategy Group feels there is merit in industry investigating what works in Asia in terms of Australian branding and how it could be used to assist SMEs and multinationals operating out of Australia to capture Asian markets.

The term ‘Made in Australia’ is misunderstood, and the Strategy Group supports the review of the substantially transformed definitions across legislation to greater reflect public perceptions of this term, especially as a large segment of the Australian community wants to support Australian jobs and farmers.

There are insights that can come from Australia’s increasingly diverse population and we need to understand the dietary differences and preferences of our key markets. Without extensive investment in market research, small businesses cannot hope to have the insight into these needs. Partnerships are needed, with either each other or multinationals, to understand and enter these markets.

More strategic engagement with Austrade is needed. Firms can be assisted to determine whether they are export ready, and successful exporters can provide leadership and know-how. There is a need for greater coordination to tap into a mammoth Asian market through networks and solid distribution channels. The Government will continue to focus on reducing tariffs for processed foods including through Free Trade Agreement negotiations. Australian Governments should also continue to help businesses that are trying to expand their markets by assisting them with regulatory design that minimises trade barriers.

Australia has outstanding research capabilities in food-related areas especially in its major public research organisation Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation (CSIRO), and universities, particularly those that are in receipt of Australian Research Council funding. Stronger linkages between industry and these institutions, and greater prioritisation from industry and policy-makers, are needed. Research is needed to help reduce input cost and quality inconsistencies, improve production processes, reduce wastage, extend shelf-lives and produce healthier ingredients.

The Strategy Group has recommended creation of a Food Innovation Hub/Network to act as a catalyst for more collaboration between researchers and industry; improve industry capabilities especially SMEs; assist with information failures related to supply chains; improve insights (especially into Asia); and attract foreign investment. Governed by four key stakeholders - academia, governments, industry and researchers, it can help deliver greater insight, oversight and prioritisation of food research; improve commercialisation from research; attract more talent to the industry; better coordinate strategic market incursions with Austrade; and help to understand better the behind-the-border barriers to entry into these markets.

Our regulatory system has given us a unique advantage in terms of food safety. Over the years the Commonwealth, States and Territories, and New Zealand have come together to try to reduce inconsistencies in the regulatory landscape. However, more can be done to reduce inconsistencies and remove ineffective, overlapping or unnecessary regulation. The mantra must be ‘minimum effective regulation’ based on evidence of market failure. This, plus a commitment to the Regulatory Impact Statement process, is needed to maintain quality and safety while improving productivity. The Strategy Group believes strongly that the Commonwealth should assume powers from the States in the areas of regulation common with New Zealand, to remove inconsistencies and remove potential incentives for manufacturers to relocate to other countries.

The regulation of emerging technologies, such as gene technology and nanotechnology, is based on science. Governments and scientists should show leadership in communicating the benefits of applying these emerging technologies in food production, emphasising the sound scientific evidence upon which their use is regulated.

Support for business from governments needs to be better coordinated - the hub and network could act as a coordinating point for support along the entire food chain.

The industry needs to continue to work together to limit the effect of its own imposts, such as Quality Assurance systems, where there is significant overlap and cost. At the same time it could look at risks in its own supply chain systems to better improve traceability and assurance.

There are benefits of small increases in productivity through the value chain multiplying exports. Distance to large markets means high transport costs reduce our competitiveness. Port, road and rail infrastructure need to be efficient and effective, but this is a common requirement for the entire manufacturing sector and food chain. Industry needs to continue to build on its efforts to improve product and raw material consistency, emerging technologies, extending shelf-lives and reducing waste.

The report was developed during a period when a number of allegations had been made against retailers regarding misuse of market power and unconscionable conduct. The Australian Competition and Consumer Commission (ACCC) is currently investigating these complaints expeditiously.

Without diminishing the effect of these complaints, the Strategy Group felt the best path for the moment was a new pact between the industry and the retail sector - appreciating each other’s pressures and strengths. The retail sector needs a healthy, innovative and quality supply chain, and the manufacturers want fair access to the consumer and fair terms of trade. If the retail sector has aspirations of entering Asia, it will want a solid supply base. All parts of the supply chain must find productivity improvements to deliver benefits to the consumer. Profits must be sufficient to allow for reinvestment in capital and innovation. Short-term thinking benefits few in the longer term. Small and medium manufacturers must have an opportunity to thrive and innovate with a degree of independence. To this end the Strategy Group has recommended all parties cooperate to review the Produce and Grocery Industry Code of Conduct. Government intervention through regulation is only recommended should this fail to improve productivity and competition in the market, and as a last resort.

Without a sufficient level of scale, small and medium companies cannot hope to enter foreign markets. Even large manufacturers find themselves increasingly small compared to the scale of overseas plants, hence making them uncompetitive. Collaborations by businesses within an industry to collectively negotiate terms with common suppliers, or merger activities, can potentially raise competition concerns. The Competition and Consumer Act 2010 contains provisions which allow businesses to seek ACCC authorisation to collaborate and achieve scale provided there is sufficient public benefit. Measures should be taken to improve industry awareness of these provisions. Governments also need to ensure barriers to entry for new players in the industry - both retailers and manufacturers - are low to ensure competition.

There must be enough profit incentives for companies to reinvest in Australia, especially in replacing out of date, unscaled plant and equipment. The Strategy Group recommends accelerated depreciation should be targeted to these items to ensure productivity gains rather than cars and office equipment, a point recently acknowledged and prioritised by the Prime Minister at the Prime Minister’s economic forum held in Brisbane in mid-June 2012.

The industry must imagine a different future for itself, focused on long-term strategic engagement with our Asian neighbours. Manufacturers, unions, academia and research institutes must work together with governments to capitalise on its strengths and opportunities.

There are significant opportunities for driving growth and competitiveness in Australia’s food processing industry. Critical to this success will be effective partnerships between business, unions, government and research communities. The Food Innovation Hub/Network would be a catalyst for enhanced collaboration which would assure the future of the industry.

Recommendations

The industry transformation strategies and recommendations for the Australian processed food industry are informed by four main themes:

  • Innovation
  • New markets and the Australian brand
  • Regulatory reform and program efficiencies
  • Marketplace relations

Innovation

Recommendation 1 - Industry, governments, research institutes and universities collaborate to create a Food Innovation Hub/Network, incorporating a strategic planning and intelligence function, and a virtual network.

Recommendation 2 - Industry to develop proposals to utilise the Australian Research Council (ARC) Competitive Grants Program, particularly Linkage Funding Schemes, the proposed Food Innovation Hub/Network, and work with universities and the VET sector to promote current R&D and food industry courses.

New Markets and Australian Brand

Recommendation 3 - Government to provide assistance and information that will facilitate exports and firm-level information be used to help match businesses to export opportunities.

Recommendation 4 - Consideration be given to regulatory clarification to define substantial transformation in relation to food products, taking into consideration consumer perceptions of meaning of the term ‘Australian Made’ and Australia’s international obligations. Industry and Government to test the reputation of Australian food in Asian markets and investigate the most appropriate brand mark for Australian products in the various Asian markets. An Asia Pacific marketing campaign to be developed by Industry to promote Australian processed foods.

Regulatory reform and program efficiencies

Recommendation 5 - Taking into account the analysis and recommendations from the Productivity Commission study: Performance Benchmarking of Australian and New Zealand Business Regulation: Food Safety (December 2009), the Government review regulatory arrangements between the Commonwealth and the States, in particular undertake a cost-benefit analysis of the Commonwealth taking over regulation of food processing and safety.

Recommendation 6 - Government to identify all programs at Commonwealth and state level and to identify opportunities to improve the business design of such programs to ensure that they complement each other and are delivered more efficiently.

Recommendation 7 - Australian Food and Grocery Council (AFGC) convene a forum to undertake a cost-benefit analysis of the current level of quality assurance (QA) auditing of the processed food industry and to develop recommendations on alternative QA options that reduce the regulatory burden on business - retail and manufacturing - without compromising food standards.

Recommendation 8 - Governments develop a statement explaining their approach to the use of emerging technologies in food, which supports the safe development and application of emerging technologies.

Marketplace Relations

Recommendation 9 - The food processing industry, together with the retailers, to review the Produce and Grocery Industry Code of Conduct (PGICC) to see if it is fit for purpose or if an enhanced PGICC and/or stronger role for the Ombudsman is required to effect behavioural change in the retail sector.

Recommendation 10 - Department of Industry, Innovation, Science, Research and Tertiary Education (DIISRTE) will work, in consultation with the ACCC, with industry bodies to increase industry awareness of the authorisations process for mergers and collective bargaining under the Competition and Consumer Act 2010 (CCA) as a means of achieving economies of scale and cost reductions through mergers, acquisitions, asset sharing, and collective bargaining.

1. Introduction

On 24 January 2011, the then Minister for Innovation, Industry, Science and Research, Senator the Hon Kim Carr, announced the establishment of the Food Processing Industry Strategy Group, bringing together key representatives of Australia’s food manufacturing industry, the union movement, the scientific community, academia and government.

In his announcement Senator Carr noted the importance of the food processing industry, Australia’s largest manufacturing sector. He made reference to ‘a time of change’, a need to step up to the new responsibilities for the future of the food processing sector. He indicated that the formation of the Strategy Group would provide a clearer assessment of the impact of our research and development investments on the sector and build new capabilities.

1.1 The Scope of This Report

The report recognises the important role played by the food processing industry in Australia. It is a vital component of the economy and was Australia’s largest manufacturing industry in 2010-11, accounting for nearly 22 per cent of manufacturing gross value added. In 2008-09 the Australian processed food and beverage industry spent around $427.8 million on research and development, of which $421.2 million was business-funded. Australia’s net processed food exports were $6.54 billion in 2010-11.

The industry also makes a significant contribution to regional communities through employment, business, investment and service opportunities. It consistently accounts for at least 23 per cent of employment in the Australian manufacturing sector; in May 2012 employment in the industry was approximately 222 800 (excluding tobacco).

The viability of the industry is inextricably linked to the price of raw materials and other input costs, a fact that has been emphasised in the numerous inquiries that have been held into the industry over the past few decades. The report attempts to build on the results of these earlier inquiries, acknowledging in some cases, how little has changed.

Despite its success, the food processing industry is currently subject to structural change and a range of challenges. These have placed pressure on it to develop proactive strategies if it is to flourish in the longer term, especially by engaging with burgeoning Asian markets.

The Strategy Group’s approach has been to focus on direct and practical solutions designed to enhance prosperity and productivity of all those involved in the sector through increased levels of innovation and competitiveness.

While the Strategy Group has attempted to tailor its recommendations to the Industry and Innovation portfolio, it has not hesitated to comment on other portfolio’s policy areas where these have resulted in barriers for the industry. In the main the Strategy Group’s ideas are practical and cost-neutral.

Consistent with the Strategy Group’s Mission Statement, a key long-term aim of the work of the Strategy Group has been to assist the food processing industry to become self-sufficient in investment, innovation, and collaboration, and in determining its own long-term strategic direction. This is in line with the policy of the Government to intervene only where there has been market failure, and where the benefits of such an intervention can be demonstrated to outweigh the costs and be in the public interest.

In general the Strategy Group’s approach has been to address aggregated sectoral issues rather than those faced by discrete sub-sectors. This is because of the complexity and diversity of the industry with over 13 015 businesses, of which 12 823 are small and medium-sized enterprises as at June 2011 - covering ten Australian and New Zealand Standard Industrial Classification (ANZSIC) groups and 238 classes.

The review recognises that Australian food and beverage manufacturers may continue to struggle with the high value of the Australian dollar for the foreseeable future, and that Australia will remain a comparatively small market with high input costs in a number of areas. The Strategy Group remains optimistic about the long-term viability of the industry and believes that Governments have a critical role in driving innovation and helping to reduce the cost of doing business.

1.2 Past Government Involvement

Australian Governments have traditionally sought to develop a more competitive and sustainable food processing sector and to increase innovation and investment in the industry. The food processing industry has been the subject of numerous government initiatives, policies and strategies over the past decade. The most significant Commonwealth Government Food Programs (1992-2012) are at Appendix A.

In 1988 an Industries Assistance Commission report indicated that Australia’s food regulation system was impeding the development of an efficient and competitive export industry, and was constraining the adoption of emerging technologies and product innovation because of inherent costs and impediments. 1 This report provided an incentive for government action by numerous federal governments over the subsequent decades.

In 1992 the Commonwealth Government oversaw the establishment of an Agri-Food Council (AFC). The objectives of the AFC included increasing the access of Australian agri-food companies to the Asian market, improving freight distribution, and improving packaging.2 The Agri-Food Industry Program received funding of $9.9 million over five years.

In 1996 Prime Minister John Howard replaced the AFC with the Supermarket to Asia (STA) program.3 This, in turn, was terminated in 2002 and replaced with the National Food Industry Initiative.4 A five-year National Food Industry Strategy (NFIS) operated from 2002-07.

In 2007 the Commonwealth Government announced a revamping of the NFIS to continue its support of the industry with funding of $75.7 million over four years. The four programs under the NFIS were administered by the Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry (DAFF). They included:

  • the Food Innovation Grants Program ($54.2 million)
  • the Technical Market Access Program ($15.0 million)
  • the International Food Standards Initiative ($2.5 million)
  • Australian Food Industry Council ($4.0 million).

From 2008 to 2010 government support for industry innovation decreased, primarily as a result of completion of the Food Innovation Grant (FIG) scheme. This scheme was replaced by the Regional Food Producers Innovation and Productivity Program (RFPIPP).5 The four-year, $35 million RFPIPP was established to boost the productivity and competitiveness of Australia’s regional food and seafood industries through innovation and technology improvements. The program is due to end on 30 June, 2012.

Numerous reports and recommendations have accompanied these programs and strategies. In addition, the Australian Parliament has actively examined a range of food industry issues. These include:

  • Senate Economics References Committee Inquiry into the Food Standards Amendment (Truth in Labelling Laws) Bill 2009
  • Senate Economics References Committee Milking it for all it’s worth - competition and pricing in the Australian dairy industry 2010
  • Senate Agricultural and Related Industries, Select Food Production in Australia 2010
  • Senate Economics References Committee Interim report: The impacts of supermarket price decisions on the dairy industry (presented out of session on 20 April 2011)
  • House of Representatives Economics Committee Food Standards Amendment (Truth in Labelling - Palm Oil) Bill 2011
  • House Standing Committee on Agriculture, Resources, Fisheries and Forestry Competition and Consumer Amendment (Horticulture Code of Conduct) Bill 2011
  • Senate Select Committee on Australia’s Food Processing Sector 2012 (current).

The Senate Select Committee on Australia’s Food Processing Sector is due to report in 2012 on matters including the viability and competitiveness of, and the regulatory environment for, Australia’s food processing sector. The terms of reference for the inquiry specifically address matters such as Government regulations regarding labelling and taxation in the context of food processing and manufacturing.

1.3 Current Government Initiatives

In Australia, the main agents for transfer of locally developed technology are the universities, and public research organisations (PRO) such as the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation (CSIRO). However, the Commonwealth Government has provided a number of incentives for companies to engage in innovation and research, as well as other programs to encourage investment and develop business skills. In addition to those current programs mentioned in Appendix A, some initiatives are outlined below.

The Research and Development (R&D) Tax Incentive

The Research and Development (R&D) Tax Concession was introduced in 1985 to encourage Australian industry to undertake more research and development activities. During the period from 26 November 2007 to 30 September 2010, 642 food-related companies registered for the R&D Tax Concession. The concession was a broad-based and market-driven program that enabled companies to deduct up to 125 per cent of eligible expenditure incurred on R&D activities when lodging their income tax returns. Interestingly, in the period 2000 to early 2011, many subsectors where Australia enjoys a competitive advantage were least successful in attaining the R&D tax concession, e.g. dairy; sugar and wine.

The R&D Tax Incentive replaced the R&D Tax Concession from 1 July 2011, and officially became law on 8 September 2011. It provides targeted tax offsets to encourage more Australian businesses to engage in R&D activities, and is open to all businesses in all industry sectors.

There are two new components of the program, which aim to provide greater financial benefits for eligible R&D activities. These include:

  • a 45 per cent R&D refundable tax offset to eligible entities with an aggregated turnover of less than $20 million
  • a non-refundable 40 per cent R&D tax offset to all other eligible entities.

The program is jointly administered by the Australian Tax Office (ATO) and AusIndustry.

Australian Research Council (ARC)

The ARC was established by the Australian Research Council Act 2001. It is a statutory authority under the Public Service Act 1999 and a prescribed agency under the Financial Management and Accountability Act 1997.

In 2010-11 the ARC had a total operating budget of $20.895 million, with additional administered funding of $5.728 million for implementation of the Excellence in Research for Australia (ERA) initiative. The ARC has also received $2.574 million to fund IT systems to support the ERA initiative.

The ARC administers the Australian Government’s National Competitive Grants Program (NCGP). In 2010-11 NCGP funding was estimated to total around $708.733 million. Together with the ERA funding, this accounted for 7.9 per cent of the Australian Government’s 2010-11 $8.923 billion commitment to science and innovation.

Through the NCGP the ARC is currently administering funding provided to almost 4650 research projects Australia-wide, involving more than 10 200 researchers.

The National Collaborative Research Infrastructure Strategy (NCRIS)

The National Collaborative Research Infrastructure Strategy (NCRIS) is a $542m, seven-year strategy spanning from 2004-05 to 2010-11. NCRIS provides and supports major research infrastructure, allowing Australia to conduct world-class research. In November 2006, twelve priority areas (capabilities) were announced, and projects have since been funded in each of these areas.

In 2009-10, the Department conducted an evaluation of the NCRIS Program. The assessment concluded that the NCRIS model is appropriate for funding medium- to large-scale, capability-based research infrastructure and has been effective in meeting research infrastructure needs.

The Strategic Roadmap for Australian Research Infrastructure was released in September 2011 and further develops Australia’s research capacity and improves innovation and research outcomes over the next five to ten years.

Commercialisation Australia

Commercialisation Australia is a merit-based, competitive assistance program delivered by the Australian Government that provides a new, integrated, hands-on approach to take products, processes and services to market by offering a range of tailored assistance measures for specialist advice and services, proof of concept and early stage commercialisation activities.

Commercialisation Australia has funding of $278 million over the five years to 2014, with ongoing funding of $82 million a year thereafter.

Enterprise Connect

The Enterprise Connect Food Processing Industry Support Network is a specialised national group. It brings together business advisers with specialist expertise and networks, and helps Australian businesses in the food processing sector which transform products for consumers, and other up-stream processors. It is targeted at improving operations, productivity, competitiveness and sustainability by providing professional business advice, development services and linkages.

National Food and Nutrition Leaders’ Science Forum (NFNLSF)

The National Food and Nutrition Leaders Science Forum (NFNLSF), supported by the CSIRO, was established by Government in 2011 within the Primary Industries Standing Committee (PISC) framework. The NFNLSF’s mechanism is to facilitate better coordination of activities and information sharing between Research, Development and Technology Transfer (RD&TT) participants, and to link industry, research providers and investors and other relevant stakeholders.

The R&D Strategy being developed by the NFNLSF aims to identify the key challenges and develop R&D priorities for the food and nutrition sector to meet the future needs of the industry and government, including a plan for implementation. The Strategy will be integrated with current initiatives and other agricultural Research and Development Extension (RD&E) sectoral and cross-sectoral strategies under the umbrella of the National RD&E framework. The main aim of this framework is to develop a more coordinated and collaborative approach to rural RD&E, and ensure national research capability will be focused, used efficiently and effectively to achieve the best outcome and uptake by industry.

Clean Technology Food and Foundries Program

The Clean Technology - Food and Foundries Investment Program provides up to $150 million over six years to food and beverage manufacturers to invest in energy-efficient equipment and low-pollution technologies, processes and products.

A merit-based grants program, it will assist the Australian food and beverage manufacturers to maintain competitiveness in a carbon-constrained economy. The grant assistance available is dependent on the size of the grant and the turnover of the applicant.

Cooperative Research Centres

The Cooperative Research Centre (CRC) program was established in 1991, with a focus on delivering economic, environmental and social benefits to Australia. In particular, the program is aimed at developing partnerships between researchers and end users to address issues.

The Minister for Tertiary Education, Skills, Science and Research is responsible for the CRC program. The CRC committee, appointed by the Minister, oversees a merit-based application process, monitoring, and evaluation of the program, with particular emphasis on aligning CRC projects with appropriateness for funding. CRCs have played a role in developing solutions for the food industry, a list of food related CRCs can be found at Appendix A.

State and Territory programs

Each State and Territory, and in some cases, discrete areas within a State (e.g. the Gippsland Food Plan) deliver programs, initiatives and assistance tailored to region-specific needs. While it cannot be argued that these programs do not provide benefit to the industry there does seem to be a lack of integration, coordination and integrated design between the different levels of government in some cases.

1.4 National Food Plan

The Australian Government committed to developing a national food plan during the 2010 election. The development of the food plan involved a community discussion about Australia’s food and the issues affecting it. An issues paper was released in June 2011 and public consultations followed.

The Issues paper noted that there are currently many government policies, programs and regulations to address food-related policy issues. However, no overarching food policy framework exists.

The development of a national food plan aims to integrate food policy along the whole food supply chain - from paddock to plate. The plan will acknowledge the important role played by State, Territory and local governments in monitoring the supply and consumption of food.

On 17 July 2012, Minister Ludwig released the National Food Plan Green Paper. The Green Paper will provide stakeholders with a chance to contribute further through written submissions and public consultations being conducted from July 2012 to 30 September 2012. The Strategy Group’s Report will feed into the development of the National Food Plan White Paper.

1.5 Food Processing Industry Strategy Group Activities

The Food Processing Industry Strategy Group comprises 16 representatives from the processed food industry covering multinationals and the small business community, key representatives from the trade union sector, research and academia. The Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry (DAFF) had observer status.

The Strategy Group was originally chaired by Michael Schwager, Head of the Industry and Small Business Policy Division. Following an internal department restructure, the new Chair was Mike Lawson, Head of the Manufacturing Division, Australian Government Department of Industry, Innovation, Science, Research and Tertiary Education (DIISRTE). There were two Deputy Chairs: Ms Rebecca-Dee Bradbury, Chief Executive Officer of Kraft Foods Australia New Zealand, and Ms Jennifer Dowell, National Secretary, Food and Confectionary Division, Australian Manufacturing Workers’ Union. A full list of members is at Appendix B.

The Strategy Group has been supported by a small secretariat within DIISRTE.

The Strategy Group met on six occasions. The secretariat also had bilateral discussions with member’s out-of-session and working groups also met to brainstorm and develop recommendations proposed within the report.

As part of the preparation of the report, it was necessary to assemble data and information on the industry to assist the Strategy Group in its review. The Strategy Group engaged consultants to:

  • collect specific data, e.g. access to a food innovation web site
  • conduct an industry survey of the food processing industry to gain insight into issues
  • conduct studies on competitiveness issues relating to the high volume, low value add Australian flour and food oil sectors
  • conduct a review of the food packaging sector and
  • plan scenarios, using a Centre for International Economics (CIE) economic modelling tool developed in collaboration with the CSIRO.

The Strategy Group completed a high-level analysis to assess the strengths and weaknesses of the industry, examined the impediments to the industry’s future development and has proposed strategies through which those impediments may be overcome.

The Strategy Group also developed Group and Industry Key Performance Indicators (KPIs) - see Appendix C.

This final report by the Strategy Group addresses the key challenges confronting the Australian food processing industry and makes practical recommendations to industry and governments to promote and increase levels of innovation, productivity and competitiveness in the sector.

 

1 Parliamentary Library, Food Regulation in Australia - A Chronology, 18 September 2001, p15

2. Economic Contribution of the Food Processing Sector

This chapter describes the significance, the structure and economic performance of Australia's food processing industry and the global food industry. This chapter provides an initial context for the following two chapters but also underpins key themes and the recommendations in Chapter Five, especially the current and future importance of innovation and new markets.

Industry gross value added by the Australian food, beverage and tobacco processing sector was $23.6 billion in 2010-11, which represented approximately 22 per cent of the gross value added of the Australian manufacturing industry and 1.9 per cent of Australian gross domestic product. The food and beverage processing industry also accounted for nearly 24 per cent of Australian manufacturing sector employment in 2010-11. The value of Australian food exports increased to $27.1 billion in 2010-11. Processed foods represent approximately 63 per cent or nearly $17 billion of total food exports for 2010-11.6

Between 2010 and 2012 closures of food processing plants occurred across Australia. In May 2012, Murray Goulburn, one of the biggest exporters of processed dairy foods in Australia, announced 301 redundancies following a detailed review of its processing sites and head office requirements. Goodman Fielder also announced over 300 redundancies in the first half of the 2011-12 financial year. A number of firms, such as Heinz and McCain, have also moved their processing plants offshore to New Zealand or Asia. These events have to be also balanced against some significant investments in recent times, such as Coca-Cola Amatil's $57 million investment in a bottle-manufacturing plant in New South Wales and Primo Smallgoods' investment in a new manufacturing and distribution facility in Wacol just outside of Brisbane.

Industry feedback suggests that the last nine months have seen a deterioration of poor trading conditions, although the evidence of this is patchy. It is anticipated that a number of factors may contribute to further food and other manufacturing plant closures in the next few years, although industry statistics may not necessarily forewarn of these potential events currently due to statistical time-lag issues.

2.1 Structure of the Australian Food Industry

2.1.1 Industry value chain

The Australian food industry encompasses many sectors - from production of raw materials used in food (the farm and fishing sectors) through to the export, import and processing sectors, and ultimately, to domestic sales to consumers. The key components of the food supply chain in 2010-11 are shown in Figure 2.1.

Figure 2.1: Value Chain for Food in Australia, 2010-11

Source: Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry, 2012, Australian Food Statistics: 2010-11
Text description for Value Chain for Food in Australia, 2010-11

The value of food and liquor retailing in Australia grew by 3.7 per cent in 2010-11 to $130.4 billion. Supermarket and grocery store expenditure continued to account for the bulk of food retailing expenditure in 2010-11, with a share of 62 per cent, down from 63 per cent in the previous year. The total value of Australian farm and fisheries food production grew by 17 per cent in 2010-11 to $40.7 billion.

Australian food exports (unprocessed and processed foods) increased to $27.1 billion in 2010-11 - $2.6 billion higher than in 2009-10. The value of Australian food imports (majority processed) also increased in 2010-11 to $10.6 billion, $0.5 billion higher than in 2009-10. Australia's net food exports (trade surplus in unprocessed and processed foods) in 2010-11 increased to $16.5 billion, $2.1 billion higher than in the previous year.

The value of Australia's processed food and beverage exports and imports will be discussed in detail in Section 2.2.2 - Trade.

2.1.2 Number of food processing businesses

As at June 2011, the total number of businesses in the food and beverage processing industry was 13 015 (Table 2.1). Of those businesses, 12 823 (almost 99 per cent) were SMEs. Almost three-quarters of the domestic industry's revenue, however, is accounted for by Australia's 50 largest food and beverage corporations, most of them large multi-national corporations.7

The bakery product manufacturing (non-factory based) industry group had the largest share of businesses (employing and non-employing) relative to other industry groups, followed by beverage manufacturing (mostly wine).

The industry is dominated by a relatively high proportion of SMEs. Some industry groups are dominated by small-scale businesses (such as bakeries and wineries) where most of the output is produced by a large number of small-scale producers that focus on niche markets and serving their respective localities. This is in contrast to industry groups such as meat processing, dairy product manufacturing and sugar and confectionary manufacturing which have a relatively high proportion of large firms (200+ employees) that are characterised by economies of scale.

Non-factory based bakeries were introduced into the ANZSIC Manufacturing Division in 2007-08 and this has had an impact on trend data for the food processing industry.

2.1.3 Entries and exits

The number of businesses operating in the food and beverage processing industry has been growing steadily over the period from 2008 to 2011. The major contributors to this increase have been the non-employing businesses and small businesses (1-19 employees) which have increased by 13.8 per cent and 8.3 per cent respectively.

The number of medium-sized businesses (20-199 employees) declined during the period from 2008 to 2010 when the Global Financial Crisis (GFC) was in effect.

The figures following show entries and exits in selected ANZSIC groups.

Figure 2.2: Business Entries and Exits in Meat Product Manufacturing

Source: ABS 8165.0 - Counts of Australian Businesses, including Entries and Exits, June 2007 to June 2011
Text description for Business Entries and Exits in Meat Product Manufacturing

The meat product manufacturing sector (Figure 2.2) saw a spike in the number of entries of small businesses (1-19 employees) in 2009-10, while the number of exits has remained relatively flat over the period from 2007 to 2011. This spike in entries could be attributed to improved economic conditions in Japan, the United States and South Korea after the GFC.

A relatively large number of medium-sized businesses (20-199 employees) exited during the 2008-09 financial year when the GFC had the greatest impact on markets.

Figure 2.3: Business Entries and Exits in Seafood Processing

Source: ABS 8165.0 - Counts of Australian Businesses, including Entries and Exits, June 2007 to June 2011
Text description for Business Entries and Exits in Seafood Processing

The seafood processing sector (Figure 2.3) is one of the smallest sectors in terms of the number of businesses in operation. It has consistently seen a large number of exits for both SMEs.

Figure 2.4: Business Entries and Exits in Bakery Product Manufacturing

Source: ABS 8165.0 - Counts of Australian Businesses, including Entries and Exits, June 2007 to June 2011
Text description for Business Entries and Exits in Bakery Product Manufacturing

For the period 2007-11, almost double the number of medium-sized businesses exited the bakery product and beverage manufacturing sectors as compared to the entrants (Figure 2.4 and Figure 2.5 respectively). This trend is rather disconcerting as both these sectors are the biggest in terms of the number of businesses and employment.

It should be noted, however, that many exits recorded could be due to mergers or takeovers of successful businesses. Australian Bureau of Statistics (ABS) data does not take into account these events.

Figure 2.5: Business Entries and Exits in Beverage Manufacturing

Source: ABS 8165.0 - Counts of Australian Businesses, including Entries and Exits, June 2007 to June 2011
Text description for Business Entries and Exits in Beverage Manufacturing

2.1.4 Geographical spread

The food and beverage processing Industry is an integral part of Australia's manufacturing base. A majority of the businesses are located along the east coast of Australia where a high proportion of the population resides. The remote and inland regions account for a small percentage of businesses compared to the capital and other large cities.

Figure 2.6 displays food and beverage processing employment in terms of geographical spread. Victoria and New South Wales have relatively higher proportions of food and beverage processing employment, indicating the traditional importance of industrial and agricultural employment in these states and their relatively higher proportions of employing food and beverage processing businesses.8 Interestingly, the employment composition depicted by Figure 2.6 has remained virtually unchanged since 1995, which is indicative of the relative stability in the sector.

Figure 2.6: Food and Beverage Processing Employment, by State and Territory

Source: ABS, 6291.0.55.003 - Labour Force, Australia, Detailed, Quarterly, Feb 2011
Text description for Food and Beverage Processing Employment, by State and Territory

Meat processing industry

Meat processing is undertaken in all states and territories. The operations are highly concentrated around the eastern states lead by Queensland (45.3 per cent), New South Wales (24.2 per cent) and Victoria (14.8 per cent).

Queensland accounts for almost half the cattle livestock herd, approximately 11.2 million in 2010.9 The region is also home to the country's largest corporate beef cattle farms.

Abattoirs are commonly located in rural areas located close to livestock producers. This provides a number of benefits, such as lower transport costs for processors and reduced time livestock is held in transit (which reduces bruising during transport). Other factors that encourage the settlement of meat processors in rural areas include the lower cost of land and municipal by-laws.

Seafood processing

Queensland has the greatest share of seafood processing facilities. This is based on demand for, and supply of, prawns from northern fisheries.

Establishments in New South Wales and Tasmania have declined significantly since 2003-04 as many smaller operators have closed or merged with larger operators. In New South Wales increased fisheries regulation and declining fish stocks have helped drive this trend. Marine farming, however, expanded rapidly in Tasmania in the 1990s to become one of Tasmania's major industries. Other secondary industries have also grown up around Tasmania's marine farming industry, creating additional economic and employment benefits to the state.

The proportion of establishments in South Australia has increased significantly due to the large number of aquaculture facilities located in the state.

The proportions of establishments in Victoria and Western Australia have remained steady. According to IBISWorld data, the distribution of establishments is not expected to change significantly in the 2011-2012 financial year.

Milk and cream processing

While milk production in Australia has grown by about 41 per cent over the past decade, milk consumption has only increased by about 6.8 per cent over the same period.

Processing operations are heavily concentrated along the eastern states, and Victoria is expected to account for 64.2 per cent of milk production over the five years through 2011-12. New South Wales follows behind Victoria, accounting for just over 11.9 per cent of milk production, as milk production in this area is more subject to drought. This is also the case for Queensland and Western Australia. Despite this, New South Wales accounts for the highest percentage of consumption, at 31.2 per cent, followed by Victoria and Queensland with 24 per cent and 22 per cent respectively. The raw milk is either processed for drinking milk or manufactured for various dairy products. There is a big variation between the states in the proportion going into drinking milk versus dairy manufacturing.

Fruit and vegetable processing

Fruit and vegetable processing occurs in all Australian states; however, a relatively high proportion of businesses are situated in New South Wales (31.2 per cent) and Victoria (27.3 per cent).10 In Tasmania, 75 per cent of the state's vegetable production goes through processors and is a critical income source for farmers especially in the North East and West of the state.

The fruit and vegetable processing industry is unlike other manufacturing industries as it is not highly concentrated in capital cities. Historically, fruit and vegetable processors have been located in major horticultural centres. Country regions like the Goulburn Valley in Victoria and the Murrumbidgee Irrigation Area in New South Wales support many fruit and vegetable processing businesses. Proximity to fruit and vegetable growers assists to minimise transportation costs while maintaining product freshness.

Sugar manufacturing

Sugar industry activity is heavily concentrated in Queensland. Since cane quality declines quickly after harvesting, sugar processors tend to locate close to sugar cane farms. Therefore, it is not surprising that the industry is heavily concentrated in Queensland, given that this state is home to about 90 per cent of the country's sugarcane farms. Queensland provides ideal climatic conditions for sugarcane production due to its subtropical weather patterns that are characterised by plentiful supplies of sunshine and a high level of annual rainfall. Queensland also has good infrastructure and reliable irrigation water supply.

Queensland currently has 16 raw sugar mills, mostly around Bundaberg and between Mackay and Cairns. Two refineries also operate in Mackay and Bundaberg. Mills and refineries are usually located close to bulk shipping terminals. Seven terminals operate along the Queensland coast.

Wine manufacturing

Wine production is closely correlated with distribution. Wine manufacturing facilities are often located near vineyards to optimise transport costs and ensure the freshest grapes are crushed. South Australia and Victoria tend to produce a higher proportion of premium wines than other states. Conversely, New South Wales produces a higher proportion of low unit value wines.

South Australia dominates the Australian wine manufacturing industry, accounting for 47.5 per cent of production. In South Australia, wineries are concentrated in the south-east of the state, around Coonawarra, Barossa and regions surrounding Adelaide. Reliable rainfall, warm summer conditions and good soil conditions make these areas optimal for grape vines.

The next most significant state for wine production is New South Wales, which accounts for approximately 28.8 per cent of Australian wine manufacturing output. A long belt of NSW wineries begins in the damp, grape-growing environment of the Hunter Valley and continues across the Great Dividing Range through Orange and Forbes, and on to Griffith in the Murrumbidgee Irrigation Area.

Victoria is another important centre for the wine manufacturing industry, accounting for 18.5 per cent of wine production. Victorian wineries are mostly located in the warm, high rainfall regions along the Murray River in Central Victoria and Western Victoria.

In Western Australia, the grape-growing regions south of Perth have become popular for winery development. The most famous is Margaret River, but Frankland and Mount Barker also sustain wineries.

Tasmania has a very small but fast-growing wine industry. The state produces cool climate wines such as Pinot Noir and Sauvignon Blanc.

2.2 Economic Contribution

2.2.1 Industry value added

In 2010-11, the manufacturing industry's gross value added (GVA) was approximately $107.6 billion. Of this, the food, beverage and tobacco11 processing sector accounted for approximately 21.9 per cent ($23.6 billion), and as Figure 2.7 illustrates, it was the largest contributor to total manufacturing GVA.

Figure 2.7: Gross Value Added Share by Manufacturing Subdivision, 2010-11

Source: ABS, Australian National Accounts: National Income, Expenditure and Product Sep 2011 (Cat No 5206.0) Table 33. Gross Value Added by Industry, Chain volume measures, annual.
Text description for Gross Value Added Share by Manufacturing Subdivision, 2010-11

From 2000-01 to 2010-11, food and beverage GVA grew on average by 0.4 per cent per annum, which was slightly below the overall manufacturing annual growth rate of 0.7 per cent. The overall (economy-wide) average annual growth rate from 2000 to 2010, however, was substantially higher at 3.1 per cent.

Figure 2.8 shows a significant divergence between the overall economy, the manufacturing and the food and beverage processing sectors. From 2000 to 2011,12 overall GVA grew 37.6 per cent, while food and beverage and total manufacturing GVA grew by 8.4 and 10.5 per cent respectively. As a result of this divergence, food and beverage GVA as a share of total GDP has decreased from 2.3 per cent in 2000 to 1.9 per cent in 2011.

Figure 2.8: Growth in Gross Value Added by Category

Source: ABS 5206.0 - Australian National Accounts: National Income, Expenditure and Product, Industry Gross Value Added, Chain Volume Measures (N.B The tobacco sector employs less than 2000 employees and there were only three tobacco product businesses - two manufacturers - in Australia as at June 2011)
Text description for Growth in Gross Value Added by Category

This same trend can be seen in other advanced economies, such as those illustrated in Figure 2.9. While more data is needed to establish the pervasiveness of this trend among developed countries, this initial snapshot does seem to confirm a trend. As Figure 2.9 suggests, over time as an economy develops, the food and beverage processing sector does seem to play a diminishing role in the economy. From 1980 to 2009, the Japanese food and processing sector went from over 3.0 per cent of the economy to around 2.5 per cent, while in Australia it went from 3.0 per cent to 1.8 per cent. The United States (US) has also seen a similar decline, with the food and beverage processing sector decreasing from over 2.0 per cent to just below 1.5 per cent. Of these countries, however, South Korea has by far seen the most dramatic shift with food and beverage processing decreasing from 3.5 per cent to just over 1.0 per cent of GDP.

Figure 2.9: Food, Beverage and Tobacco Gross Value Added and Gross Domestic Product

Source: Ecowin, Reuters
Text description for Food, Beverage and Tobacco Gross Value Added and Gross Domestic Product

This trend among developed countries may be largely explained by economic consumer theory; in particular, that of inferior and normal goods.13 As countries continue to grow and develop, and income per capita increases, the proportion of total income spent on inferior goods, such as food and beverage products, becomes smaller. Consumers begin to spend their increasing incomes on luxury items and services (e.g. dining at restaurants), while the expenditures on inferior goods holds constant or even declines. Therefore, in industries such as the food and beverage product manufacturing industry, absolute growth will depend on population growth, while relative growth (proportion of GDP) will rely on opportunities outside of the domestic market, such as increasing exports into other markets.

Industry value added by selected industry groups

Meat processing (excluding poultry, bacon and ham processing - $2 126 million); cheese and other dairy product manufacturing (excluding milk and cream processing - $1 804 million); and soft drink, cordial and syrup manufacturing ($1 791 million) were the largest industry groups in 2009-10 in terms of industry value added (Figure 2.10). Oil and fat manufacturing, and ice-cream manufacturing were the smallest industry groups in terms of industry value added ($174 million and $170 million respectively).

Figure 2.10: Industry Value Added by Selected Industry Groups, 2009-10

Source: ABS, 8159.0 - Experimental Estimates for the Manufacturing Industry, 2009-10
Text description for Industry Value Added by Selected Industry Groups, 2009-10

2.2.2 Trade

The global food industry

In 2010, the value of global food exports was estimated at US$1026 billion of which the world's largest food exporter, the United States, had a market share of 10.8 per cent. The global food industry has seen rapidly increasing international food prices over the last decade which is underpinned by rising global demand.

Australia ranked 16th in the world as a food exporter in 2010 and 32nd as a food importer (Table 2.2). Australia is a significant exporter for food products such as wheat, sugar, barley, beef and sheep meat, live sheep and cattle, and wine. Interestingly, many of the other big food exporters are also significant importers of food.

Australia's geographic location gives it access to the largest growth market, Asia, which has seen considerable expansion, especially China, whose value of food imports increased by 32 per cent in 2010.

For processed foods, alcoholic beverages were the most globally traded food and beverage product in value terms in 2010, followed by meat (Figure 2.11).

Text description for World Exports of Substantially Transformed Food, 2010

Largest markets

The United States is the world's largest food exporter and importer with market shares of 10.8 per cent and 9.3 per cent respectively.

It has a large and diverse population which demands healthy, high-quality food in addition to catering for new ethnic group migrants adding to its already significant multicultural mix. Of particular note has been an increasing demand in the speciality food segment. Gourmet food which was previously considered the food of the elite is now being chosen by the mainstream population.

Growth markets

For rapidly developing countries in Asia, the proportion of incomes being spent on processed foods relative to unprocessed foods (agricultural products) is only a small fraction of that seen in developed countries. As these countries develop and incomes grow, consumers in these markets are likely to spend an increasing proportion on processed foods. This suggests that markets for processed foods will expand quickly in the years to come, and this represents an opportunity for Australia as it has a comparative advantage in processed foods such as meat and dairy. This opportunity for Australian food manufacturing companies may diminish as these countries begin to increase the quality, reliability and safety of their food industry outputs and as more multinationals locate and establish their factories in Asia.

China

In 2010-11, Australian processed and unprocessed food exports to China were valued at approximately $1.5 billion,14 up 6 per cent from 2009-10. This increased interest and demand for Australian food is driven by China's increasing per capita income. As living standards have improved, so too has the Chinese consumers' desire for more variety and higher quality foods, and increasing demand for protein such as meat and dairy.

There is a window of opportunity here for Australian food manufacturing companies which may diminish as China begins to increase the quality, reliability and safety of its food industry outputs and as more multinationals locate and establish their factories in China.

However, China is also currently a significant exporter of food as well as of consumer goods, and like other industrialised countries is facing many challenges to maintain its level of outputs, including the availability of resources, and energy and water use. It is therefore a moot point as to how long China can continue to expand its food exports while domestic demand increases.

Foreign supermarket chain retailers (such as Wal-mart, Trust-Mart, Makro, Jusco, Carrefour, Tesco, Lotus, Metro, Park'N Shop, 7-Eleven and Watson's), and major domestic chain store outlets (such as Hualian, Lianhua, and Vanguard) have rapidly expanded in China. These supermarkets have become more diversified and sophisticated, and offer consumers an increased range of imported foods and beverages, as international food brands are gaining popularity with Chinese consumers.15

Growth in the number of food retail outlets and chains also present an opportunity for Australian exporters of food to China. All of the foreign-funded supermarkets plan to increase their investment in China.

Australia is recognised by Chinese consumers as having a 'clean and green' environment with good quality products and brands. Many Australian exporters have already taken advantage of this competitive edge by establishing a position as a supplier of products such as meat, dairy products, fresh fruits and seafood. Access to China by SMEs will be difficult unless they operate under a trusted brand, and identifying distribution channels will be critical.

Market feedback has shown interest in Australian suppliers of: milk powder; Ultra High Temperature treated (UHT) milk; cheese; butter and margarine; seafood (oysters, live lobster, live abalone, live king crab); fresh fruit (citrus); wheat, barley and rice; frozen red meats (beef, veal, lamb); processed foods, particularly baby food; wine and beer; fruit juice; convenience and 'instant' foods; and confectionery and snack products.

Australian wine exports to China in 2010-11 were valued at $178 million, up 23.6 per cent from the previous year. China has grown to be Australia's fourth-largest market in terms of value of wine exports.16

Following entry into the World Trade Organization (WTO), China reduced its overall average tariff for agricultural products from 21.2 per cent to 15.3 per cent. However, it still has pockets of high tariff protection - as high as 65 per cent on some key products of interest to Australia, such as wheat. Nonetheless, the Australia-China Free Trade Agreement (FTA) negotiations are progressing on tariffs and other market access issues. These include tariff rate quotas, standards and technical regulations.17

Republic of Korea

South Korea is the third-largest economy in the Asia-Pacific region, behind Japan and China. Its population of 49 million is highly urbanised with nearly a quarter of the population living in Seoul. It imports a considerable proportion of its food requirements, around 60 to 70 per cent. Food safety is of critical importance to consumers and the South Korean Government is tightening regulatory systems in response to various food incidents and labelling issues.

Overall, the South Korean market for food and beverages is vibrant, rapidly adapting to new trends and focussed on cost and value. Most products need to be customised for taste and packaging and a good partner in the market is essential to business success. Australia is seen as a supplier of safe and high-quality food, and the South Korean Government is keen to learn from our regulatory systems. Customers will purchase Australian products if competitively priced and suppliers are flexible and responsive.

South Korea has relatively high tariff rates and quota restrictions covering a broad range of imported food products. South Korea, however, is also proactive in developing bilateral FTAs with trading partners and this represents an opportunity for Australia. Australia's future competitiveness in the Korean market will depend to a large extent on the successful completion of an Australia-Korea FTA.

Indonesia

During 2009, Indonesia was the third-fastest growing economy in the G20. Increasing per capita income over the past decade has led to a thriving middle and upper-class market which accounts for 30 million consumers. There is a growing preference in this segment of the market for imported goods, particularly meat, dairy and convenience snacks. Being a predominantly Muslim country, Islamic dietary conditions are observed, so products that satisfy halal requirements sell best.

Principal Australian exports to Indonesia include live cattle, dairy, fresh produce, meat (excluding beef), lamb, cereal, beverages and wine.

The United States, Canada, Australia and New Zealand are Indonesia's top sources of food imports, although China's share is increasing as it becomes a key source for fresh fruit and vegetables and other food products.

The retailing infrastructure continues to improve with developments of new shopping precincts and expansion of retail chains, especially in the major cities. Australian products face a receptive audience with established ties held by Indonesians having travelled, studied or lived in Australia.

Malaysia

Malaysia is one of South East Asia's more developed nations. The Malaysian food sector is expanding after strong economic growth in the late 1980s and early 1990s. Growth in this sector, a middle- to high-income market, coupled with a sizable expatriate population and tourist market, indicates that there are good growth prospect opportunities.

Malaysia's food and beverage sector consists of a mix of traditional markets, restaurants, street vendors, sophisticated modern retailing (supermarkets, hypermarkets, convenience stores) and food service operations (hotels, restaurants and institutions).

On 22 May 2012, the Australia-Malaysia FTA negotiations concluded with the signing of the final Australia-Malaysia FTA. The agreement will allow annual increases in Australian exports of liquid milk at zero tariff; the immediate elimination of tariffs on processed foods and the phase out of tariffs on tropical fruit (mangoes, melons, pineapples and longans) by 2016. The agreement also includes business-friendly rules of origin provisions, including a simpler declaration of origin instead of certificate of origin requirements for exporters. The agreement is expected to enter into force in 2013.

Thailand

Within Thailand's population of 63 million, a growing and increasingly affluent middle class has emerged and increased the demand for imported foods. In conjunction with that, Thailand has a large expatriate population and is a popular tourist destination. These players demand a range of Western-style products and high-value goods, of which a significant proportion is imported.

Supermarkets and hypermarkets have grown dramatically in number and turnover in the last six years and provide significant opportunities for exporters. Imported meat, fruit, wine, dairy products and grocery items are now mainstream products in many Thai food retail outlets. The convenience sector is also growing strongly and increasing its offering of imported products.

There has been growing concern among Thai consumers about food security, and that food needs to meet international safety and quality requirements. These fears stem from food scares related to bird flu, bovine spongiform encephalopathy (commonly known as 'mad-cow' disease) in the United States, and, most recently, contamination of milk products from China. Hence the increased demand for products which meet the safety and quality requirements presents significant opportunities for Australian food exporters.

A fundamental change in the trading relationship between Australia and Thailand took place when the Thailand-Australia FTA was signed in 2005. This agreement liberalised trade in many sectors including food and has led to a range of new opportunities. Each year tariffs are reduced on a range of food products giving Australia a significant competitive advantage over its major competitors. For example, under the Australia-Thailand FTA, the tariff on table grape exports to Thailand was immediately reduced from 33 per cent to 30 per cent, and will be phased to zero by 2015. Between 2003-04 and 2008-09 there has been a fourfold increase in table grape exports, which are now valued at over $24 million. Australian beef exports to Thailand have also benefitted. The tariff on beef was reduced from 51 per cent to 40 per cent on commencement, and will be reduced to zero by 2020. The value of beef exports to Thailand has more than doubled between 2003-04 and 2008-09.18

The Philippines

The Philippines is one of the world's fastest growing economies and has one of the youngest populations, with approximately 65 per cent of its 101 million residents under the age of 30. A rapidly expanding food service sector driven by foreign investment will provide considerable opportunities for Australian food exporters. A lack of employment opportunities and low income has led to an increasing number of Filipinos working overseas who send money back to their families, thus boosting their purchasing power. The middle and upper-class consumers are based in the national capital region, Manila and major urban centres including Cebu and Davao. Supermarkets, hypermarkets and convenience stores are expanding in the urban centres.

While the general macroeconomic outlook has improved dramatically over recent years, the Philippines needs to continue its economic reform agenda to ensure its long-term fiscal stability and to catch up to other South East Asian economies. The Philippines has a volatile political history; however, since 2001 the country has been relatively stable. The Philippines still has issues with rapid population growth, natural disasters and crime which are hampering growth.

Recent economic liberalisation has resulted in a trading system that is more transparent and has some of the lowest applied tariffs in the region. Tariff rates (currently undergoing unilateral reduction) for most consumer-oriented products range from 3 per cent to 15 per cent, except for industries considered sensitive such as poultry, pork, potatoes and coffee, which have minimum access volumes and significantly higher tariff rates. The Philippines is a member of the WTO and ASEAN and as such is an important member of the Australian-NZ-ASEAN FTA.

Australian food trade

Australian total food exports (fresh and packaged) were valued at approximately $27.1 billion in 2010-11. Over the past eight years food exports have remained relatively constant, barring a sharp rise in value of approximately $28 billion in 2008-09 due partly to the high world grain prices and the low Australian dollar at that time, the latter which helped drive demand.

Grains and meat have consistently been the two largest sectors in the Australian food industry and accounted for approximately 54 per cent of the value of Australian food exports in 2010-11.

Japan remained the leading destination for Australian food exports in 2009-10 (see Figure 2.12). Other major export destinations for Australian food are USA, New Zealand, Hong Kong and UK.

Figure 2.12: Australian Total Food Exports, by Selected Destination

Source: The Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry, 2011, Australian Food Statistics: 2009-10
Text description for Australian Total Food Exports, by Selected Destination

However, Japan's share has been steadily declining over the last few years as other countries in Asia gain increasing prominence as export markets for Australian processed food. Table 2.3 shows that growth in Australian exports of processed foods is mostly driven by China (21.3 per cent), Taiwan (6.3 per cent), Singapore (4.7 per cent) and Indonesia (4.7 per cent).
