While Asia presents many opportunities for Australian exporters of processed foods, it is not homogeneous, and exporters need to be familiar with the varying import requirements and food regulation in the various markets. Trade barriers can make exporting difficult where free trade agreements do not exist. Currently there appears to be a lack of transparency for industry regarding import requirements for Asian markets, especially food regulation. There is also a lack of clarity on the level of domestic subsidies active in potential export markets, the latter an important consideration for Australian food manufacturers seeking to export for the first time. The Australian Customs Service is attempting to address this issue by publishing a Subsidies Register; however, information on subsidies is limited to those goods for which Customs has undertaken anti-dumping investigations. Currently, the Register only features one item of interest in this context, brandy.19

Australian trade in processed food and beverage products

In 2010-11, the food and beverage processing industry was the second-largest manufacturing exporter (Figure 2.13), accounting for approximately 20.2 per cent of total manufacturing exports. From 2005-06 to 2010-11, food product exports grew on average by 1.6 per cent per annum, while beverage and tobacco product exports declined on average by 4.8 per cent per annum.

Figure 2.13: Australian Manufacturing Exports by Sub-Sector

Source: ABS, 5368.0 - International Trade in Goods and Services, Australia, Aug 2011
Text description for Australian Manufacturing Exports by Sub-Sector

As a percentage of total merchandise/goods exports, the food and beverage processing sector accounted for 6.9 per cent. However, as shown in Figure 2.14, food and beverage product exports as a proportion of both total merchandise/goods exports and manufacturing exports have been gradually declining since 2005.

Figure 2.14: Food and Beverage Exports as a Proportion of Total Manufacturing Exports and Total Merchandise/Goods Exports

Source: ABS, 5368.0 - International Trade in Goods and Services, Australia, Aug 2011
Text description for Food and Beverage Exports as a Proportion of Total Manufacturing Exports and Total Merchandise/Goods Exports

During 2010-11, the food and beverage processing industry exported $16.97 billion worth of goods, while food and beverage processing imports were $10.43 billion. This represented net exports of $6.54 billion worth of food and beverage products in 2010-11, and represents an export ratio (exports/imports) of 162.7 per cent, which is significantly higher than that of the total merchandise export ratio (114.8 per cent).

Figure 2.15 illustrates that Australia continues to be a significant net exporter of processed food and beverage products. However, Figure 2.15 also illustrates that imports of processed food and beverage products have been increasing since 2005, while exports have remained relatively flat since 2005.

From 2005-06 to 2010-11, food product imports grew by 8.2 per cent per annum, while beverage and tobacco imports grew by 8.4 per cent per annum. This is in contrast to exports, where food product exports grew on average by 1.6 per cent per annum, while beverage and tobacco product exports declined on average by 4.8 per cent per annum over the same period.

Figure 2.15: Australian Food, Beverage, and Tobacco Trade

Source: ABS, 5368.0 - International Trade in Goods and Services, Australia, Aug 2011
Text description for Australian Food, Beverage, and Tobacco Trade

One key factor that has accounted for much of the increase in processed food and beverage product imports is the appreciation of the Australian dollar (Figure 2.16).

Figure 2.16: Australian Exchange Rate

Source: ABS, 5368.0 - International Trade in Goods and Services, Australia, April 2012
Text description for Australian Exchange Rate

The Australian dollar has appreciated by 39 per cent since July 2005 against the US dollar and by 11 per cent against the currencies of Australia's main trading partners (measured by the trade-weighted index).

One advantage of a stronger Australian currency is that it reduces the relative cost of imported inputs to food processing, including fuel and capital equipment. However, for many food and beverage processors, these cost savings are likely to only partially offset the impact of the high exchange rate. The strong Australian dollar has adversely affected some export and import-competing food and beverage processing sectors, as they are facing increasing competition from cheaper imports due to the high Australian dollar, and has also lead to lower export earnings as most export contracts are denominated in US dollars.

Australian food exports and imports, by industry groups

A detailed overview of Australia's food exports and imports by industry group over the period 1995-96 to 2010-11 is provided in Figure 2.17. The grains, meat, wine, beer, malt and spirits, and dairy industries have been the major contributors to the overall growth in Australia's food export earnings since the mid-1990s. In contrast, export earnings from processed seafood, processed fruit and vegetables, sugar and fresh/chilled vegetables have declined.

Major contributors to the growth in imports have been processed fruit and vegetables, processed food not elsewhere classified, bakery products, confectionary, beer and malt, and wine.

CASE STUDY

Harvey Fresh, a leading Western Australian dairy, fruit juice and wine manufacturer, has acquired juice processor Nugan Quality Foods. There is currently a booming export market for Australian fruit and vegetable juices in South East Asia, and Harvey Fresh is hoping to pull ahead of competition from New Zealand and South America with the acquisition of Nugan which already exports to the Asian market. Nugan has been exporting juice products to Asia since the late 1980s and has developed positive relationships with customers and suppliers.

Figure 2.17: Australian Food Exports and Imports by Industry Groups (2010-11 dollars)

Source: Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry, 2012, Australian Food Statistics: 2010-11.
Text description for Australian Food Exports and Imports by Industry Groups (2010-11 dollars)

Food and beverage product exports by level of transformation

Processed food and beverage exports are divided into two categories:20 substantially transformed goods (STG) and elaborately transformed goods (ETG). STG are goods such as meat, some dairy products (such as milk powder) and sugar. ETG goods are goods such as biscuits and confectionary. As one might expect, ETG are more intensive in intellectual property, capital, and innovation. During 2009-10, ETG exports accounted for 2.1 per cent of total processed food and beverage exports, which was up slightly from the previous financial year, when it was 1.9 per cent. As shown below in Figure 2.18, the composition of exports, in terms of transformation, has changed little since 2002-03. STG exports, as a proportion of total processed food and beverage exports, have hovered around 97-98 per cent.

Figure 2.18: Food and Beverage Exports by Level of Transformation

Source: Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry, 2011, Australian Food Statistics: 2009-10.
Text description for Food and Beverage Exports by Level of Transformation

The composition of imports by level of transformation is similar to exports, with the majority of food and beverage imports being made up of STG. During 2009-10, ETG imports accounted for 4.6 per cent of total food and beverage imports, with the remaining 95.4 per cent comprised of STG. From 2002-03 to 2009-10, ETG imports have increased slightly from 3.6 per cent of total food and beverage imports.

In absolute terms, imports of both ETG and STG have grown at a faster rate than exports. From 2002-03 to 2009-10, exports of ETG and STG grew at an average annual rate of 1.2 and 0.2 per cent respectively. Imports of ETG and STG, however, grew at an annual rate of 7.9 and 11.7 per cent respectively.

The divergence in growth rates of exports versus imports has, as Figure 2.19 shows, led to a decline in the net export figures for both ETG and STG. This decline has been particularly acute for ETG, which went from a net export of $161 in 2002-03 to a net import of $73 million in 2009-10. Over the same period, net STG exports decreased by a quarter from $10 billion to $7.5 billion.

Figure 2.19: Net Exports by Level of Transformation

Source: Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry, 2011, Australian Food Statistics: 2009-10.
Text description for Net Exports by Level of Transformation

2.2.3 Employment

Employment in the food and beverage processing industry is relatively stable and variability in this sector is mostly attributed to changes in numbers of causal and seasonal workers. The level of casual and seasonal workers is often dependent on the sub-sector.

As of May 2012, approximately 222 869 persons were employed in the food and beverage processing sector, making it the single largest employer within the manufacturing industry, accounting for nearly a quarter of total manufacturing employment. This figure is significantly higher than it was in November 2003, when the food and beverage sector accounted for 18.8 per cent of total manufacturing employment. Figure 2.20 displays employment shares by manufacturing sub-division as at May 2012.

Figure 2.20: Manufacturing Employment by Subdivision, May 2012

Source: ABS, 6291.0.55.003 - Labour Force, Australia, Detailed, Quarterly, May 2012
Text description for Manufacturing Employment by Subdivision, May 2012

Employment within the food and beverage sector, as can be seen in Figure 2.21, is dominated by bakery product and meat and meat product manufacturing, with these two groups making up over 50 per cent of total food and beverage employment. It should be noted, however, that 43 per cent of bakery product employment is comprised of non-factory-based workers and bakers who are perhaps more closely aligned to the retail sector than the food and beverage processing sector.

The composition of food and beverage processing employment has changed little from 1995 to 2010. Aside from this, only marginal variations have occurred in the more minor sub-groups of food and beverage processing, which do tend to vary from year to year. Grain milling and fruit and vegetable processing have declined as a proportion of total food and beverage employment.

Figure 2.21: Employment Proportion by Food and Beverage Industry Group

Source: ABS, 6291.0.55.003 - Labour Force, Australia, Detailed, Quarterly, Feb 2011
Text description for Employment Proportion by Food and Beverage Industry Group

When compared to the rest of the manufacturing sector, the food and beverage processing sector recorded relatively strong employment growth throughout most of the 2000s. Figure 2.22 compares food and beverage processing employment to the rest of the manufacturing sector and shows a clear divergence in employment growth from 2003 onwards. Prior to 2003, food and beverage processing employment growth largely mirrored the rest of the manufacturing sector. From 1995 to 2003 the food and beverage processing average annual employment growth rate was -0.4 per cent, while from 2003 to 2008 (just prior to the Global Financial Crisis (GFC)), this growth rate accelerated to 3.9 per cent. The corresponding figures for the overall manufacturing sector, excluding food and beverage processing employment, were -0.3 per cent from 1995 to 2003 and -0.6 per cent from 2003 to 2008.

The impact of the GFC clearly had a profound effect on the manufacturing sector, and the food and beverage processing sector received some shocks. From 2008 to 2009 food and beverage processing employment decreased by 5.3 per cent (12 400 persons), while the manufacturing industry (excluding food and beverages) declined by 3.9 per cent (32 100 persons). Unlike the rest of the manufacturing industry, however, the food and beverage processing sector experienced a significant recovery. From 2009 to 2010, food and beverage processing employment grew 2.6 per cent, while non-food and beverage processing continued to decline, recording a drop in employment of 2.8 per cent. It is worth noting that employment growth from 2009 to 2010 in the food and beverage processing sector was only slightly below that of the economy as a whole, which grew by 2.7 per cent.21

Figure 2.22: Employment by Sector

Source: ABS, 6291.0.55.003 - Labour Force, Australia, Detailed, Quarterly, Feb 2011
Text description for Employment by Sector

As shown in the left-hand side of Figure 2.23, when compared to overall employment growth from 1995 to 2010, both food and beverage processing and total manufacturing (excluding food and beverage processing) employment were significantly lower. However, as shown in the right-hand side of Figure 2.23, which re-bases the employment index to 2003, food and beverage processing employment was actually quite similar to overall employment growth. Furthermore, had the effects of the GFC not been so concentrated on the food and beverage processing and manufacturing sectors, food and beverage processing employment would likely have been higher.22

Figure 2.23: Employment Growth by Sector

Source: ABS, 6291.0.55.003 - Labour Force, Australia, Detailed, Quarterly, Feb 2011
Text description for Employment Growth by Sector

Full-time and part-time employment

Of the 222 869 food and beverage processing employees (as at May 2012), 23.8 per cent are classified as part-time,23 which is at a higher rate than the rest of the manufacturing sector (10.4 per cent), but less than the overall part-time employment rate of 30.0 per cent.

As shown in Figure 2.24, the part-time employment rate24 in the food and beverage processing sector has been gradually increasing, rising from around 17 per cent in 1995 to nearly 23 per cent in 2010. As Figure 2.24 suggests, the higher part-time rate can be attributed to relatively high growth in part-time employment, while full-time employment growth has been relatively poor. From 1995 to 2010, part-time employment increased 47.5 per cent, while full-time employment increased only 7.5 per cent.

Figure 2.24: Full-time/Part-time Employment in the Food Processing Sector

Source: ABS, 6291.0.55.003 - Labour Force, Australia, Detailed, Quarterly, Feb 2011
Text description for Full-time/Part-time Employment in the Food Processing Sector

There are a number of possible explanations behind the upward trend in part-time employment, comprising both demand and supply drivers. On the demand side, institutional reforms and labour market liberalisation have made it easier for firms to create part-time jobs. Such reforms include provisions for part-time and casual employment in awards (which did not previously exist), and the removal of certain restrictions on part-time work.25 Also on the demand side is the significant restructuring of manufacturing business models and supply chains in the wake of globalisation26 and microeconomic reform. As companies have faced increasing pressures to become more flexible, firms may have increased part-time employment to more efficiently spread their production costs over cyclical fluctuations. The seasonal nature of some food processing sectors may also be impacting on the increase in part-time workers, e.g. an attempt to even out the fluctuations of seasonal employment peaks and troughs.

On the supply side, a number of demographic shifts such as the ageing workforce and increased female participation rates have been partially responsible for the overall upward trend in part-time employment. From 1995-96 to 2009-2010, manufacturing workers aged 15-24 have declined in absolute numbers by 26 per cent, while those aged 25-54 have declined by 3 per cent. The share of manufacturing workers aged 55 years and over, however, increased by 75 per cent.27 Also, given the preference for part-time work by mature workers,28 it was inevitable the overall part-time rate would increase as their share of the workforce increased.

2.2.4 Labour productivity

In terms of labour productivity, the food and beverage processing industry had, until 2004, performed relatively well. Food and beverage processing GVA per worker29 growth from 1985 to 2003, as shown below in Figure 2.25, had tracked fairly close to that in both the total manufacturing sector as well as the overall economy. In fact, from 1996 to 2003 food and beverage processing GVA per worker grew at 2.8 per cent per annum, which was significantly higher than in both the manufacturing sector and the overall economy (both grew at 2.2 per cent per annum). From 2003 to 2010, however, GVA per worker in the food and beverage processing industry has been on the decline, falling on average by 1.3 per cent each year. Over this same period the manufacturing sector and the overall economy have grown by 1.4 and 0.8 per cent respectively. The cause of this divergence is somewhat explained by the increase in number of casuals, and when the GVA is expressed against per hour worked the trend is negative.

Figure 2.25: Gross Value Added per Employee

Source: ABS 5206.0 - Australian National Accounts: National Income, Expenditure and Product, Dec 2010.
Text description for Gross Value Added per Employee

ABS, 6291.0.55.003 - Labour Force, Australia, Detailed, Quarterly, Feb 2011.

As with Figure 2.25, Figure 2.26, which shows GVA per hour worked, also demonstrates a relative decline in food and beverage processing productivity. From 2003 to 2010, GVA per hour decreased from $803.60 to $738.10 (8.2 per cent). Over this same period, manufacturing and overall GVA per hour increased by 7.7 and 5.9 per cent respectively. Interestingly, productivity growth prior to 2003 was substantially higher than both manufacturing and overall productivity growth.

Figure 2.26: Gross Value Added per Hour Worked

Source: ABS 5206.0 - Australian National Accounts: National Income, Expenditure and Product, Dec 2010. ABS, 6291.0.55.003 - Labour Force, Australia, Detailed, Quarterly, Feb 2011
Text description for Gross Value Added per Hour Worked

As seen in Figure 2.27, food and beverage processing GVA per hour grew by 3.7 per cent, while manufacturing and overall productivity grew by 2.8 and 2.5 per cent, respectively. Furthermore, since 2008, productivity growth in the food and beverage processing sector has grown comparatively faster. From 2008 to 2010, food and beverage processing productivity grew on average by 3.9 per cent, compared to just 1.1 per cent in total manufacturing and 0.8 per cent in the overall economy.

Figure 2.27: Productivity Growth

Source: ABS 5206.0 - Australian National Accounts: National Income, Expenditure and Product, Dec 2010. ABS 6291.0.55.003 - Labour Force, Australia, Detailed, Quarterly, Feb 2011.
Text description for Productivity Growth

Figure 2.28: Industry Value Added per Employee by Selected Industry Groups, 2008-09

Source: Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry, 2012, Australian Food Statistics: 2010-11
Text description for Industry Value Added per Employee by Selected Industry Groups, 2008-09

Figure 2.28 shows that the greatest value added per employee in 2008-09 occurred in beer and malt manufacturing ($481 000 per employee in 2010-11), while the lowest was in bakery products ($57 000 per employee). This disparity partly reflects the capital-intensive nature of the beer and malt processing industry, and the relatively more small-scale and labour-intensive nature of bakery production.

2.2.5 Domestic investment

Total domestic investment in new capital (that is, tangible assets including equipment, plant and machinery, and building and structures) by private enterprise in the food product manufacturing sector has been decreasing slightly since 1998 (Figure 2.29), but for the past few years has begun to trend upwards. Total investment in new capital in the beverage product manufacturing sector has also been decreasing steadily since 1994.

Figure 2.29: Total New Capital Expenditure/Investment by Private Enterprise, 1987 to 2010

Source: ABS 2010b, Private New Capital Expenditure and Expected Expenditure, Australia, cat. no. 5625.0, Australian Bureau of Statistics, Canberra.
Text description for Total New Capital Expenditure/Investment by Private Enterprise, 1987 to 2010

To save on operating costs and ensure longer term sustainability, the food and beverage processing sector is investing in more energy efficient machinery and equipment. The Australian Government is seeking to support these processors by introducing the Clean Technology Food and Foundries Investment Program. This is a $200-million, competitive, merit-based grants program to support Australian food and foundry manufacturers to maintain competitiveness in a carbon constrained economy. The program will provide grants for investments in energy efficient capital equipment and low-pollution technologies, processes and products.

2.2.6 Foreign investment

In the past decade, corporate investors, both Australian and foreign (plus some sovereign wealth funds), have shown growing interest in large-scale agribusiness and agrifood investment in Australia.

Foreign Direct Investment (FDI) offers clear benefits to Australia. Economic consultancy Access Economics reported in October 2009 that FDI contributes:

  • 37 per cent of R&D
  • 50 per cent of exported goods
  • 46 per cent of value of services exported
  • 14 per cent of employment.

Foreign investment, both direct (FDI) and indirect (portfolio), has always played a significant part in Australia's agriculture and food sector, from agricultural inputs like seeds, fertilisers and machinery, through to agricultural production, food and beverage processing, distribution, food service and food retailing, and domestic and international trading.

More broadly, foreign investment plays a key role in supplementing domestic savings and by facilitating development and maintenance of new internationally competitive industries and businesses. Foreign investment has similarly facilitated modernisation of substantive sectors of the economy, and in turn, driven efficiencies to enhance Australia's competitiveness.

European and North American investors have long had a strong presence in Australia and have been significant players in the country's domestic food and beverage industry. For example, Schweppes opened its first factory here in 1877; followed by Nestlé in 1911; Cadbury, Kraft and Kellogg in the 1920s; and Heinz in the 1930s.

Investment by leading European and North American agribusiness companies, like Clyde Agriculture and Cargill, as well as the multinational food and beverage processors, helped grow the Australian agriculture and food sector, and helped link Australia to world markets.

Despite the important role foreign investment plays in Australia's agriculture and food sectors, community concerns over a lack of transparency over foreign ownership of Australia's agricultural land and agricultural food production and the implication for Australia's domestic food security remains. On 23 November 2010, the Australian Government announced an information-gathering process to address some of these community concerns, which included a study commissioned to consider the role and history of foreign investment in the development of agriculture in Australia and the extent of foreign ownership of Australian agricultural land. This initiative also included the collection of data by the ABS on foreign investment in Australian agriculture, as information on this was limited. The ABS subsequently conducted a survey and the results show that as at 31 December 2010:30

  • 89 per cent of agricultural land was entirely Australian owned and a further 5.5 per cent at least 50 per cent Australian owned
  • 99 per cent of agricultural businesses, by number, in Australia were entirely Australian owned
  • 91 per cent of water entitlements for agricultural purposes were entirely Australian owned

The survey results are broadly comparable with levels of foreign ownership of agricultural businesses and land collected in the Agricultural Census of 1983-84.

Regulation of foreign investment in Australian agriculture is greater than many OECD countries, with Australia ranked tenth-most restrictive of all OECD countries. The Government has well-established arrangements under which foreign investments are examined and applies a rigorous national interest test to all significant foreign investment applications.

Foreign investment assists in maximising food production and supporting Australia's position as a major net exporter of agricultural produce, by financing investment, and delivering productivity gains and technological innovations. Foreign investment in agriculture supports agricultural production, job creation and contributes to the prosperity of rural communities and the broader Australian economy. Any reduction in investment in the agricultural sector as a result of a reduction in capital inflows would be likely to result in lower food production with potentially higher food prices, lower employment, lower incomes in the sector and lower government revenue.

2.3 Outlook: challenges and opportunities

2.3.1 Employment forecasts

Forecasts made by the Centre of Policy Studies (CoPS) suggest that employment in the food and beverage industry will decline by an average of 0.4 per cent per year to 2017-18. As shown in Figure 2.30, however, this rate is less than the forecasted decline in the overall manufacturing sector (-1.4 per cent), which will result in an increase in the proportion of food and beverage processing workers. According to these estimates, food and beverage processing employment will decrease to 225 100 in 2017-18, which is a decrease of 3.7 per cent from the 2009-10 level. Over this same period, the proportion of manufacturing workers in the food and beverage processing sector is expected to rise to 25.4 per cent. As a proportion of total employment, however, food and beverage processing is expected to decline from 2.1 per cent to 1.8 per cent in 2017-18, reflecting the expected stronger growth in other sectors of the economy.31

Figure 2.30: Employment Forecasts: Food and Beverage Processing Employment

Source: MONASH Economic Forecasts, DIISRTE Version, Centre of Policy Studies, March 2011
Text description for Employment Forecasts: Food and Beverage Processing Employment

Table 2.5 outlines the employment forecasts by select food and beverage groups. The seafood processing industry is forecast to experience the greatest decline in employment, a 9.1 per cent decline from 2009-10 to 2017-18. The oils and fats manufacturing industry is the only industry that is not expected to experience a decline in employment from 2009-10 to 2017-18.

It should be noted that any forecasted value should be viewed with caution. Estimates are based on historical data and various assumptions on future growth. These forecasts are subject to high levels of variability, particularly when viewing more disaggregated data, such as that used here. While estimates relating to broad categories are more likely to be more accurate, employment levels in individual subdivisions are less likely to follow those forecasted by CoPS.

2.3.2 Economic modelling

The Department of Industry, Innovation, Science, Research and Tertiary Education has commissioned economic modelling and analysis by the Centre for International Economics (CIE) on some of the challenges and opportunities facing the food processing industry.32 Specifically, the CIE has examined pessimistic, business-as-usual and optimistic scenarios to quantify the challenges and opportunities faced by the food processing industry.33

Using a general equilibrium model of the Australian economy that quantitatively captures the behaviour and interactions of supply, demand and prices in the Australian economy and its industry sectors (including the food and beverage processing industry and its sub-groups), the CIE has developed three distinct scenarios - pessimistic, business-as-usual and optimistic - for the Australian food processing industry to 2020 in order to examine the impacts on production and exports. The model quantifies the challenges facing the sector to identify what needs to be done (in terms of broad industry productivity increases, and domestic and foreign demand increases) to lift the pessimistic scenario to the business-as-usual and optimistic scenarios.

The optimistic scenario assumes strong global demand growth for food; productivity34 growth in the Australian food processing industry; relatively low growth in energy prices; and an easing mining boom in Australia taking the pressure off the Australian dollar and production and input prices. The business-as-usual scenario assumes average long-run productivity growth in the food processing industry and average long-run productivity growth in the mining sector. The pessimistic scenario assumes increasing strength in the mining sector (and therefore increasing pressure on capital and input prices); increasing energy prices; sluggish growth in international demand for food as well as below average long-run productivity growth in Australia's food industry, leading to increased import pressure.

Table 2.6 outlines the CIE simulation results in terms of the percentage change (annual and over eight years to 2020) in food production by sub-sector under the three separate scenarios.

For the pessimistic scenario, results indicate that the food processing industry has some resilience under adversity in terms of changes in production. A pessimistic scenario involving high mining-sector productivity, low food-industry productivity and increasing energy prices sees only modest retractions (-2.73 per cent over eight years and -0.35 per cent annually) in processed food output over the next eight years to 2020.

There are variations across the food processing sub-groups within the food processing industry, however. Other food product manufacturing and dairy product manufacturing experience the largest retractions (-7.44 per cent and -4.81 per cent respectively) over eight years to 2020. Flour, confectionary and biscuit manufacturing and oil and fat manufacturing, however, experience expansions of 4.69 per cent and 1.77 per cent respectively over eight years to 2020 under the pessimistic scenario. The results also vary by state. Queensland, Tasmania and Western Australia all experience contractions in processed food production. Victoria and New South Wales, however, expand under the pessimistic scenario.

Under the business-as-usual scenario, however, the food processing industry expands by 8.63 per cent over eight years to 2020 (refer to Table 2.6). It also outperforms the agricultural sector (3.40 per cent growth over eight years). Compared to the pessimistic scenario, the business-as-usual scenario predicts that industry output will be approximately 11.5 per cent higher. The fruit and vegetable product manufacturing sub-groups expand the most relative to other sub-groups over the eight years to 2020.

Under the optimistic scenario, the food processing industry expands by nearly 30 per cent over eight years to 2020 (refer to Table 2.6). Under this scenario, the Australian mining sector contracts while the Australian food processing industry experiences rapid productivity growth close to its historical upper productivity growth bound. Export demand is growing and only relatively modest increases in energy prices are experienced. All sub-groups within the food processing industry experience relatively strong growth, with fruit and vegetable product manufacturing and other food product manufacturing being outstanding performers in terms of output growth.

The differences in the change in production across the scenarios are mostly explained by differences in the change in exports of food products which are highly sensitive to prices. While annual growth in domestic consumption of processed foods increases from 1.7 per cent in the pessimistic scenario to a modest 2 per cent in the business-as-usual and optimistic scenarios, annual growth in exports of processed foods increases, from -10.5 per cent in the pessimistic scenario (low industry productivity) to -1 per cent in the business-as-usual scenario (average industry productivity) to 9 per cent in the optimistic scenario (high industry productivity - refer to Figure 2.31), indicating that growth in exports is highly influenced by productivity growth in the food processing industry, as it leads to falls in prices and hence enables manufacturers and their products to be more price-competitive internationally.

Figure 2.31: Annual Change in Exports

Source: CIE Food Processing Model - C IE simulations
Text description for Annual Change in Exports

Challenges

If all scenarios discussed thus far are feasible, then an obvious challenge would be to avoid the pessimistic outcome. The CIE measured the extent of such a challenge by assessing the change required in key factors (such as improvements in productivity and increases in export demand) over which industry and government may have some control.

To avoid the pessimistic scenario and stay close to the business-as-usual situation would require either:

  • Food processing industry productivity to grow by 1.43 per cent per annum (or 11.92 per cent over eight years to 2020) - an additional 0.85 per cent annually, relative to the assumed growth of 0.5 per cent assumed in the pessimistic scenario; or
  • Export demand to increase to 7.2 per cent per annum - an additional 5.7 per cent annually, relative to the assumed growth of 1.5 per cent assumed in the pessimistic scenario.35

Lifting the annual productivity growth rate by an additional 0.85 per cent annually is not an unrealistic target given historical achievements. Lifting export demand growth from 1.5 per cent to 7.2 per cent is a big target, however, and if it were to be achieved, would probably require improvements in market access through reduced trade protectionism and a significant focus on export markets. Alternatively, a combination of the two, such as doubling productivity growth rates (a 0.5 per cent increase) and doubling export demand (a 1.5 per cent rise) would also achieve the output of the business-as-usual scenario.

To achieve growth captured by the optimistic scenario would require either:

  • Food processing industry productivity to grow by 2.58 per cent per annum (or 22.36 per cent over eight years to 2020) - an additional 1.42 per cent annually, relative to the assumed growth of 1 per cent assumed in the business-as-usual scenario; or
  • Export demand to increase to 10.78 per cent a year - an additional 8.78 per cent annually, relative to the assumed growth of 2 per cent assumed in the business-as-usual scenario.36

These are big challenges. It would require more than a doubling in productivity growth and more than a five-fold increase in export demand growth. Of course this challenge could be dissected into various combinations of both factors; however, they would still represent very large increases above business-as-usual growth rates. For this to be feasible, it would require a boom in Asian demand (and Asian income growth) for Australian processed foods. It would be akin to what the mining sector is experiencing currently. It would also require improvements in market access through substantial reductions in trade protectionism favouring Australian processed food products.

Opportunities for productivity gains

As identified in the previous discussion, increasing productivity is probably the most important and achievable target the industry can pursue to face challenges and pursue opportunities. Productivity improvements are most likely to be achieved through:

  • removing regulatory impediments through regulatory reform
  • adopting emerging technologies and capital with embodied productivity improvements which may also require removing regulatory and cultural impediments relating to technologies such as gene technology, nanotechnology and irradiation
  • achieving greater economies of scale through growth, amalgamations and vertical integration
  • raising labour productivity by investing in a skilled labour force
  • investing in R&D that leads to the development of new processing technologies and
  • working collaboratively with agriculture suppliers to increase agricultural productivity to lower the costs of agricultural inputs.

Raising export demand to capitalise on the growth in Asian countries is most likely to require:

  • continued activity by government to reduce trade barriers, making it easier to access new markets and building the Australia brand and
  • targeted consumer insight research as well as targeted consumer marketing to produce products that appeal to consumer tastes and dietary preferences and capitalising on Australia's reputation as a safe and clean food producer.

A more strategic alignment of industry and government assets to help drive exports is also likely to improve industry's ability to enter these new export markets. However, it should be noted that Asian markets are not a tabular rasa, i.e. many MNEs, including those that have a presence in Australia, already have a presence in those Asian markets, hence the ongoing need for Australian food manufacturers to improve their competitiveness.

2.4 Summary

The key economic features of the Australian food processing industry are:

  • There are a total of 13 015 businesses across ten ANZSIC Groups in the food and beverage processing sector. Small and medium-sized enterprises comprise 98.5 per cent of the food and beverage processing industry
  • The food and beverage processing sector is the largest employer within the manufacturing sector and has recorded relatively strong growth. Declines are forecast by 2017-18
  • The bulk of the sector's employment is in the traditionally industrial and agricultural states of New South Wales, Victoria and Queensland
  • In 2010-11, the food and beverage processing sector exported $16.97 billion worth of goods, while food and beverage processing imports were $10.43 billion. This represented net exports of $6.54 billion worth of food and beverage products
  • Australia's Asian neighbours provide a significant opportunity for future market growth
  • Food and beverage processing gross value added is growing and is the largest contributor to total manufacturing gross value added in Australia
  • Gross value added per worker from 2003 to 2010 in the food and beverage processing sector has been on the decline, falling on average by 1.3 per cent each year. Over this same period the manufacturing industry and the overall economy have grown by 1.4 and 0.8 per cent respectively
  • Food manufacturers should focus on increasing their productivity as it is one of the main factors within the control of industry and is essential to remaining competitive and viable in the long-run. Manufacturers could also focus on increasing exports of their products to capitalise on demand growth in Asian countries

3. Current Operating Environment

This chapter examines the forces affecting the industry's current operating environment. Like most of Australia's manufacturing sector, the food industry is facing challenging times, and there is now a new norm of a high Australian dollar and a strong resource sector. A review of the food manufacturing performance is followed by discussion of a number of environmental factors that affect the industry, such as the cost of direct inputs like raw material, labour, packaging; transport, water and energy costs; the cost of assets and investments; cost of overheads such as marketing and advertising; and the structure of the retail sector.

3.1 Financial Performance

At the outset it should be noted that as the vast majority of food businesses are private companies, financial information is not generally available at a high level of granularity. In addition, any official data at a disaggregated level is provided too late to observe current trends necessary to enable appropriate and immediate action. In addition, governments must comply with the Information Privacy Principles when sharing private data that may have been provided to them. As a result, much of the Australian Bureau of Statistics (ABS) data is provided in aggregate terms.

Studies such as the Food and Beverage Industry CEO Survey37 can provide insight on a small scale about how the industry margins have been tracking in what have been challenging trading conditions over the last three years. Eighty-two Chief Executive Officers (CEOs) interviewed as part of the study discussed their views on the industry, including financial performance. A key measure of financial performance used in the survey was Return on Assets (RoA). RoA is a measure of Earnings Before Interest, Tax, Depreciation and Amortisation (EBITDA) over total assets in a given financial year. RoA is therefore important to identifying profitability with regard to assets. For CEOs surveyed, RoA was approximately 10 to 15 per cent38 (Table 3.1).

Table 3.1: Return on Assets by Industry Sub-sector (September 2011)

In addition to RoA, measuring the changes in EBITDA as a margin of total revenue for firms within the sector enables one to identify if profitability in firms (and the industry more broadly) is shifting. It is therefore an important tool to characterise the health of the food manufacturing sector.

Considering the size of the industry, data extracted from IBISWorld of the top 70 food companies (which fall within the top 2000 companies by revenue) operating within Australia were analysed, to identify key characteristics of the sector. From 2000 to 2011, fluctuations are evident in average EBITDA as a portion of total revenue (Figure 3.1):

  • From 2000 to 2007 EBITDA fluctuated between 10 per cent and 11 per cent. Post-GFC there was a decline from 11 per cent to 9.5 per cent in 2009. Then from 2009 to 2010, there was an increase in EBITDA to 13.6 per cent. However, it is important to note in this period a smaller sample of companies was analysed (29)39

Similar to EBITDA margins, fluctuations in gross operating profits (another measure of performance) are also evident for the sector. Based on seasonally adjusted gross operating profit data in the 2011 Business Indicators publication (collated by the ABS), a general increase in gross operating profits is evident for the food manufacturing sector annually. Profits peaked (at $2148 million) in 2010, but there has been a decline of approximately $608 million from 2010 to December 2011 (to $1540 million) (Figure 3.2).

  • During 2009 a significant decline in manufacturing (excluding food) is evident (27 per cent), however the decline in the food manufacturing sector was least evident (12.7 per cent); and
  • In 2010, a significant increase in the food manufacturing sector is evident, despite a steady decline in manufacturing overall (excluding food). More recently, the trend has been downward, although ostensibly not very sharply
  • It is important to note that the increase in gross operating profits during this period may be explained by several factors. Importantly, increases for several food-related commodities are evident during this period,40 particularly sheep meat, sugar and wheat, of which the value of outputs increased in comparison to the previous year. It is also important to note here that no significant positive or negative statistical pattern on profitability is yet evident.

Global analysis of the food sector suggests that Australia is marginally behind. In a report prepared by UBS Securities Australia, Exploring Treasure Island, the performance of several key competitors in the food manufacturing sector was analysed. One of the key findings of this report concluded that Australia presents the lowest average percentage of Earnings Before, Interest and Tax (EBIT) margins (13.8 per cent) in comparison to the global average of 15.9 per cent for the period 2008-2011.41 (Figure 3.4).

3.2 Direct Inputs

3.2.1 Raw materials

Notwithstanding that some of the key inputs into food processing manufacture are internationally traded commodities and that Australia is therefore a price-taker in this context, Australian manufacturers do enjoy some cost advantage in some ingredients.

The CIE42 has computed the revealed comparative advantage (RCA)43 for selected countries or regions by processed and unprocessed food products based on 2007 figures. A country that has a RCA value greater than one for a product indicates that the product from this country had a comparative advantage, because the export share of the product in the country is greater than the world average share of the product. In contrast, a RCA of less than one indicates a comparative disadvantage because its export share is lower than the world average share.

Figure 3.5 shows the countries with strong revealed comparative advantage.44 It should be noted the scales are different for each graph. It reveals that Australia had a strong comparative advantage in beef (represented as bovine meat), sheep meat (represented as cattle/sheep), dairy, wine (represented as bev or tob) and wheat. In comparison, New Zealand had a very strong comparative advantage in most food categories, especially in dairy and beef.

Countries listed in Figure 3.6 generally faced comparative disadvantages in more products than they held advantages, and countries listed in Figure 3.7 faced no comparative advantages at all. Notably, China had no comparative advantage in any processed and unprocessed food products listed, except for livestock. Similarly, the South-East Asian region had no comparative advantage in processed food products where Australia had a comparative advantage, such as dairy, beef, or other processed meats. Japan had no comparative advantage in any food products (Figure 3.7) and is a major importer of processed and unprocessed food from Australia.

In 2007 the Australian dollar ranged from 78 to 90 US cents. With the dollar around parity, the opportunities for Australia to capitalise on its comparative advantage have lessened significantly. Australian businesses are now citing the lack of availability of affordable raw materials as a major factor in reduced Australian competitiveness. Lately, the high Australian dollar has meant that imported raw material costs have increased and Australian raw material costs may be considerably higher than in competitor countries and, as a result, it has been harder for suppliers to compete with imports.

Figure 3.5 Regions with Strong Comparative Advantage (2007)

Source: CIE, 2012, Australian Food Processing: Quantifying the challenges and opportunities
Text description for Regions with Strong Comparative Advantage (2007)

Figure 3.6 Regions with moderate to little advantage (2007)
Text description for Regions with moderate to little advantage (2007)

Figure 3.7 Regions with no Comparative Advantage (2007)
Text description for Regions with no Comparative Advantage (2007)

Recently, the cost of raw materials has been cited by some large multinationals (such as SPC Ardmona, Heinz and National Foods) as one of the principal reasons for the closure of local food processing factories in Australia.45

Australia can produce better quality products than those it can import; however, unless the consumer is concerned about quality over price, or is aware of the differences in quality, or the origins of key inputs into the finished product, this is unlikely to be an important factor. Imported food and packaging inputs are currently often cheaper than Australian inputs.

Foreign competitors are frequently assisted by their governments through farm intervention and export subsidies to overcome the disadvantage caused by having to use more expensive raw materials. Unfortunately, as Chapter Two indicated, the extent of these subsidies is unknown.

Sometimes there are other reasons to import inputs, such as seasonality, insufficient domestic supply or no domestic growing capability. However, many Australian manufacturers would prefer to use Australian product due to the reliability of supply, closeness to market, freshness and quality attributes. Once local production capacity is lost, Australian processors have no option but to import.

In 2011 PricewaterhouseCoopers (PwC) conducted an industry survey on behalf of the DIISRTE to gain insights into issues. While the survey was not statistically significant because of its small sample size (92), raw materials were found to be by far the greatest cost component within this sector (60 respondents claimed this made up more than 20 per cent of their costs), and the increasing cost of raw materials was seen as a reason for preventing investment in business. In addition, productivity in agriculture has been relatively stagnant for a number of years. The fluctuating price of raw materials/produce was seen to be a key challenge for 80 of 92 businesses surveyed by PwC, since they form one of the largest components of a business's costs. Innovation to improve yields and productivity in this area must therefore be seen as a priority.

Perhaps unsurprisingly, businesses surveyed acknowledged the quality of Australian produce - inputs to production and finished goods - (86 businesses agreed with the statement that Australia is strongly associated with producing good quality food). In fact, two-thirds of them claimed that they gained a competitive edge by using the label 'Australian made, Australian grown' where appropriate. So, despite the perceived costliness of utilising Australian products, 87 businesses claimed they used them to an extent. Three in ten businesses claimed they used between 81-100 per cent Australian-made or grown products. The fruit/veg and dairy industries were more likely to use Australian products, and the seafood, oil/fat and grain/cereal industries were least likely to use a large percentage of Australian-made or grown inputs.

Australia has a reputation for supplying clean and natural products with low chemical residues. Surprisingly perhaps, while 'Australia has a major asset in the willingness of international consumers to expect quality, natural and "safe" foods from Australia, such as wine, many Australian foods and beverage producers are not exploiting more effectively this positive pre-disposition of international consumers'.46

Importing inputs on an opportunistic basis because of price continues to be a risk to the continuity of supply from domestic growers. In addition, economies of scale in some industries are difficult to achieve because of distance, geographic and topographic reasons. For example, potato production in Tasmania, compared to the Canterbury plains in New Zealand, is limited by the lack of flat land. In addition, Australian road transport regulations and the width of roads can prevent the use of large-scale machinery such as multi-row potato harvesters which provide greater efficiencies.

Despite this, some niche marketers have been able to undertake innovative product development and marketing to enable them to compete in value-sensitive markets despite raw materials being near or at world parity price, e.g. Harvey Fresh (see Chapter Two).

3.2.2 People and skills

The PwC survey found that survey respondents had some difficulty in attracting employees across all skill levels.47 In particular, 53 businesses of the 92 interviewed claimed they had difficulty in attracting engineers, and 46 respondents claimed they had difficulty in attracting food technologists. However, over a third of all respondents also had difficulty in attracting production workers and plant maintenance workers. It should also be noted that difficulties in recruiting workers can be expected at a time of relatively low unemployment and strong competition for labour from other sectors.

Strategy Group members have also noted the need for more leadership and business training, especially for SMEs. The call for an increased emphasis on this aspect of training is supported by findings from Enterprise Connect SME business surveys.

When compared with both the overall manufacturing industry and the Australian economy, the food and beverage workforce is comprised of a relatively high proportion of low-skilled workers.48 Roughly two-thirds of the food and beverage workforce is classed as lower skill, and the employment of managers and professionals is lower than other areas of manufacturing, making up only 18.6 per cent of the labour force (compared to 34.8 per cent in the overall labour force). The industry is characterised by fewer workers with a tertiary qualification and an over-representation of workers with no post-school education.

Forecasts made by the Centre of Policy Studies suggest that while employment in the food and beverage sector will decline by an average of 0.4 per cent per year to 2017-18, in terms of educational attainment,49 tertiary-educated workers in the food and beverage processing sector are likely to grow by 17.1 per cent from 2009-10 to 2017-18 (5900 persons) and vocationally educated persons to increase by 5.8 per cent (4500 persons). Some firms are currently filling employment gaps by employing overseas-trained food scientists.

The expected decline in unqualified workers will result in this group representing 46.0 per cent of the food and beverage processing workforce, which is significantly less than the 52.4 per cent in 2009-10.

Figure 3.8: Employment by Skill Level, 2009-10

Source: MONASH Economic Forecasts, DIISR Version, Centre of Policy Studies, March 2011.
Text description for Employment by Skill Level, 2009-10

Technical skills

Food Science and Biotechnology studies in higher education system have remained relatively steady in recent years (see Table 3.2). After a period of declining enrolments, commencements in Food Science and Biotechnology have increased by 6.1 per cent from 2009 to 2010. Enrolments in Food Science and Biotechnology have increased by 1.3 per cent from 2539 in 2009 to 2572 in 2010. Completions in Food Science and Biotechnology have increased by 11.9 per cent from 674 in 2009 to 754 in 2010.

Table 3.2: Higher Education Food Science and Biotechnology Students, 2006-2010

 Food Science and Biotechnology
YearCommencementsEnrolmentsCompletions
2006 1108 3040 815
2007 1031 2828 769
2008 998 2738 721
2009 960 2539 674
2010 1019 2572 754
% change 2009-2010 6.10% 1.30% 11.90%
% change 2006-2010 -8.00% -15.40% -7.50%

Source: DIISRTE Higher Education Statistical Collection

However, in the five years to 2010 there was an 8.0 per cent decrease in commencements; a 15.4 per cent decrease in enrolments and a 7.5 per cent decrease in completions for Food Science and Biotechnology student (see Table 3.5). This is in contrast to a rising trend in the number of student enrolments and completions in nutrition related courses. Industry advice is that Food Science graduates are invariably employed within the food industry, although not perhaps in roles as researchers but rather in quality assurance. A number of organisations have identified this problem and have developed initiatives that are attempting to address it but there is no mechanism to coordinate and capture synergies between these disconnected but otherwise useful activities. Examples include initiatives by Australian Institute of Food Science Technology (AIFST), AFGC - outlined below - Food Technology Association of Australia (FTAA), and several state governments.

The reason that few students elect to study the food sciences could be due in part to the negative perceptions of suitable work available within the sector for qualified individuals and the lack of a clear career path in food science and technology.50 Other factors include low remuneration and poor career options. Demand for locally trained food scientists is also trending downwards due perhaps to a perception of declining standards in locally trained food scientists and/or a declining aggregate demand for local R&D.51 A survey of industry employers conducted by the Allen Consulting Group on behalf of the FTAA confirmed that many food science and technology graduates do not possess the technical skills employers consider important.52 Indeed, a report prepared by the Australian Institute of Food Science and Technology (AIFST) noted that the introduction of more nutrition-based subject areas in food science and technology courses at the expense of engineering and other fundamental food science subjects may not equip graduates with the knowledge and skills to be effective food scientists/technologists.53

The 2011 survey conducted by the Allen Consulting Group observed that the majority of survey respondents believe that it will become more difficult to recruit suitable food science and technology graduates domestically in the next five years.54 Currently, this may not present too much of a risk to the food industry which is not generally looking to expand its R&D profile because of present economic conditions, and which can source food science graduates from overseas on 457 visas. However, in the medium to longer term the lack of domestic supply of suitable food scientists poses a significant risk to an industry looking to significantly expand its R&D capacity and capabilities, not least to exploit the opportunities of the Asian century.

UQ-AFGC partnership to overcome technical shortages

In October 2010, the University of Queensland (UQ) and the AFGC announced the establishment of the AFGC Chair in Food Sciences and Technology, and a partnership to address the growing demand for high calibre food science and technology graduates and raise the profile of food science and technology courses. An industry advisory committee will provide advice on industry training and education needs. The initiative will also encompass an industry-sponsored scholarship scheme to develop better linkages with education providers and industry and attract undergraduate and graduate students to undertake industry placements in food companies as part of their degree programs.

In 2011 the University of Tasmania, the CSIRO and the Defence Science and Technology Organisation (DSTO) announced a collaboration to develop a new centre for food innovation at the DSTO site in Scottsdale, Tasmania.

Initiatives such as these are crucial for supporting the viability of food science and technology courses. Indeed, the AIFST noted that continued university support for food science and technology courses with low student enrolments will require more support from industry in the form of sponsored scholarships and academic positions.

Production skills

The Australian Vocational Education and Training (VET) system provides training across a wide range of subject areas relevant to the food processing industry. It is delivered through a variety of training institutions and enterprises. The courses are managed and accredited by various national and state authorities.55

AgriFood Skills Australia is one of 11 independent, not-for-profit Industry Skills Councils established by the DIISRTE. It is led by industry and funded by the Australian Government, and is charged with driving the skills and workforce development agenda across five main sectors including:

  • Rural and related industries (including agriculture, horticulture, conservation and land management, animal care and management)
  • Food, beverage and pharmaceutical processing
  • Meat
  • Seafood and
  • Racing (greyhound, thoroughbred and harness).

AgriFood Skills Australia data from 2005 to 2009 appears to confirm that there has been a decline in students (excluding apprentices/trainees) commencing VET courses relating to the food processing industry, from approximately 8000 students in 2005 to approximately 4000 students in 2009.56 This trend contrasts to the number of all students completing their VET courses in the food processing industry, which appears to have been relatively stable from 2005 to 2008.

The training commencements and completions of apprentices and trainees in the food processing sector appear relatively stable from 2005 to 2009, and it seems that a relatively larger proportion of apprentices and trainees commencing an apprenticeship are completing their apprenticeships than there are students commencing a general VET qualification.

In 2009 the majority of students and apprentices/trainees in the food processing sector were located in, and were undertaking training, in the major cities and inner regional areas. The regional location of the industry's workforce poses particular problems for attracting, developing and retaining an appropriate workforce with the specific skills needed to meet industry sector requirements. One of the main difficulties is the sustainability of the provision of vocational education and training in regional areas with small education markets. This is exacerbated by projections of further decreases in the 15 to 24-year age group in many regional areas.57 Online training via the National Broadband Network could alleviate problems with distance training.

The majority of food processing industry VET graduates are either active in the food processing industry (approximately one-third of all food processing graduates), or are active in other (non agrifood-related) industries (approximately one-third of all food processing graduates).58 If graduates employed in the meat processing, rural and seafood industries are considered, then over half of all food processing industry graduates are active in agrifood-related occupations six months after education and training.

Only a relatively small proportion of food processing VET graduates is unemployed or not in the labour force post-completion.

This data gives the impression that the majority of food processing graduates remain or are employed in their area of industry specialisation post-completion. However, it also implies that not all graduates remain or are employed in the industry related to their training package post-completion - hence relevant skills are not entirely captured and retained by the sponsor industry.

For the food, beverages and pharmaceuticals industry (no separate data is available for the food processing industry alone), the number of students enrolled in food-related VET qualifications as a proportion of the food, beverages and pharmaceuticals labour force (4.1 per cent) is relatively lower than in any other agrifood industry, including the seafood industry (5.2 per cent), the rural and agriculture industries (8.1 per cent), and the meat industry (7.9). It is also much lower than the proportion of the entire labour force undertaking accredited training (15.5 per cent).

The Strategy Group was told that the ongoing training of basic skills at an operator level was essential but inadequate. The National Food Institute was acknowledged as a good Registered Training Authority (RTO) model but was considered to be under-promoted. Some Strategy Group members thought that the VET system was too inflexible, and that many Registered Training Organisations (RTO) were delivering training programs that delivered on the RTOs' needs, their business plan etc, rather than the needs of industry. It is unclear whether the shortfall in education and training is being compensated for by unaccredited in-house training and on-the-job training, which is currently outside the scope of the national VET data collection.

Effects of a decline in graduate numbers (including suitably qualified and skilled graduates) and the inability of the Australian processing food sector to attract and retain skilled labour, especially in regional communities, are a potential reduction in the ability to innovate and increase productivity.

3.2.3 Packaging

'Packaging fulfils an important role in our lives. It protects food and other goods on their journey from farm or factory via warehouses and shops until they arrive at homes, offices or wherever they are used. A key role of packaging is to avoid spoilage and damage in the supply system and in the home. And yet, recent surveys show that consumers believe packaging is the top environmental problem in relation to the products they buy. Alongside addressing these concerns, packaging policy needs to contribute to greater resource efficiency and the fight against climate change, as part of the wider goal to reduce the overall environmental impacts of our supply chains'.59

Based on consumer needs, manufacturers and packers continue to invest in developing packaging that is convenient, eco-friendly and protects products. Innovation in design, materials and efficiency serves to meet cost and environmental concerns, as well as convenience, shelf-life and safety issues.

Demographic change has impacted on packaging needs for convenience food, non-food and beverages. The growing population of older consumers is leading packers to introduce packaging more suited to the needs of the elderly, such as being easier to open and lighter, as well as introducing spill-proof closures and labelling which is easier to read.

In the 2011 PwC industry survey, 31 firms surveyed thought that consumers' purchases were driven by goods with a longer shelf-life and 29 companies indicated consumer purchase was driven by a desire for improved packaging.60

'Packaging has an important marketing role. It is the "flag-bearer" of branded products. Packaging also plays an important role in differentiating competing products and can be decisive in influencing the purchasing choice made by consumers. The package as a deliverer of brand equity has to work not only on the retail shelf but "online" as well'.61

There are three broad categories of packaging in the food processing industry:

  1. Consumer packaging or primary packaging: that is, packaging which constitutes a sales unit to the final user or consumer at the point of purchase.
  2. Grouped packaging or secondary packaging: or, packaging which constitutes, at the point of purchase, a grouping of a certain number of sales units, whether the latter is sold as such to the final user, or consumer, or whether it serves only as a means to replenish the shelves at the point of sale. It can be removed from the product without affecting its characteristics.
  3. Transport packaging or tertiary packaging: that is, packaging designed to facilitate handling and transport of a number of sales units or grouped packaging in order to prevent physical handling and transport damage.

Increasingly, manufacturers are looking to merge - or better coordinate - the functions performed by these levels of packaging in order to reduce costs and facilitate good presentation. Packaging suppliers are helping to develop innovative ways to achieve this, with the total cost being less than the sum of the original parts and with functionality either unimpaired or improved.62

However, the Packaging Council of Australia acknowledges that changes in the packaging environment have occurred rapidly, with retailers now constituting the largest brand-owners; rapidly growing packaging imports from low-cost countries (such as China), and moves to package overseas. Despite this, packaging remains a fertile area for further R&D in Australia.

Food packaging is a significant contributor to waste.63 According to the Environment Protection Heritage Council (EPHC) in 2006-07 Australia generated 43.8 million tonnes of solid waste of which 4.4 million tonnes was packaging. In the food industry, while the growing demand for smaller product sizes has increased packaging waste, retailers are also contributing to increased packaging waste by requesting secondary packaging to reduce labour costs in stocking shelves. Despite this, 'waste reduction continues to be a driver for changes in food packaging, both within food processing itself and beyond (e.g. transportation and shelf-life). Companies note that waste reduction can be expected to result in cost savings (less material required and reduced waste disposal costs). There is considerable interest in reducing the loss of product from packaging failures both within the food processing plant and in transport. This is linked to the quality of packaging, with some companies reporting unsatisfactory performance from imported materials'.64

Some industry stakeholders believe that consumers have only a superficial understanding of sustainability in relation to food packaging. Sustainability goes well beyond recyclability to include such issues as the carbon footprint of different packaging materials and the energy costs involved in their recycling. Stakeholders believe that consumers are confused by the various recycling and sustainability logos and seem to accept any claims regarding sustainability. When sustainability comes at a higher cost, consumers tend to buy the lower cost item. This reduces the incentive to invest in more sustainable packaging.65

3.2.4 Freight

The food processing industry is one of the dominant users of freight logistics services in Australia, particularly refrigerated freight. Food processing companies comprise 15 of the 33 major logistics users in Australia.66 Food comprises a large part of the non-bulk freight task which is projected to grow at nearly 4 per cent annually to 2020, in terms of tonnage kms.67

Routes and modes

Road dominates the freight task on most major Australian intercity corridors, carrying over three-quarters of the non-bulk freight compared to 20 per cent for rail. The only intercity transport corridors where rail dominates are the Adelaide-Perth and Eastern States-Perth routes (see Figure 3.9).68 For a significant fraction of food freight there is no alternative to road transport, as the rail network is far less extensive than the road network. Rail transport has also become a much more expensive option too in some sectors/areas. For example, many abattoirs in Queensland were established near railway lines when Queensland Rail was a State-based asset. Since Queensland Rail's privatisation, rail has become a much more expensive option. Furthermore, the flexibility and reliability of road transport is preferred for time-sensitive food freight, particularly perishable foods.69

Figure 3.9: Inter-capital Non-bulk Freight Task and Mode Shares, 1972 and 2005

Source: BITRE, Information Sheet 34: Road and Rail Freight - Competitors or Complements?, July 2009, p6.
Text description for Inter-capital Non-bulk Freight Task and Mode Shares, 1972 and 2005

Food and live animal comprise one third of all road freight cargo on the Melbourne-Brisbane freight corridor.70 According to Coles-Myer, most of its rail food cargo is dry foods, whereas their road transport freight is primarily fresh produce.71 The Australian government has committed more than $36 billion in land transport infrastructure over the six years to 2013-14 - including road and rail funding. There are current policy reforms that include the creation of single national regulators for heavy vehicles, rail safety, and maritime transport; the National Land Freight Strategy (in development); and the National Ports Strategy.

Costs

In a recent survey of the food processing industry 46 of the 83 businesses surveyed identified transport costs as were greater than 5 per cent of total business costs.72 Transport costs are likely to be higher for rural-based businesses which have greater transport distances to cover and for SMEs which often have less access to competitive logistics services.

Goodman Fielder, one of Australia's biggest food manufacturers, has suggested that distribution accounts for almost half the delivered cost of the loaves it bakes every day of the week and then trucks to 20 000 retail outlets. These delivery costs amount to 43 per cent of each loaf, well ahead of raw materials at 30 per cent and manufacturing at 27 per cent.73

In a study by PwC on food oils and flour milling, commissioned by DIISRTE, transport represented approximately 7 per cent of total costs of production. For one WA mill, its transport expense had increased 15 per cent over the last three years. Stakeholders said these costs are expected to continue to increase at 3.5 per cent per annum. Transport and freight were noted as significant costs and a major concern for the oil-crushing industry - being the industry's third-biggest cost after the raw material and packaging material costs. Crushers stated an average transport cost of $1 000 per tonne, noting that this is higher than the total manufacturing cost. For refiners, transport was the fourth-greatest expense after raw materials, packaging materials and direct labour. Stakeholders also noted there had been large increases in these expenses: crushers had seen transport increase by 6 per cent on average over the last three years.74

Carbon price

The Australian Government is introducing a carbon price into the Australian economy from July 1 2012. Transport is the fourth-largest contributor to Australia's CO2 emissions, accounting for approximately 15 per cent of total emissions.

The Government will apply an effective carbon price on certain transport businesses, through reductions in existing fuel tax credits for rail, domestic shipping and, from 1 July 2014, for heavy vehicles. Commercial freight (with the exemption of air freight) is not subject to the carbon price in the period 2012-15.

Retail market concentration effects on transport

According to a 2003 study by Australian and New Zealand suppliers' and retailers' industry group, Efficient Consumer Response Australasia, optimisation of freight systems could reduce costs to retailers by 15-18 per cent and 4-12 per cent to suppliers.75 Subsequently, retailers have led the drive to improved freight efficiency, as suppliers were generally lagging in supply chain reform and retailers had greater potential benefits.76 Woolworths' primary freight control initiative is part of its larger Project Refresh which delivered $6 billion worth of savings to the firm in the decade since it started in 1999.77

In Australia, suppliers are likely to experience significant adverse effects as a consequence of primary retail freight control,78 as both Coles and Woolworths have adopted freight-control strategies and jointly account for 70-80 per cent of domestic dry goods food sales. As of mid-2009, Woolworths controlled 35 per cent of its primary freight task.79 A 2004 Australian study by Strategic Design & Development estimated that some suppliers may have their logistics freight volume reduced by 70 per cent, dramatically reducing their buying power for the remainder of the logistics service needs.80 A 2011 survey of 82 food manufacturing CEOs found this concern was shared by suppliers of whom 83 per cent, particularly SMEs, believed that power is too concentrated in the supply chain.81

One possible option for SMEs is to work cooperatively, to use common distribution networks and jointly commission logistics services for distribution to retailers. This method has been trialled in Finland, where 11 SMEs in the meat and bakery sectors developed two distribution networks, adopted a common online ordering system for logistics services and developed an energy passport to show the eco-value of low transport miles.82 The project offered a number of benefits: significant costs savings, greater buying power and access to logistics services, reduced transport mileage, access to new retail markets, and additional knowledge-sharing in selling and promotion of products.

3.2.5 Energy

Meat, dairy, sugar and malt are the biggest energy users. The impact of the carbon tax is expected to be significant for some sectors; for example, the Australian Meat Processor Corporation Ltd83 anticipates that the impact of a carbon price across the permit liable plants will be anywhere between $1m - $3.1m per plant in 2010 and rising in future years. This equates to a cost per head of around $5.64 per beef animal and $0.78 per sheep. Based on 2009 slaughter levels, this equates to an increased cost to industry of at least $63.5 million.

However, under-investment in energy infrastructure over past years is likely to have a greater impact. The Clean Technology Food and Foundries Investment Program will allow successful applicants in a number of high carbon-emitting industries, including the meat sector, to invest in new equipment and processes to reduce their emissions and therefore their exposure to the carbon price. Such investment may also allow meat and other manufacturers to reduce the costs of waste treatment from their processes.

CASE STUDY

Tamburlaine Winerya

Tamburlaine Winery in the Hunter Valley has reduced its overall electricity consumption by 48 per cent and refrigeration electricity use by 54 per cent following recommendations from an energy audit. The energy audit conducted through Sustainability Advantage Energy Saver showed refrigeration accounted for 75 per cent of the winery's energy use in 2010 and identified total efficiency savings of $114 000 a year. By improving running time and thermostat controls, upgrading heat exchangers and making pipework modifications, Tamburlaine has saved $54 000 a year and the investment paid for itself in less than 12 months.

(a) Westfield-Farrow Media Food Processing Network

3.2.6 Water

Efficient water use is important to the success of the food industry. In Australia, manufacturing as a whole is the second-largest industry user of water, using 5 per cent of the annual water consumption, similar to the mining industry and second only to agriculture which uses 52 per cent.84 The food, beverage and tobacco sectors consumed 2.2 per cent (301GL) of national water used, or 46 per cent of water consumed by the manufacturing industry.

Figure 3.10: Water Consumption by Sector in the Manufacturing Industry (1996-2010) (excluding manufacturing sectors transport equipment, machinery, equipment, furniture and other)

The food processing sector has consistently had the highest industry value added (IVA) in the manufacturing industry in the last 15 years. However, while all other sectors have improved water efficiency per IVA since the mid-1990s, water efficiency in the food processing sector has declined by 5 per cent (refer to Table 3.3).8586

The major components of water use in food processing are cleaning functions and process operations (see Figure 3.11). Water savings have the potential for significant cost savings, as water heating and wastewater management comprise the bulk of the costs, particularly for water used in cleaning.87 To date, water cost rises have not been sufficient to encourage major water savings. However, rising energy costs, waste water treatment costs and projected water cost increases are starting to drive greater water efficiency in the sector.
