3.2.7 Waste

Food waste is potentially a source of productivity gains. 'Food waste occurs at different points in the food supply chain, although it is most readily defined at the retail and consumer stages, where outputs of the agricultural system are self-evidently "food" for human consumption.'88

Food waste is inextricably linked to other variables in the food chain, including packaging, processing reliability, input variability and quality, the application of R&D and technology transfer. Food is wasted at all stages of the food chain - by producers, processors, retailers, caterers and consumers. One of the greatest waste producers is bakeries. In the UK, of the 8.3 million tonnes of food thrown away every year, bakery waste is the fourth-largest category, with nearly 800 000 tonnes thrown away.89

In Australia domestic food waste is very high. According to the Australian Institute,90 'Australians readily admit to spending more than $5 billion a year on food that they throw away', and while it is in the direct financial interests of most households to reduce food waste, many households continue to throw away large quantities of food. This food is the equivalent of enough to feed the entire nation for three weeks. The Institute contends that it is only through government action that the causes and consequences of household waste can be properly addressed because the problem is not a lack of awareness but one of translating this knowledge into behaviour change.

Using 'paddock to plate' calculations, WRAP91 in the UK estimates that one ton of food waste generates 3.8 tons of CO2 equivalent emissions. Using the same figures, food waste in Australia is responsible for an estimated 15.4 million tons of CO2 equivalent emissions every year. However, there is not only a cost to the atmosphere, but in the inputs to produce the food. CSIRO data indicate that dumping a kilogram of beef wastes the 50 000 litres of water it took to produce that meat and throwing out a kilogram of white rice will waste 2385 litres.

It is not just governments that regard food waste as a major issue. For example, in its submission to the National Food Plan, the Sanitarium Food Company recommended that waste minimisation, including that of water, energy and materials, be a priority for all stakeholders.92

The results of the PwC industry survey found that most food processing businesses thought that waste management was fundamental to business. Of the 92 businesses surveyed, 39 indicated that waste management was critical - both commercially and economically (22 per cent for both).

The short shelf-life of products was identified as the primary source of waste produced within the business (by 15); however, poor process control (13) and poor-quality materials (11) were also perceived to be contributing factors. Those businesses within the dairy, grain/cereal, and bakery industries were more likely to claim that short shelf-life is the source of the highest level of waste within their business - which was of little surprise given the nature of their products.

Three-quarters of businesses claimed to have taken some form of action to reduce waste production via their manufacturing and processing methods (48). The second-most likely way businesses had addressed the problem of waste production was to recycle waste products (33). A quarter claimed they had sourced better quality materials (25). Those in the meat, oil/fat, grain/cereal and beverage industries were more likely to have sourced better-quality ingredients (qualitative observation due to low base sizes).

Improved packaging (not necessarily the amount of) can prevent considerable food waste.

  • It has been alleged that some retailers require their suppliers to pack product to even tighter weight specifications than those required by legislation, effectively demanding that no given pack of a product have 'short-weight' even if that shortfall is within the tolerances allowed by regulation. This means that manufacturers would need to lift their average target weight, or rework any product below the minimum weight. This would result in waste and rework and lost productivity.

3.3 Investment and Asset Costs

3.3.1 Plant and equipment

During the 1970s and 1980s there was significant growth in the variety of processed food products; however, the changing nature this market caused a movement away from standard food machinery products to a diverse market of specialised food machinery (typically smaller machines for specific niche products).

The cost of developing and producing equipment in the food processing industry can be high, as much depends on sophisticated technology. The Australian industry is small. Germany, Italy and the United States are the major sources of imported food processing machinery for the domestic market, with an estimated 54.5 per cent share anticipated by 2015-16.93 A high Australian dollar offers opportunity for investment in overseas equipment.

Some Strategy Group members think that the age of the assets of Australian processed food firm's results in higher labour costs compared to similar enterprises overseas. However, a study commissioned by the Prime Minister's Manufacturing Taskforce suggests that: 'there is no obvious problem with the age of capital in manufacturing, [including processed food manufacturing] although again associated with poor trading conditions manufacturing investment has fallen relative to GDP since the Global Financial Crisis'.94

By end 2016-17 private capital expenditure on machinery and equipment is expected to have increased at an annualised rate of 5.1 per cent and be worth $135.8 billion.95 Analysis of machinery expenditure growth over the last 45 years suggests that the life-cycle exhibits a fairly regular pattern. IBIS World estimates a life-cycle commodity period of approximately eight years.

Today there is an increasing trend to invest in more energy-efficient machinery and equipment in the food and beverage processing sector to save on operating costs and to ensure the longer-term sustainability of the industry.96

Beer and malt are largely produced under large-scale factory conditions and are capital-intensive. This sub-sector has the greatest value added per employee in 2008-09 ($481 000 per employee in 2010-11 dollar terms). A large share of bakery production takes place under small-scale operations.97 This sub-sector has the lowest value added per employee in 2008-09 ($57 000 per employee in 2010-11 dollar terms.

While maintenance and repairs can be used to extend machinery's effective life, changes and development of new technologies can mean manufacturers can no longer compete with competitors (despite having relatively new equipment) due to commercial obsolescence. This is particularly problematic for SMEs which do not have the necessary capital to adapt to these changes. In many cases manufacturers may continue with operations at reduced productivity levels and profit margins rather than abandon the asset altogether.

In the 2011 PwC industry survey, of the 92 businesses surveyed, the majority claimed to invest in innovation (80); however, most of the investment was below 5 per cent of total costs (55). The areas of the business which seemed to receive most of the innovation investment were plant improvements (73), new product formulations (60) and product extensions (52).

The development of new processing techniques can influence the nature of products, especially when they extend their life or enhance flavour. There is great diversity in the food processing sector and the type of machinery used is often highly specialised and sometimes purpose-built.

IBISWorld estimates major markets for Australian machinery uptake are the meat and seafood processing sectors.98 These sectors are converting to energy-efficient machinery to reduce operating costs. Upgrades to increase automation have also influenced growth. In fact, food businesses generally are looking to reduce waste and prepare for a carbon price.

The issue with plant and equipment may not be that of its age, but its underutilisation. Of 92 respondents to the PwC survey, 68 said they had the capacity to increase the number of shifts. Forty-seven respondents said that market demand was preventing greater sales, compared to just 21 who said line capacity constraints were the problem. Underutilisation of capital can mean administrative overheads represent a greater portion of unit costs. It is likely greater utilisation of capital would increase productivity. The issue of scale is addressed in Chapter Four.

3.3.2 Intellectual property

Overall, the total number of Australian patents granted to Australians has been growing slowly, at approximately 0.8 per cent per annum since 2000.99 The technology group quantifying agriculture and food-related machinery is one of the highest areas of patenting in Australia, ranking eighth. However, the agriculture and food patenting (not related to machinery) is typically in the bottom 20 per cent technology group in terms of granted patents in Australia.

As the following table shows post-2006, worldwide applications for Australian patents dropped dramatically.100 Based on the number of applications since 2006, we can expect patents awarded to fall in the next few years. Despite this expected slump, food-related patents awarded to Australians have recovered, increasing by approximately 20 per cent, and total food-related patents granted to Australians has grown faster in this area than any other technology group between 2005-2009.However, many patents awarded post 2006 are likely to have been applied for in the period 2003-06, so this increase may reflect to some extent this earlier activity.101

Figure 3.12: Patent Applications from Australians and all Applicants, 1989-2011

Source: IP Australia, private communication, 9 Feb 2012.
Text description for Patent Applications from Australians and all Applicants, 1989-2011

Only 12 per cent of Australian manufacturing firms hold patents.102 Food manufacturers comprise two-thirds of manufacturing SMEs; however, only 0.23 per cent of food-manufacturing SMEs have intellectual property investments.103 Patent rates, however, do not accurately represent innovation in food manufacturing firms, as patents are not appropriate for most innovation activities by these firms. A significant fraction of food business innovation is likely to be in new formulations, process operations and supply chain management, which is only weakly reflected in R&D expenditure and patenting.

3.3.3 Innovation

R&D funding and patent issuing are important measures of innovative practice, but they measure only one component of innovation and do not track things such as improved business processes, new business models, new services and creative cultural products.104

Innovation can be open or closed. Closed innovation is the traditional model. It involves companies undertaking all R&D within the business, requiring control and confidentiality, and not necessarily producing the optimum solutions. Business bears all the costs under this model and similar activities are duplicated across the industry. Intellectual property (IP) which is not used is 'locked up'. Businesses generally innovate when they believe that the effort will bring higher margins and/or accelerated growth, and not for the social and economic benefits of the country as a whole.105

Open innovation occurs by combining internal and external ideas, as well as internal and external paths to market, to advance the development of new technologies.106. It involves a flexible approach and an open policy on IP. Access to knowledge and technology from outside the organisation is utilised in order to achieve: shorter development times and 'speed to market'; reduced R&D costs; IP leverage, and co-investment and development of innovation networks.

Process innovation occurs when new or significantly improved production or delivery techniques are employed. A number of Australian governments provide assistance to food businesses to improve processes and share knowledge. For example, Innovate SA and the SA Food Centre work closely with industry to improve process development in South Australia.

Product innovation involves the introduction of any good or service with fundamental characteristics or intended uses that differ significantly from other products or services offered by the industry.

Of the 92 respondents to the PwC survey, 79 claimed they had invested in plant/equipment innovation improvements, 65 had claimed they had invested in new product formulations, 57 had done product extensions.

In Smith and West's view 'Australia has two fundamental needs concerning the future of innovation in Australia. The first is for a modified knowledge infrastructure policy, less focused on direct commercialisation and more orientated towards the generic and scientific knowledge bases that underpin the Australian economy. The second is a modified business development system, addressing the real characteristics of the innovation faced by business(es)'.107

Industry consultations indicate that there is a need for the food industry in Australia to create sustainable competitive advantage through innovation along the value chain. Valuable innovation in the food processing industry has occurred in Australia; however, the extent to which it has been shared in the 'open space' is unclear.

3.3.4 Research and development

Australia's food industry has a history of and is capable of being highly innovative and adaptable to new challenges, and its significant R&D108 capabilities have the capacity to make it a competitive player in the international markets. As the world's food prices rise (driven by population growth, climate change, 'peak' oil and phosphorous, among other reasons), it is vital that continued investment is made in R&D for the development of new processes and technologies. To encourage R&D the Australian Government provides tax incentives to businesses.

R&D Tax Incentive

The current R&D Tax Incentive (which replaced the R&D Tax Concession) applies to income years commencing on or after 1 July 2011. The two core components of the new program are:

  • a 45 per cent refundable tax offset for company groups with aggregated annual turnover of less than $20 million, doubling the support to 15 cents in the dollar and
  • a 40 per cent non-refundable tax offset for all other company groups, increasing the base rate support by a third to 10 cents in the dollar.

The new R&D Tax Incentive features higher base rates of assistance for all genuine R&D conducted by eligible businesses. There is particular emphasis on supporting R&D conducted by smaller businesses and innovative start-up companies. These companies typically need cash flow to support their R&D activities and the new incentive features significantly greater access to refundable tax offsets

To further improve the access to the R&D Tax Incentive for small and medium enterprises, the Government will introduce quarterly credits from 1 January 2014. Quarterly credits will provide smaller firms with the option to receive the benefit of the R&D Tax Incentive as instalments during the income year in which they undertake their R&D activities. This will get the benefit sooner to these enterprises and significantly improve their cash flow and incentive to invest in R&D.

Industry is supportive of the Government's R&D Tax Incentive and many businesses in the food and beverage processing industry will benefit from the assistance provided by the Government.

The new program includes a 'dominant purpose' test for supporting R&D Activities in some circumstances. Generally, supporting R&D activities will be eligible for the R&D offset concession where they are 'activities directly related to core activities'. However, a stricter 'dominant purpose' applies to supporting core R&D activities if the activities: appear on the 'exclusions list' (such as market research, prospecting, management studies, and so on); or are (or directly related to) the production of goods or services. A Strategy Group member has suggested that this requirement may potentially prevent some businesses from applying/accessing this program.

Generally, only R&D activities conducted in Australia qualify for the R&D tax incentive. However, R&D activities conducted overseas also qualify if Innovation Australia makes a finding that the activities meet the conditions specified in section 28D of the Industry Research and Development Act 1986'.

R&D Expenditure

According to the Australian Bureau of Statistics, R&D expenditure on food product manufacturing totalled $427.8 million in 2009-10, an increase of approximately 9.9 per cent over the previous year (refer to Figure 3.13). Since 2005-06, R&D expenditure on food product manufacturing has consistently increased by a margin greater than 5 per cent on a yearly basis. It was one of the few subdivisions within the manufacturing industry to increase as the advent of GFC set in from late-2007 to mid-2009.

Figure 3.13: R&D Expenditure by Manufacturing Subdivision

Source: ABS 8104.0 Research and Experimental Development, Businesses, Australia

Unsurprisingly, given that the bulk of food processing businesses are located in Victoria, Queensland and NSW most of the R&D expenditure increases in food product manufacturing between 2005-06 and 2009-10 occurred in New South Wales, Victoria and Queensland, and close to 89.6 per cent of total R&D expenditure was in these states.

The business human resources devoted to R&D on food product manufacturing in 2009-10 totalled 2104 person years of effort (PYE), an increase of 23.8 per cent from 2005-06 (refer to Table 3.4).

The Australian Bureau of Statistics classifies R&D activities according to the type of research effort, namely, pure basic research, strategic basic research, applied research and experimental development. A large proportion of the R&D expenditure on food product manufacturing goes to experimental development, which involves using existing knowledge gained from research or practical experience that is directed to producing new materials, products, devices, policies, behaviours or outlooks; to installing new processes, systems and services; or to improving substantially those already produced or installed.

Food product manufacturing R&D expenditure on experimental development has increased by approximately 35.1 per cent from 2005-06 to 2009-10 (refer to Table 3.5); however, the proportional movements of other categories during this period were much higher. According to the ABS report, pure basic research increased by 75.4 per cent, strategic basic research increased by 46.0 per cent and applied research increased by 49.0 per cent (albeit from relatively low absolute expenditure).

A significant amount of the R&D expenditure in food product manufacturing industry is undertaken by the businesses themselves, i.e. the industry invested $421.2 million out of a total of $427.8 million in 2009-10 (Table 3.6). This includes all funding for R&D sourced from the business itself, including: equity; borrowings; retained earnings; and non-R&D specific transfers from related entities. More broadly, the R&D expenditure across the whole of the food supply chain by the Australian Government totalled $715.0 million in 2008-09 (Productivity Commission 2011). This includes investment in rural R&D corporations, CSIRO, Cooperative Research Centres and other avenues. In the same year, state and territory government rural R&D investment was $416.0 million and private/industry investment was $364.0 million.109 CSIRO's specific investment in the processed food sector R&D has focused on the following areas over the last three years:

  • Food safety
  • Novel processes (such as high pressure processing)
  • Technologies leading to new food ingredients such as microencapsulation and
  • Foods for health (such as new grain products e.g. BarleyMax).

In 2009-10 CSIRO's R&D investment in these areas totalled an estimated $17.6 million; for 2010-11 the estimated investment was $20.1 million; and for 2011-12 the figure was $23.5 million. 110

The R&D expenditure on food product manufacturing by businesses with 0-4 employees has decreased significantly by approximately 28.1 per cent while businesses with 5-19, 20-199 and 200 or more employees increased by 107.3 per cent, 55.9 per cent and 36.8 per cent respectively. Results from the PwC survey suggest that the surveyed large food processors' R&D investment is about 1.0 to 1.3 per cent of sales, which is expected to continue. Smaller companies invest less than 0.5 per cent of sales on R&D. The Commonwealth tax rebate provided to companies from the R&D Tax Incentive and prior similar programs is not quantified in any of the figures provided.

CASE STUDY

Devro in Bathurst

Devro is a leading supplier of collagen casings for food, used by customers in the production of a wide variety of sausages and other meat products. It initially pioneered the development of edible collagen casing as part of Johnson & Johnson. Through the assistance of the Regional Development Board and the Bathurst/Orange Development Corporation, it has been able to develop independently. Its R&D is mostly done in-house with linkages to CSIRO in regards to hide processing to extract collagen and leather. The company has developed an innovative design for the scissoring and twisting equipment which has lowered its energy costs.

Types of innovation

The reasons given by processors for the high rate of new product introduction included:

  • supermarkets' desire for innovation and diversity
  • high levels of competition and large food processors wanting to increase their market share of a declining branded product market
  • the need to renew core products and
  • the revenue from the lifecycle of a product is decreasing.

For smaller companies, new product development is limited and tends to focus on niche or private labels, with a higher failure rate of branded products reported. A recent Review of Victoria's Food Industry Requirements for Pilot Food Processing Plant and Product Innovation Services commissioned by the Victorian Department of Primary Industry which surveyed a number of key food processors in that state (including several SMEs) concluded, inter alia, that: 'There has been a shift towards a defensive innovation strategy to "stay in business" with an emphasis on process and product innovation to reduce costs. This has come at the expense of product innovation to "grow the export market" and investment in break through innovation to "create options for future business growth".'111

The Australian food processing industry has the capacity to grow as the result of innovative solutions112 such as:

  • Collaborative partnerships - sharing of skill sets/new processes reducing costs and risks -'sharing means saving', particularly where government is one of the partners
  • Open innovation (enabling SMEs to collaborate with large firms)
  • Proactive skills development and 'knowledge infrastructure' in universities
  • ICT solutions in the 'paddock to plate' supply chain, such as bar codes, radio frequency identification devices and remote sensors
  • Niche marketing: organic foods, health-enhancing or functional foods, boutique products such as cheese, honey and beer
  • Increasing proximity of food processing facilities to agricultural inputs and final markets facilitated by a range of factors including improved transport logistics
  • Biotechnological applications to breeding and growing to improve productivity in agriculture
  • Innovative packaging and
  • Improvements in inventory management.
Biotechnology - gene technology

Gene technology (one aspect of biotechnology) refers to techniques to alter/introduce new characteristics to organisms by modification of genes. Gene technology can be used to produce genetically modified organisms (GMOs). 113 In Australia, the Office of the Gene Technology Regulator (OGTR) oversees the development and environmental release of GMOs under the Gene Technology Act 2000.

A bi-national government agency, Food Standards Australia New Zealand, is responsible for the regulation of all food products for retail sale, whether domestically produced or imported. GM foods are regulated under Standard 1.5.2 - Food produced using Gene Technology of the Australia New Zealand Food Standards Code. A GM food means a food which has been derived or developed from an organism which has been modified by gene technology. Standard 1.5.2 (an enforceable law) has two provisions - mandatory pre-market approval (based on a food safety assessment) and mandatory labeling requirements. New GM foods are assessed by a scientific comparison with the conventional counterpart food with a history of safe use. The safety assessment focuses on the properties of the new gene product(s), including potential allergenicity and toxicity, and examines any compositional differences in the food compared with the conventional form.

The benefits of genetically modified food/crops continue to be disputed domestically despite continued international growth. Of the 29 countries planting biotech crops in 2011, 19 were developing and 10 were industrialised countries. 114 Five countries - the United States, Brazil, Argentina, India and Canada grow more than 90 per cent of the world's GM crops. Only two GM food crops, cotton and canola, are approved for cultivation in Australia. As well as these, various types of GM soybean, canola, corn and cotton produced overseas have been approved by FSANZ to enter the food market in Australia. As at June 2012, 56 GM foods are approved for use in Australia and New Zealand, and a further two applications are undergoing assessment.115

Studies within the agricultural sector (e.g. cotton industry) have demonstrated how first-generation issues of environmental stress (heat, salt, nutrient deficiency, pest and disease resistance) can improve with GM technology. In addition, GM technology has the potential to address supply and demand concerns, reduce costs, improve innovation and maintain sustainable industries.116

GM technology still faces negative commentary for a variety of reasons, encompassing broad social, environmental, philosophical or ethical views in the production of food. Sceptics argue that long-term impacts of GM technology cannot be measured effectively due to the relatively short period for which the technology has been implemented - i.e. 2011 marked the 16th year of the commercialisation of biotech crops117.

However, the 2010 European Commission report analysing EU-funded GMO research concluded that biotechnology and, in particular, GMOs were not per se more risky than conventional plant breeding techniques.118 This report was based on more than 130 research projects covering a period of more than 25 years of research and involving more than 500 independent research groups.

Current mechanisms for engagement in public research

Technological know-how is highly developed in Australian public and private sectors. It is critical that there are mechanisms for industry to drive public research. Our public research, especially in food and agriculture, is one of Australia's strengths (see Chapter Four).

The Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry (DAFF) has been instrumental in supporting the development of strategies for various food sectors, including the pork, seafood, dairy, sheep meat, poultry and sugarcane industries. CSIRO leads the Food and Nutrition Leaders Strategy that will add to these and report to the Primary Industries Ministerial Council (PIMC). The R&D Strategy being developed by the Food and Nutrition Leaders will be available in late 2012.

The Cooperative Research Centres (CRC) Program supports end-user driven research collaborations to address major challenges facing Australia, many of which are global challenges. Current and past food-related CRCs are listed in Appendix A. There are no CRCs funded directly for the food processing sector.

The CSIRO has suggested that research efforts in the food processing sector in Australia have been historically concentrated on large national challenges and opportunities which have resulted in a well-focused, multi-disciplinary science approach. However, this has resulted in comparative neglect of many small and medium enterprises. To address this problem the CSIRO has established a SME Engagement Centre which focuses on a small number of very innovative SMEs who have the skills to adopt new technologies.

R&D investment is intrinsic to improving productivity and profitability in existing industries and supporting the development of new, embryonic industries. The food manufacturing industry believes more must be done to encourage collaboration between the private and public sector, and through this report suggests a number of strategies to achieve this.

3.3.5 Packaging innovation

In the 2011 PwC industry survey of 92 businesses, 48 thought that innovation in packaging would lead to significant gains in productivity and seven businesses said that it would lead to the biggest gain.

A number of submissions to the National Food Plan highlighted the importance of packaging to maintaining a sustainable food processing industry. Owens-Illinois, for example, stressed the importance of ensuring Australia's competitiveness across the food chain in order to reduce the increasing trend of importing fully packaged finished products, in part by considering the sustainability attributes of different packaging types that reduce environmental impact.

In the active packaging technology arena, chemical absorbers and emitters have been adopted in niche applications to preserve shelf-life and the quality of foods and beverages. This technology is used in the packaging of canned perishable food, such as canned fruits, beans and meat, among others. The development of absorbers and emitters that can be suitably integrated into packaging materials is expected to present a major opportunity for packagers worldwide.

Recently, low-permeability barrier materials have been developed and commercialised, and new polymers introduced for their barrier properties and heat resistance. Additives to plastic packaging provide UV resistance, improve biodegradability, and increase electrical or thermal conductivity. 119

In food processing technology, notable advances have been made to aseptic and retort packaging, self-cooling technology, non-destructive quality control mechanisms for monitoring contamination and the bond strength of seals. Food, beverage and non-food categories are all likely to see further development in terms of packaging requirements in the near future.

Packaging technology such as time-temperature indicators (TTIs) are now widely used for a diverse array of commercial applications. The Coors Light cold-activated label is a good example of packaging that features a target temperature indicator. Thermal ink on the product's label changes from white to blue when the beer has been appropriately chilled. Biosensors are considered to be a promising area for future development and will be helpful in moving towards intelligent packaging. Biosensors measure biological activities, such as microbiological growth, and can indicate the freshness of food.120

Product packaging has also been used to improve sustainability. Coca-Cola's plant bottle is comprised of recyclable materials - 30 per cent plant-based sources and 70 per cent recycled plastic (PET polyethylene terephthalate). Plantic have commercialised an eco plastic which is of a multi-layer structure, predominately consisting of Plantic biopolymer which is certified as biodegradable and compostable (see case study overleaf).

CASE STUDY

Plantic - An Australian Success Story

Plantic Technologies was incorporated in Victoria in 2001 for the purposes of acquiring, developing and commercialising intellectual property developed by the Cooperative Research Centre (CRC) for International Food Manufacture and Packaging Science.

The CRC identified that a range of cornstarch-based formulations could be used to manufacture a flat sheet bioplastic, which then could be thermoformed to produce biodegradable trays. This discovery led to the lodgement of a provisional patent in 1999.

In 2002, Plantic was granted ownership of all the intellectual property by the CRC. By July 2003, Plantic had established supply to Cadbury Schweppes (Australia) and Kenman Kandy, a division of Mars/Masterfoods. The company officially launched its Plantic® material and its presence to the packaging industry in August 2003 at the Auspack Trade Show in Melbourne, Australia and won two packaging awards for its Cadbury Milk chocolate box rigid tray.

In 2004, Plantic began operations in Europe, in close proximity to several leaders in the European food and confectionery industry. In 2005 Plantic Technologies Ltd commenced supply the European market with market leader clients such as M&S and Nestlé. Since 2005 Plantic Technologies Ltd has established distribution agreements with global leaders and customised its technology to suit an ever-broader range of conversion techniques and applications, including thermoforming, injection moulding, film extrusion and blow moulding, as well as rigid and flexible packaging.

In 2009, a revolutionary bioplastic was developed that would change the nature of plastics globally. Plantic's eco plastic is a multi-layer structure, predominately consisting of Plantic biopolymer which is certified as biodegradable and compostable. The moisture protective skins are necessary as containment and a sealing layer for industry standard lidding technology. Future development of skins structures will enable a 100 per cent biopolymer construction.

Plantic has a packaging supply contract with Coles. In October 2011, Coles were awarded the Bronze Award for Sustainable Packaging by the Packaging Council of Australia for their Plantic ecoPlastic™ retail meat trays. In May 2012 Plantic reopened their manufacturing facilities following extensive upgrading to increase capacity by 400 per cent.

3.4 Regulatory Costs

3.4.1 Regulatory environment

The Australia and New Zealand Food Regulation System is a bi-national arrangement involving the Australian Government, State and Territory Governments and the New Zealand Government. In Australia, food regulation involves all three levels of government.

One of the most important features of the system is the separation of policy decision making from the development of standards. Policy is agreed by the Legislative and Governance Forum on Food Regulation (FoFR) and is then referred to by Food Standards Australia New Zealand (FSANZ) to develop food standards for the Australia and New Zealand Food Standards Code.

FSANZ is an independent statutory authority established under the Food Standards Australia New Zealand Act 1991.

The food regulation system is underpinned by the following instruments and governance structures:

  • The Food Regulation Agreement (FRA), an Intergovernmental agreement with the State and Territories committing to a national system of food regulation
  • the Agreement between the Government of Australia and the Government of New Zealand Concerning a Joint Food Standards System
  • the FoFR, a forum made up of Australian, State, Territory and New Zealand Ministers with responsibility for food. The FoFR is chaired by the Australian Government Parliamentary Secretary. The FoFR is responsible for developing food regulation policy guidelines for domestic food standards, and for promoting a consistent approach to compliance with, and the enforcement of food standards. The FoFR can also amend or reject food standards and request that these be reviewed
  • the Food Regulation Standing Committee (FRSC), a high-level officials group which develops policy advice, as well as providing advice on the best ways to involve stakeholders in policy development and
  • the Implementation Sub Committee (ISC), a senior government officials group, which facilitates consistent implementation and enforcement of food regulations and standards.

Australia is held in high regard worldwide for its safe and clean food supply, and the system in place is certainly critical in the maintenance of that reputation. However, there are still a number of issues associated with the application of Australian food standards and related regulation, including:

  • differences in food safety regulations and enforcement between the States and Territories
  • the ability of New Zealand to choose to vary or opt out of some standards
  • biosecurity fees
  • costs and time delays in changes to the Food Standards Code. Industry has advised that applications may cost between $50 000 and $250 000, and take many months to be approved. An unpaid application may take a further 6-9 months; and
  • the Model Food Provisions contained in Annex A and Annex B, gives effect to a commitment by Commonwealth, State and Territory governments to a national approach to food regulation in Australia. However, to date there is still some inconsistency between the food acts in each jurisdiction. A 2007 AFGC study121 comparing State and Territory food Acts to the Model Food Bill found major variations, with jurisdictional legislation and differing levels of prioritisation, resourcing and enforcement of food safety. The different mandatory food safety requirements and degrees of enforcement in each jurisdiction cause large unnecessary costs for food manufacturers operating in multiple jurisdictions.

Two major national retailers (Coles Group and Woolworths) raised concerns about inconsistencies across jurisdictions in training competency requirements for staff working with and selling liquor. For example, each jurisdiction had different requirements for Responsible Service of Alcohol (RSA) training and, with the exception of Western Australia, will not recognise RSA training that is completed in another jurisdiction. This imposes a barrier to the transferability of staff between stores in different states since staff must be retrained to work in another jurisdiction. The AFGC claimed that the development of separate food hygiene regulations by individual states and territories had resulted in differing application of those rules.

In 2008 the Productivity Commission Report on Regulatory Burdens on Businesses in the Manufacturing and Distributive Trades122 recommended that the Model Food Bill (now the FRA) be implemented consistently across all jurisdictions unless there was demonstrable need for local or regional requirements, and that enforcement of the regulations be the responsibility of the Commonwealth.

Applications by industry to amend the food safety standards can constitute large direct costs to manufacturers (typically over $100 000), and further indirect costs of opportunities lost (including inability to compete with imports not subject to the same standards) due to delays of several years in application processing. Such applications must be submitted to FSANZ for consideration, before assessment or potentially subsequent requests for reviews by the FoFR. A number of applications have taken excessive lengths of time to reach a resolution.

For example, the AFGC claimed that the application process by Australian Beverages Council to enable domestic manufacturers to produce vitamin-fortified beverages was estimated to have caused an opportunity cost of $350 million and took four years to complete.123 Similarly, it took four years to approve calcium fortification of juices and biscuits.124 Such costs are likely to be prohibitively large for many MNEs; much more for SMEs.

The Productivity Commission Report also recommended:

  • amendment of the FSANZ Act to improve the timeliness of the development and amendment of food standards
  • amendments to ensure new food standard proposals include a full regulation impact statement demonstrating the cost-benefits of the proposal and
  • changes to the FRA to improve the transparency of the FoFR decision-making process.

The National Partnership to Deliver a Seamless National Economy specified the National Partnership Implementation Plan. In reference to this plan, in December 2010, the COAG Reform Council identified that in relation to food regulatory reforms, that the following reforms had been completed:

  • All jurisdictions through FoFR (then known as the Australia and New Zealand Food Regulation Ministerial Council) to undertake a comprehensive review of food labelling law and policy, with a progress report to COAG by July 2009.
  • The Commonwealth to provide a report to COAG, by May 2010 on progress in redrafting the Food Regulation Agreement reflecting revised FoFR voting arrangements.
  • The Commonwealth to provide a report to COAG, by May 2010 on progress in drafting the intergovernmental agreement confirming the Australian Government and the State and Territory Governments commitment to establish a centralised interpretive advisory service.

Table 3.7 shows the FSANZ application fee structure (based on an hourly assessment rate of $115 per hour). There is no charge to an applicant unless an exclusive 'capturable' commercial benefit applies to the application or where an applicant chooses to pay a fee for work on their application to be expedited. FSANZ is currently reviewing its cost recovery model which includes a proposal to increase its hourly rate from $115 an hour to $180 per hour.125

There are also a number of recognised food safety management systems - primarily based on the Hazard Analysis Critical Control Point (HACCP) principles - under which food manufacturers must be certified to do business. Manufacturers can be certified by a HACCP auditor; however, most manufacturers supplying to multiple retailers will choose certification against one of the standards below, and often have additional certification requirements imposed by the retailers. Manufacturers must fulfil and pay for these mandatory certification requirements of their retailers, in addition to any other retailer specific certification requirements imposed by the retailer to differentiate their quality. Examples of international food safety standards are:

  • British Retail Consortium (BRC) Global Standard for Food Safety
    • Grade A and B: audited every 12 months
    • Grade C: audited every six months
  • ISO 22000 Food Safety Management System Standard
  • SQF 2000 Code127
  • AS/NZS ISO 9001:2008 Quality Management System Standard
    • Overlaps with approximately half of the BRC, but has a significant number of unique additional requirements
    • Applicable to a range of industries (including food, hospitality, utilities, resources, construction, agriculture, etc)

Some high-risk food sectors, such as water, meat, seafood, eggs and organic foods, have specific certification requirements.

Food manufacturers supplying branded and private label products for the major supermarket and retail food service providers have a mandatory requirement to comply with the retailers' QA programs. These requirements are different to the certification required under jurisdictional law, but involve substantial duplication, and require additional periodic audits (typically 6-12 months apart) imposing a significant cost overhead (approximately $1000-2000 for each Woolworths Quality Assurance standard audit128). Consultation with manufacturers has indicated that large manufacturers may face up to 50 QA audits per year, causing direct costs of over $100 000 and imposing indirect costs several times higher.

Some of the QA programs of the major grocery and food service retailers are listed below:

  • Woolworths Quality Assurance (WQA) standard129assessing quality and food safety
    • Involves an audit every six months
    • Applies to private label and branded product
    • Some similarities to the BRC, but many additional requirements
  • Coles Supplier Requirements (CSR)130
    • Period audit with frequency dependent on previous audit outcome
    • Applies to private label products only
    • Required in addition to one of five alternative industry standards:
      • BRC Global Standard for Food
      • Global G.A.P131
      • Freshcare132
      • SQF1000-L3133
      • SQF2000-L3134
  • Metcash Food Safety Program
  • Aldi QA Checklist
    • Required in addition to BRC standard certification
    • Involves an audit every 12 months
      • Applies to all products
  • McDonalds Supplier Quality Management System Program135
  • Qantas
    • Requires ISO 22000 certification of suppliers
      • Spotless Food Safety Standard for Suppliers

The value of QA audits is not disputed as they ensure that Australia's reputation as a clean, safe food producer is maintained. However, the information sought by the separate audits required by each jurisdictions and major retailers, and which are undertaken across various standards are duplicative, amounting to substantial and potentially unnecessary increased direct and indirect costs, and ambiguity in regulatory requirements. This was acknowledged by Woolworths when it gave evidence to the Senate Select Committee on Australia's Food Processing Sector.136

3.4.2 Trade and tariffs

Australia's agriculture and food sectors are heavily dependent on exports, and hence have a major stake in the maintenance of an open world trading system. Australia provides competitive agricultural and food produce to world markets without the high levels of financial support, protection and other trade-distorting practices used by many other countries to prop up these sectors. An enthusiastic participant in World Trade Organisation (WTO) negotiations, Australia has sought to promote policies that encourage trade liberalisation and create a more open market for agriculture and food businesses.

Internationally, agriculture and food traders, including Australian traders, are severely disadvantaged by trade distortion. The three main types of support distort measures are market access barriers, domestic subsidies and export subsidies. These measures are intended to subsidise the producer's income in the country imposing them, and they impact adversely on international production and trade-flow decisions.

Australia has led by example, through reforms which saw it reduce its tariffs on agricultural and food products in the early 1970s. These reforms have led to productivity growth in the agriculture and food sector and to benefits to the consumer.

A sustained and concerted effort is required through multilateral, bilateral and regional trade negotiations and agreements to tackle the barriers to trade. Raising tariff barriers works in exactly the opposite direction to what is needed. Lowering trade barriers encourages international trade and stimulate demand, and in so doing reduce the impacts of a financial crisis elsewhere137.

3.4.3 Quarantine

Australia's quarantine policy is crucial to ensuring the protection of human, animal and plant health. To safeguard against exotic pests and diseases, Australia has adopted a stringent approach to imported agricultural products. Having such measures help protects our agricultural industries and protects or exports. These conservative quarantine settings have been justified by a series of disease outbreaks over the recent years, including the bovine spongiform encephalopathy (BSE or 'mad cow' disease), foot and mouth disease and avian influenza (bird flu). However, in the eyes of some members of the World Trade Organization, Australia's strict quarantine requirements are seen as a 'technical trade barrier' used to keep out foreign competitors. DFAT is working with WTO members to counter any misunderstandings they may have about our quarantine process.

There are benefits to having a disease-free status as unexpected export opportunities can be realised as was the case in the lucrative beef market. In 2003, United States reported a single case of BSE. This discovery had a profound impact on beef trade patterns in the Asia-Pacific, especially in Japan where the demand for and value of Australian beef increased substantially.

DAFF Biosecurity is in charge of controlling Australia's quarantine regulations which are designed to prevent the introduction, establishment and spread of human, animal and plant pests and diseases. A vast majority of the agricultural and food produce exported from Australia is inspected by DAFF Biosecurity. The costs associated with these inspection services, which are rigorous, have undergone considerable change over the last few years.

In November 2001 the Government introduced a 40 per cent contribution toward the costs of DAFF Biosecurity Export activities, whereby the DAFF Biosecurity Export Programs operated under a 60 per cent cost-recovery arrangement, with the remaining 40 per cent of costs met by the Government contribution. This funding lapsed on 30 June 2009 and required that fees and charges for DAFF Biosecurity Export Programs return to a 100 per cent cost-recovery arrangement. Since that time the costs in the program have increased and it is causing considerable aggravation to processors and exporters who are blaming it for affecting their ability to compete in the international market.

In response to these concerns, the Government provided $127.4 million for the Export Certification Reform Package project. This project developed revised service delivery arrangements for the export sectors to reduce regulatory costs and introduced efficiencies into the export process to generally reduce costs for exporters.

If we observe the DAFF biosecurity costs associated with the Meat Export Program (refer to Table 3.8), these clearly indicates that inspection service costs have increased significantly over the last few years. DAFF biosecurity export inspection costs have been discussed by the Export Meat Industry Advisory Committee (EMIAC) which is chaired and run by DAFF biosecurity with representation from all major export industry bodies.

One of the main issues with the inspection service fees is that they appear to have grown rapidly, creating an excessive burden on small businesses, cutting their margins and putting them in a precarious position. In addition, industry does not believe that it is consulted sufficiently. A stringent quarantine regulation regime is necessary to compete in the export market; however, a more through consultation process is required with regards to fee structure, which should take into account smaller players in the market.
