3.4.4 Taxation

In 2010, the final report of the Australia's Future Tax System Review (the Henry Tax Review) was released. The Review examined Australian and state government taxes. Some of the recommendations of the Review were welcomed by industry and business groups, including a reduction in the company income tax rate and new small business asset write-off and depreciation rules.

The Government's response to the Review's recommendations supported a number of recommendations in principle. Some of the initiatives that will benefit business include:

  • From the 2012-13 income year small businesses will be able to write off the full value of eligible business assets in the year of purchase (including motor vehicles costing less than $6500). Small businesses will be able to depreciate other assets (other than buildings) in a single general small business pool - saving money and paperwork.
  • Small businesses will be able to depreciate assets costing more than $6500 (other than buildings) in a single depreciation pool at a rate of 30 per cent (15 per cent in the first year). The simplified depreciation pooling arrangements will also allow small businesses to depreciate some longer-life assets more quickly (at a rate of 30 per cent instead of 5 per cent), and will help to reduce compliance costs.
  • A one year loss carry-back will apply in 2012-13 where tax losses incurred in that year can be carried back and offset against tax paid in 2011-12. For 2013-14 and later years, tax losses can be carried back and offset against tax paid up to two years earlier. Companies will be able to carry back $1 million of losses each year. This will provide a cash benefit of up to $300 000 a year. Ninety per cent of the companies to benefit from this new tax measure are expected to be small businesses.

Many stakeholders in the food processing industry identify state payroll taxes as being a major disincentive to investment and to employing more people, and to be of most burden to labour-intensive industries and businesses.138 Unlike a reduction in the company tax rate, abolishing payroll tax would benefit employers based on turnover rather than whether their business is incorporated or not.

Similarly, in its Budget 2012-13 Submission to Treasury, the Australian Food and Grocery Council called on Government to introduce accelerated depreciation provisions for food and grocery manufacturers to purchase new plant and equipment to improve productivity and energy efficiency and also encourage investment during this period of high exchange rates. The Industry members of the Strategy Group support this request.

A previous reduction in the company tax rate to 30 per cent (effective from 2001-02) was largely funded by the removal of accelerated depreciation and basing depreciation write-offs on assets' effective lives. The effective life reviews follow the recommendations of the Ralph Review of business taxation to ensure that the revised depreciation schedule is as accurate, representative and comprehensive as possible.

The Prime Minister has made a cut in the company tax rate a key priority for Labor coming out of a Federal Government economic forum on Wednesday 13 June 2012.139 The Strategy Group supports this commitment.

3.5 Other Overhead Activities

3.5.1 Marketing

Advertising expenditure for the supermarket sector in the period Oct 2010-Sept 2011 was $204.2 million, a growth of 3 per cent over the previous financial year, despite tightening consumer spending.140 Market exposure to private label (retailer brands) and price discounting campaigns have served to improve the consumer mindset of perceived private label quality against branded products. Food retailer Coles, in an attempt to develop product differentiation themes, has introduced 'No Added Hormones Beef' to its product range in January 2011.

This tactical marketing shift has been influenced by the consumer pursuit for balance, health and wellbeing, with increased prominence for the organics sector. Marketing campaigns have increased focus on produce that is natural, chemical-free, free from additives, preservatives and environmentally sustainable.

For 39 of 92 respondents in the 2011 PwC survey, marketing was their focus area for innovation improvements. In addition, marketing was listed along with sales, innovation and processes as being critical areas to improve business competitiveness.

Large businesses tend to conduct market research and consumer insights to a greater extent than small businesses which tend to lack the capacity or capability. Those that do it well, manage to reduce the number of new product failures. Respondents in the 2011 PwC survey were asked if their businesses undertook market research or consumer insight surveys as a means to improve competitiveness. Of the 92 respondents, 52 businesses had undertaken some form of market research, 39 had not and one was unsure.

As mentioned earlier in the chapter, the respondents to the PwC survey stated lack of market demand as the biggest inhibitor of sales. When asked what proportion of costs their business spent on areas incorporating HR, finance and marketing activities, three businesses spent 0 per cent, 33 businesses allocated 1-5 per cent of costs, 24 businesses allocated 6-10 per cent of costs, five business allocated 11-20 per cent of costs, four businesses spent greater than 20 per cent of costs, 9 businesses were unsure and 10 businesses had no comment.

3.5.2 Advertising

Food category advertising in the 12 months to September 2011 slumped 14 per cent by share of estimated main media spend.141 Of the 10 sectors with the highest advertising spends; only two, snack food and meat, recorded growth. Advertising by the supermarket and retail sector had moderate growth with supermarkets accounting for $204.2 million, representing 86 per cent of all media spending.142 Research suggests that supermarket catalogues influence consumer spending habits (only 15 per cent are disposed by consumers) and have the potential to lift incremental sales by approximately 20-25 per cent.143 Research has also found that Australian consumers equate advertising with unnecessary price premiums. Sixty-seven per cent of consumers are under the belief that well-known brands are more expensive than private labels due to the extra spend on advertising.144 The overall retail advertising sector remained overall in good shape in 2011 finishing just down year-on-year.145

From a regulatory standpoint, food advertising has been subject to much public debate given the trends in childhood obesity. Under the Broadcasting Services Act and Code of Practice (section 123), advertisements directed to children for food and beverages should not encourage or promote an inactive lifestyle; should not encourage or promote unhealthy eating or drinking habits; and must not contain any misleading or incorrect information about the nutritional value of the product (clause 6.23).

While food advertising to children will not be discussed in this report in great detail, it is estimated that restricting unhealthy food advertising between the hours of 7:00am and 8:30pm could reduce children's exposure to unhealthy food advertising by as much as 79.2 per cent.146

Confectionary company Cadbury has found a novel way to educate the consumer and encourage them to 'enjoy treats as part of a balanced lifestyle.' The 'Be Treatwise' logo and campaign on Cadbury packaging encourages consumers to 'get to know the percentage Daily Intakes (% DIs) to assist in decision making for a balanced diet.147

Food companies advertising products which are considered as 'healthy alternatives' have embraced the use of well-known logos such as the National Heart Foundation 'Tick of Approval' or the Glycemic Index Foundation 'GI symbol program' in their marketing campaigns. Print and media advertisements for these products often focus on healthy lifestyle pursuits and include some form of celebrity endorsement.

3.5.3 Branding

Branding normally through trade-marking is a valuable asset for business to drive demand, secure business partners and reassure financial markets. In the 2012 Brand Finance report which analysed Australia's top 30 most valuable brands, supermarket retailer Woolworths maintained the rank of Australia's most valuable brand - a position held since 2009148. On the flipside, the supermarket retailer also experienced the biggest absolute loss of brand value - $504m in the 2011-12 financial year. This shift resulted largely from improvements made by competitor Coles in terms of price, efficiency, in-store design and marketing communications. In the 2011-12 financial year Coles improved its absolute value by 14 per cent and was ranked as the third-most valuable brand in Australia.

For the grocery food sector, branding is seen as a process of creating distinctive and durable perceptions in the mind of consumers. Strong brands, such as Kraft, Cadbury, Kellogg's, Uncle Tobys, Sanitarium, Coca-Cola and Arnotts, for example, build customer loyalty by marketing an image of quality - i.e. positive product associations based on past user experience (reliability). Clever and effective use of advertising coupled with strong product names/images/logos can not only increase market awareness but also increase market share. According to the 2011 Nielson Special Report which examined Marketing to Grocery Buyers; combining the power and mass reach of TV and food magazines is a potent mix for fast-moving consumer good (FMCG) brands to target the grocery buyer. The top 20 advertisers in the FMCG space each spent between $4-$12 million on media in the first half of 2011.149 Of these advertisers, the top three (Kellogg's (Aust), Nestlé Australia (Ltd) and Unilever Australia) spent a combined total of $29.9m.

The Australian Brand

Retailers and associations including the AFGC have launched marketing campaigns to strengthen and support the purchase of 'Australian Made' products. In the 2011 PwC survey, food processing industry respondents noted that using the 'Australian made, Australian grown' label provided them with a greater competitive edge, yet they were often restricted in using these products due to the cost of Australian raw materials.

The Australian Made logo certification trade mark was created by the Australian Government in 1986 to promote Australian-made products in local and export markets and to assist consumers identify Australian products. It is estimated that 94 per cent of Australian consumers recognise the triangular logo encasing a stylised kangaroo.150 The Australian Made, Australian Grown (AMAG) logo (managed now by the Australian Made Campaign Limited (AMCL)), when included on product packaging, may be used with claims of Australian origin, including: 'Product of Australia,' and 'Australian Made.'

The 212 food businesses which use the AMAG logo range in size from big retailers to tiny niche manufacturers or growers. The minimum licence fee is $300 (plus GST), based on a turnover of goods less than $300 000 per annum. For sales of eligible goods up to $20 million the fee is 0.1 per cent of sales, up to $45 million is 0.02 per cent of sales and over $45 million is a flat fee of $25 000 plus GST. The AMAG logo is one of several marketed as a means of promoting products of Australian origin.

According to an August 2009 report by Roy Morgan Research commissioned by Australian Made Campaign Limited:

  • Eighty-one per cent of Australians would prefer to buy Australian-made or grown food and beverages, which is greater than other types of products (range from 44 to 60 per cent)
  • The likelihood of consumers buying Australian-made or grown products increases with their age. More than 50 per cent of consumers over the age of 35 are conscious of purchasing Australian-made or grown products
  • It becomes more difficult for people over the age of 50 to identify that a particular product is Australian made or grown.
  • Eighty-five per cent of consumers have confidence that the Australian Made logo to is an accurate indicator of origin compared, to 50 per cent of consumers for the terms 'made in Australia' or 'Australian-made' appearing without the logo
  • A lower price can persuade 53 per cent of consumers to buy imports over Australian-made products, although this influence tends to decrease with age, especially once a consumer is over 50
Product of Australia

'Product of Country' is a premium claim defined in the Australian Consumer Law [part of the Competition and Consumer Act 2010 (CCA)] and adopted by the Food Standards Code. To qualify for the protection afforded to a 'Product of Australia' claim, and each significant ingredient must be derived from Australia and all or virtually all of the production or manufacturing processes must be carried out in Australia.151

Made in Australia

For goods labelled as 'Made in Australia', as defined in the CCA, the product must have been substantially transformed in Australia and 50 per cent or more of the cost of producing or manufacturing the goods must take place in Australia.152 While the latter part of that requirement is measurable, the former is less clear. ACCC guidelines only provide two extreme examples - one that reconstituting fruit juice does not involve substantial transformation of the fruit juice and another producing a cake from a variety of ingredients does involve the substantial transformation of those ingredients. This information is not necessarily sufficient for many manufacturers to make a claim with confidence.

Many Australian food producers and their customers are confused about the current rules for making country of origin claims, particularly in relation to the representation 'Made in (country)', and the interpretation of 'substantial transformation'. More guidance is needed on what is substantial transformation. For example, the definition of substantial transformation under the AMAG logo appears better articulated and arguably more restrictive than under the ACCC guidelines. For instance, the AMCL states that canning, preserving, blending, marinating and coating are not considered to be substantial transformation.

While the food industry believes more must be done to educate consumers on Country of Origin Labelling (CoOL) claims, the first step should be to determine an appropriate definition of substantial transformation for food products that ensures 'Made in (country)' claims in relation to food are not misleading to the average Australian consumer.

In response to recommendation 42 of the Labelling Logic: Review of Food Labelling Law and Policy report, and, in recognition of the confusion with terminology, the Council of Australian Governments' Legislative and Governance Forum on Food Regulation will request relevant consumer protection agencies to review existing CoOL materials (including publications, guidelines and other educational material) in a consultative process and, if appropriate, develop an education campaign with the specific objective of clarifying CoOL.

There are claims that Australia's country of origin labelling framework allows food imported into New Zealand to be repacked and labelled "product of" or "made in" New Zealand before being exported to Australia. 153 This claim is based on the exemption of New Zealand imports from the country of origin labelling provisions of the Food Standards Code and the Competition and Consumer Act 1910 (CCA) due to the operation of the Trans-Tasman Mutual Recognition Act 1997 (TTMRA). This has led to calls for tougher country of origin labelling laws.

Under Australia's current country of origin labelling framework, food could not be imported into or sold in Australia if it is labelled "product of" or made in" New Zealand unless such a statements are true and not misleading. The TTMRA exempts New Zealand imports from the application of the Food Standards Code and the CCA only if they could legally be sold in New Zealand. New Zealand's laws do not allow the sale of goods with false or misleading country of origin representations. Representations that food imported into and merely repackaged in New Zealand is "product of" or "made in" New Zealand would be considered false or misleading. Therefore, such goods could not be lawfully sold in New Zealand, and would not be exempt from the Food Standards Code or the CCA if imported into Australia.

Also, all goods imported into Australia, including from New Zealand, must comply with the Commerce Trade Descriptions Act 1905 (CTDA), which is specifically excluded from the operation of the TTMRA. Regulations made for the purposes of the CTDA require all food and beverage articles imported into Australia (including from New Zealand) to be marked with the country in which they were made or produced, and the CTDA prohibits the importation of goods bearing a false trade description.

Further regulation

In April 2012, a proposal to extend mandatory Country of Origin Labelling (CoOL) to unpackaged beef, sheep and chicken meats was approved by FSANZ.154 Subsequently, in June 2012, the Legislative and Governance Forum on Food Regulation (FoFR) requested FSANZ to review this proposal by 20 September 2012. Finalisation of this proposed amendment to the Food Standards Code is subject to this review and FoFR's agreement.

While CoOL can be shown to be necessary to address fully articulated 'market failure', reservations have been placed on mandatory origin labelling for 'consumer value' reasons. Consideration must be given to any potential costs to industry resulting from regulatory burden. That being said, there is value in industry-initiated self-regulatory intervention to allow for a more structured approach to consumer value issues.

3.6 The Retail Sector

3.6.1 Structure

Australia's food retail supermarket landscape consists of two major players, Woolworths Ltd and Westfarmers Ltd (Coles) who account for 71 per cent market share.155 The remaining 29 per cent is split between Independent Grocers of Australia (IGA) (7 per cent), Aldi (4 per cent), Australian United Retailers (trading as Foodworks) (2.3 per cent), Franklins (<1 per cent) and SPAR (<1 per cent).156

Coles and Woolworths are not only large by Australian standards but are reaching world scale in their operations, ranked 21 and 18 respectively in Deloitte's 2012 Global Powers of Retailing Report.157

Wholesale food distribution within the Australian market is dominated by Metcash Limited which specialises in divisions of grocery and fresh produce (IGA Distribution), liquor (Australian Liquor Marketers), hardware (Mitre 10) and other fast-moving consumer goods (Campbells Wholesale). All divisions are fully owned by Metcash with the exception of Mitre 10, of which it owns 50.1 per cent.158 Metcash also has control of the Franklins supermarket chain resulting from its July 2010 acquisition of 85 supermarket (77 corporate, eight franchised) stores.159

Figure 3.14: Australian Supermarket Estimated Market Share, 2011

Source: IBISWorld 2011, Supermarkets and Other Grocery Stores in Australia- December 2011
Text description for Australian Supermarket Estimated Market Share, 2011

In a report titled Exploring Treasure Island (2012), UBS Securities Australia Ltd states that the collective market share of the top three retailers in the Australian retail market is significantly greater than of the top three retailers in the United States of America. In addition, the report suggests that the market concentration in Australia presents pressure on stakeholders, diminishing the bargaining power of suppliers, logistics, and landlords (Figure 3.15).

Figure 3.15: Retail Market Concentration

Source: UBS Securities Australia Ltd, 2012, Exploring Treasure Island - Mach II, p.8
Text description for Retail Market Concentration

After New Zealand, Australia has the highest concentration of retail grocery power in the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development.160 This has an impact on some manufacturers' margins and their R&D spend and ambitions (see 3.6.3). 'Buyer power may suppress investment by suppliers in process and product innovation as well as in maintenance and upkeep if it reduces suppliers' expected returns from such investment. Consumers are harmed by a lower rate of innovation and product quality. If the exercise of buyer power results in fewer new products coming to market, a reduced variety of products and/or a reduction in product quality, consumer welfare could be harmed. This is likely to hinge on the existing profitability of suppliers: the more profitable they are, the less likely that such effects will materialise. However, if suppliers are currently struggling to earn sufficient profits to permit them to make the necessary investments or even stay in business, then increased buyer power could have these detrimental welfare effects.'161

The Australian food and grocery retail sector appears to correspond most closely to an oligopsonistic economic model, i.e. a small number of buyers and many sellers.162 Coles, Woolworths, and at a wholesale level, Metcash, have substantial market power, ostensibly as the consequence of organic growth and merger and acquisitions over time. Oligopsonistic market conditions are a form of imperfect competition, where the buyer has some degree of power (depending on the elasticity of supply and the number of buyers in the market) in setting prices below the price that would prevail in a perfectly competitive market. The extent to which retailers are able to leverage their buyer power in negotiations regarding pricing and trading terms depends largely on the alternative options available to the supplier to sell its products as well as the strength of the supplier's brand in terms of providing countervailing bargaining power in negotiations. Typically, few suppliers of processed foods have alternative options available as the grocery distribution channel is the major route to consumers, and few suppliers have strong countervailing bargaining power across their entire product range. As a result, the major grocery retailers are able to purchase products - and other services across the supply chain, such as transport and packaging - on better trading terms than other buyers.

A recent report by the British Institute of International and Comparative Law has confirmed that the issue of unfair commercial practices and unequal bargaining power between retailers and suppliers in the food supply chain is an area of concern for a large number of Member States in the EU.163 'At present, no Member State employs a single method of enforcement that is fully effective. This is, to a large extent, because the majority of mechanisms are based on competition law, but also because it is difficult to create a model that adequately takes into account the unique nature of the food retailer/supplier relationship'.164

The researchers concluded that a mix of hard law and soft law solutions may be the most effective. The concept of unfair practices in business-to-business trading relations could be achieved by converting the standards or code into a statutory requirement and/or applying competition law in a way that creates precedents for behaviour by retailers on which suppliers are dependent. 'This might include pursuing dominance cases, where unfair practices are imposed by a retailer which has market power over suppliers through, for example, controlling access to its shelves which in turn are the only conduit to a cohort of consumers who cannot be accessed by suppliers in any other way'165. In addition, the report suggests structural remedies could extend to restricting vertical integration of wholesaling by retailers, when they adversely affect the ability of suppliers to reach a range of retailers and the ability of smaller retailers to obtain grocery products at competitive prices from a range of competing wholesalers.

On 21 June, 2012 the Norwegian Government, through its ministers responsible for consumer affairs, competition, food and agriculture, announced that it will establish a committee of legal experts to elaborate a legal solution to securing good business practices by companies, as well as consumer interests, in the food and groceries market. Consumer interests are to be given a broad definition, comprising price, range of choice, quality and availability. Enforcement of the law will be considered as an integral part of the work. The precise mandate and composition of the legal committee is yet to be established but work will commence in the autumn of 2012.

Some Strategy Group members noted that there is an upside to fewer retailers. Distribution affects profits and competitiveness since it can contribute up to 50 per cent of the final selling price of some goods. This affects cost competitiveness as well as profits since margins are squeezed by distribution costs. Some producers prefer fewer distribution channels as it makes sales and marketing easier; however, none of the businesses in the PwC industry survey said this was an advantage.

Planning and zoning

The regulation of planning and zoning is primarily the responsibility of the States and Territories.

The 2008 Report of the ACCC inquiry into the competitiveness of retail prices for standard groceries (ACCC Grocery Inquiry Report) and the Productivity Commission's 2008 Market for Retail Tenancy Leases in Australia inquiry report each made similar recommendations on planning and zoning laws restricting competition in the supply and use of retail space. The key finding of the ACCC's Grocery Inquiry Report was that while grocery retailing is 'workably competitive', there are a number of factors that limit the level of price competition, including 'the complexities of planning applications...[which] provide the opportunity for Coles and Woolworths to "game" the planning system to delay or prevent potential competitors entering local areas'.

The ACCC considered that the appropriate policy response was to attempt to lower barriers to entry and expansion, in both retailing and wholesaling, of independent supermarkets and potential new entrants. On 5 August 2008 the Government released its Preliminary Action Plan in response to the ACCC Grocery Inquiry Report and referred the potentially anti-competitive impacts of state and local planning and zoning laws to COAG.

On 29 November 2008 COAG agreed to examine further planning and zoning from a competition perspective as recommended in the ACCC Grocery Inquiry Report. At the COAG meeting of 7 December 2009 it was agreed that planning laws should not unjustifiably restrict competition in grocery retailing. It was also agreed that the Productivity Commission would report on the States' and Territories' planning and zoning systems to identify practices that support or unjustifiably restrict competition.

In April 2010 the Government requested that the Productivity Commission examine and report on the states' and territories' planning and zoning systems, particularly as they impact on business compliance costs; competition; and the overall efficiency and effectiveness of the functioning of cities. The Commission was also asked to report on best-practice approaches that support competition.

On 16 May 2011 the Productivity Commission's final report, Performance Benchmarking of Australian Business Regulation: Planning, Zoning and Development Assessments, was released. The report found that some restrictions on competition may be required to improve the overall efficiency of urban land allocation and use. However, it identified a number of constraints imposed by planning and zoning systems that unjustifiably restrict entry into markets and reduce the flexibility of businesses operating in a particular zone or centre.

The Productivity Commission identified a range of processes from various jurisdictions which it considers to be leading practice, and which it advocated that states and territories should implement. The Productivity Commission reiterated many of the abovementioned recommendations in relation to planning and zoning in its November 2011 inquiry report into the Economic Structure and Performance of the Australian Retail Industry.

In its December 2011 response to the report, the Government encouraged State and Territory governments to carefully consider and implement where appropriate the findings of the Commission's report and its previous benchmarking report; particularly, the scope to give greater weight to economic considerations in planning decisions to ensure that competition is not restricted unless it can be demonstrated that the benefits of the restriction to the community as a whole outweigh the costs, and the objectives can only be achieved by restricting competition. The Government also announced that the newly-established Retail Council of Australia would consider the issue.

3.6.2 Rise of the private label

The rise of the 'value/price-conscious shopper' and the profit proposition for retailers and a better return to shareholders has largely influenced the growth and popularity of private label products (also referred to as own label, retailer brands, store brands, house brands, generics or distributor-owned brands), which account for 25 per cent of the national grocery market. Private label goods are forecast to have solid growth over the next decade rising to an estimated share of 40 per cent by 2020.166

Figure 3.16: National Grocery Market Product Segmentation, 2011-12

Source: IBISWorld 2011, Supermarkets and Other Grocery Stores in Australia- December 2011
Text description for National Grocery Market Product Segmentation, 2011-12

While Australian private label growth lags behind European (53 per cent) and North American (35 per cent)167 markets, an increasing proportion (an estimated 57 per cent) of branded supplier's contract-manufacture for retailers. For the Private Label Manufacturers Association, private labels represent the choice and opportunity to regularly purchase quality food products at savings compared to manufacturer brands.168

Private label products have been in Australia for many decades. The catalyst for their growth was the deregulation of the dairy industry. The growth and increased popularity of private labels products in the Australian market was further assisted by the entry of German retailer ALDI in 2001. Through its offering of unknown international home-brand labels, ALDI became a viable alternative to consumers seeking value for money and cost savings in their weekly shopping. Over time, the major retailers have developed more private label merchandise to compete with this changing consumer trend, although the lack of availability of sourcing options has meant the growth of this sector has not been as rapid as has been experienced in some other countries.

In November 2011, Woolworths released its five-year plan to double its customer take up of own brand products. Tjeerd Jegen, Managing Director of Australian Woolworths supermarkets noted, 'our customers have given us mandate... to expand our range, lower price, further improve quality and strengthen brand presence'. Woolworths currently has three private label tiers- 850 products under 'Homebrand,' 1000 products under 'Select' and 400 lines under 'Macro' targeted at health and wellbeing.169 Coles has five private label tiers, 'Coles Smart Buy,' 'Coles,' 'Coles Finest', 'Coles Organic' and 'Coles Green Choice.'

Several well-known companies are contracted directly by the retail market to make private label products. These include (but are not limited to) Goodman Fielder, Manildra and George Weston.

For lower involvement commodity areas, where consumer decision-making processes are relatively simple and straightforward, such as fresh milk, private labels have a greater tendency to gain significant market share.

The attitude of the Australian consumer is changing. In the two-year period following the GFC, the consumer mindset has shifted to a greater willingness to try private labels (23 per cent) in categories not previously purchased. While consumers are willing to switch brands to save money, perceived value (a subjective trait among consumers) and improvements in perceived quality are also taken into consideration. For the most part, consumer sentiment suggests private labels (in some category areas) are the same if not better than their branded counterparts.170

Consumer demand for supermarket and grocery items, while driven largely by price, is also influenced by the level of real household disposable income, consumer sentiment, product availability, population levels and the economic environment. Penetration of private labels is also highly category sensitive. For high-value categories where branded manufacturers have established brand equity, there is greater reinforcement of premium positioning through investment and promotional activities. As such, movement in these categories has been relatively moderate as the perceived risk to switch to private labels is deemed higher, or loyalty to well-loved brands prevails.

International markets indicate a strong correlation between market concentration and private label penetration. 'In the short term, the growth of private labels may benefit consumers through lower prices for certain products. However, in the long term, Australians may be faced with less choice as the supermarket giants increase in-house offerings'.171

3.6.3 Repercussions

'Private labels influence both the competition within food supply chains and the range of food products that are available to consumers. Private labels increase the range of available products and thus increase inter-brand (price) competition'.172

The growth of retailer or private label products is in direct competition to branded products. Retailers are in effect vertically integrating by contract manufacturing. This means they have some control over the whole chain and can in effect crowd out any competitors more effectively. The growth of private label products has a number of implications for upstream suppliers (manufacturers and processors), as well for consumers.

Lowering prices and increasing competition

Expansion of the private label range of the two big retailers is putting downward price pressure on suppliers, and if suppliers can not deliver at a lower cost, retailers may source products from other suppliers and overseas manufacturers to meet their needs. In perfectly competitive markets this leads to improved efficiencies along the value chain and lower prices for the consumer.

'Cannibalising' of brands can occur when the private label and the branded product are produced by the same manufacturer (retailers demand narrower margins on the private label - prices on shelves are lowered). While contract manufacturing of private labels can provide some certainty in the short-term through increases in production volumes, these manufacturers run the risk of effectively competing with themselves, potentially cannibalising their own margins, market share and reducing their long-term competitiveness.

Innovations

Branded suppliers note pressure to share new product development ideas 12 months from launch, often in competing product categories.173

Retailers may use innovation associated with branded product manufacturers even when the latter does not produce a similar private label product for the retailers. This may be done under coercion - the manufacturer fearing delisting. This practice confers no obligation on the part of the supermarkets to invest in their own product R&D and, because of the pressure placed on supplier margins by retailers, it diminishes the ability of the suppliers to continue to invest in R&D, particularly capital-intensive R&D.

Under blind tasting, it has been shown that private labels have the potential to be mistaken for national brands. In particular, three in four taste testers were unable to differentiate between Coles-branded chocolate biscuits and the market leader, Arnott's Tim Tams174.

Ultimately this may prove to be an unsustainable situation for investment and innovation in the Australian processed food sector.

Pricing strategy and shelf-space control

Retailers control the selling interface to the consumer. There is the potential for supermarkets to engage in anti-competitive behaviour when pursuing a private label strategy. Common complaints raised about private label strategies include: retailers consistently setting the retail price of branded products above private label products and private label products being provided with privileged position on shelves compared to branded products , e.g. so that the latter can't be found as easily.

Rod Sims, head of the ACCC, warned the supermarkets in April 2012175 that they risk prosecution if they are misusing their market power to favour their private labels to disadvantage other grocery brands.

Copycat branding

While private labels are legitimate offerings, there have been examples where retailers have produced packaging that looks very similar to established brands (e.g. colours, size and style). Manufacturers may be reluctant to take legal action with respect to passing off to remedy this situation if they fear retribution.

Retailers are being accused of using 'copycat' packaging to mislead consumers into buying their private label products. According to the AFGC's former Chief Executive, Kate Carnell, supermarkets are targeting brands with a strong market share and copying their designs as closely as possible without infringing trademark laws.176

Reduction in choice

Some industry commentators have suggested that the retail giants' strategy is to have a private label, the other market leader and perhaps one other across each of their product lines.

In most developed countries, 'the rise of private labels has put a squeeze on manufacturer's brands. But the impact has been asymmetric. It is the weaker manufacturer's brands - those that do not occupy number-one or number-two market positions - that have borne the brunt of the negative impact. The assortment strategy at many retailers with active private label programs is to accept only the top two or three manufacturer brands. They delist the rest of the weaker brands, replacing them with their own private labels. Even if the weaker brands are able to get on retailer shelves, the manufacturers have to pay disproportionate amounts in terms of retailer margins and support to persuade the retailers to stock them.'177

Certainly SMEs struggle to attain access to market when trading with large retailers. Under these conditions access to market for SMEs through the large retailers is likely to prove increasingly difficult, especially if the major retailers continue to increase their market share.

It is claimed that it is difficult for SMEs to get access to customers as the two big retailers are limiting variety on their shelves. For example, in the period 2008-10 Coles' and Woolworths' share of sales by volume of their private labels in the retail grocery sector grew from 76 to 92 per cent for tomato paste and from 82 to 84 per cent for frozen fish.178

Industry participation has experienced a considerable decrease in the number of small-scale operations, which are being steadily replaced by larger businesses. The number of business enterprises has declined over the last five years as have the number of business establishments.179

Australian food manufacturers need to become more innovative to protect their brands and look for alternative customers (either domestically or overseas). Increases in market share are forecast for companies which increase their online sales presence. It is estimated that within two years 33 per cent of large companies in the food and beverage industry expect to sell online; accounting on average, for 16 per cent of total sales.180 This will reduce the buyer power of the major retailers.

3.7 Future Consumer Demands and Trends

The food industry is in a state of transition. Changes to consumer demand mean opportunities exist for companies to increase revenue growth if they can actively engage with market trends. Food and beverage companies (domestic and international) were asked to rate consumer trends which offer the greatest opportunity for growth in Managing through uncertainty: Food and beverage industry in transition (the Grant Thornton International Business Report 2011). Of these trends, health and well-being, in-home cooking, and natural and/or organic were seen to provide the best avenues for strategic direction.

Healthy eating, a critical consumer driver, will have considerable influence over company strategies within the next few years. Mintel Research describes the rise of 'healthy snackers', those who choose to consume snacks that are healthier than others, while reducing consumption of less-healthy nibbles.181 While consumers look for 'healthy' options, more often than not they are confused by a lack of transparency and definitional guidelines. For example, organic products are those 'not enhanced', while functional foods usually signify 'enhanced'.182

Trend cycles emerge more rapidly as a result of technological advancements, accelerated social change, instantaneous communication and consumer acceptance (or inability to escape) of new ideas and concepts. It is worth noting that unless a product innovation gains consumer acceptance, it will be overlooked irrespective of how unique or 'on-trend' it is. According to market research provider DataMonitor, there are eight megatrends influencing the modern consumer marketplace (see Figure 3.17).

Megatrends capture the bigger global market picture reflecting consumer values, attitudes and societal change. Megatrends can exist as counter-trends, can crossover or can be inter-dependent. This reflects the inherent complexity of consumer behaviour. Yet with this complexity there is potential for market opportunities. Pressures from input factors of raw material availability (the greatest cost component within the sector), people and skills (the high proportion of low skilled workers and the reduction in high skilled employees), packaging (an industry in constant flux) and freight (high cost coupled with lack of transportation mode alternatives) will no doubt continue to influence the productivity of the sector.

Figure 3.17: Global Consumer Megatrends

Source: Datamonitor 2012, Trendsights: Tomorrow's Consumer Brand Landscape
Text description for Global Consumer Megatrends

When respondents to the 2011 PwC survey were asked to identify what they thought drove consumer purchasing decisions, 67 businesses felt that consumers primarily wanted lower prices. Sixty businesses said consumers wanted new products, 58 businesses said consumers wanted consistent quality and 50 businesses said consumers wanted better nutritional value products. A qualitative observation drawn from these results is that the trend towards lower prices was more apparent in the seafood, dairy and grain cereal industries.

However, while Investment opportunities can be difficult for SMEs to access due to financial constraints, all companies (SMEs, larger businesses, multi-nationals) understand the importance of R&D and have continued to invest if somewhat moderately in innovation. The operational requirements of the sector continue to grow, especially in new Asian markets, where population growth is being accompanied by rapidly increasing income levels. Investment into new capital equipment is trending upwards and companies are looking at new ways to improve their energy, water and reduce waste levels to transition into a low-carbon future. There are also opportunities for the food processing sector to utilise new technologies to address future predicted food shortages. Industry transformation strategies are discussed further in Chapter Five.

3.8 Summary

The current operating environment in Australia provides food processors with a range of opportunities and challenges. The most significant challenges are:

  • An uncompetitive cost base and shallow profit pool - Australian businesses cite the lack of availability of affordable raw materials as a major factor in reduced Australian competitiveness. Lately the high Australian dollar has meant that the cost of Australian raw materials can be considerably higher than in competitor countries and, as a result, it has been harder for suppliers to compete with imports.
  • A challenging regulatory environment - while Australia is held in high regard worldwide for its safe and clean food supply there are still a number of issues associated with the application of Australian food standards and related regulation including: differences in food safety regulations and enforcement between the States and Territories; the ability of New Zealand to choose to vary or opt out of some standards; and the costs and time delays in changes to the Food Standards Code
  • A hostile retail environment - the growth of 'retailer or private label' products in direct competition to branded products. Retailers are in effect vertically integrating by contract manufacturing. This means they have some control over the whole chain and can in effect crowd out more effectively any competitors. The growth of private label products has a number of implications for upstream suppliers - manufacturers and processors as well for consumers.
  • the Australian Meat Industry Training Package (comprised of various Meat Processing qualifications at the I, II, III, IV, Diploma and Advanced Diploma certificate levels and includes subject areas such as slaughtering and boning, meat retailing, and food services);
  • the Food Processing Training Package (comprised of various Food Processing and Retail Baking qualifications at the I, II, III, IV and Diploma certificate levels); and,
  • the Seafood Industry Training Package (comprised of various Seafood Industry qualifications at the I, II, III, IV and Diploma certificate levels and includes aquaculture, fishing operations, seafood processing and seafood sales and distribution). For a detailed list of all the food processing, meat and seafood industry VET courses available by state and training provider, please refer to Appendix D.

The ACCC in its 2008 inquiry into the retail sector preferred a bilateral bargaining power model (as opposed to a monopsony model) as the more appropriate analytical framework to analyse buyer power in the food and grocery retail industry. It based this on the belief that grocery supply markets are a series of bilateral relationships rather than wholesale markets, and trading terms are negotiated individually with each supplier. As a result, ACCC contends that any judgements on impact of buyer power on suppliers must be based on evidence from specific factual situations, and not on whole market.
