4. Opportunities and Threats

If Australia is to increase employment and investment in the food processing industry, it must innovate, grow and improve its sustainability, and exploit more effectively its significant natural and other advantages in the growing and processing of food. To win in the international market and to grow domestically, Australia's food processing industry needs to capitalise on its strengths, harness opportunities and mitigate its weaknesses. In addition, Australia must address a number of impediments to future sustainable growth including the key issue of access to markets, domestically and overseas, among others. This chapter identifies the strengths, weaknesses, threats and opportunities for the industry.

4.1 SWOT Results

The Strategy Group undertook an analysis of 'strengths, weaknesses, opportunities and threats' (a SWOT). The group noted that the food processing industry already has a good track record with some outstanding sectors, that Australia has strong natural advantages, is good at growing food, has an educated multicultural workforce and has a well-developed entrepreneurial spirit. In addition, Australia's public research facilities can facilitate innovation in the industry. Australia needs a strong framework to underpin sustainable development of our natural resources, exploit counter-seasonal opportunities and maximise export opportunities in Asia.

Australia's excellent food safety credentials, its disease-free status, strong regulatory regimes and innovations in traceability, quality assurance and supply chains, are considered by international analysts to be important factors in the food industry's future domestic and export success. The Strategy Group confirmed these conclusions in its SWOT. Individual items from the overall SWOT analysis from this survey are listed in Table 4.1 and Table 4.2.

Many of the items generated by the SWOT were supported by insights from the PwC industry survey of 92 food companies (see Figures 4.1 and 4.2).

Figure 4.1: Survey Results - Greatest Strengths according to PwC Survey


Text description for Survey Results - Greatest Strengths according to PwC Survey

Figure 4.2: Survey Results - Biggest Challenges according to PwC Survey


Text description for Survey Results - Biggest Challenges according to PwC Survey

4.2 Strengths

The Strategy Group looked at strengths and weakness of the sector, outlined at Table 4.1. Threats and opportunities are identified in the next part of this chapter.

Table 4.1: Industry - Strengths and Weakness

StrengthsWeaknesses
1. Reputation for product safety and high-quality including disease-free status 1. Small domestic market means manufacturing economies of scale are difficult to achieve, profit pool in Australia is shallow and shrinking.
2. Large, high-quality public research institutions 2. Labour costs are higher than in the developing countries
3. Available energy, raw materials and land (that crosses multiple latitudes) for large-scale food production. Counter-seasonality with northern hemisphere 3. Land and factories are expensive to set up
4. Educated innovative, motivated and multicultural workforce 4. Freight costs (at current scale) to reach market are high whether domestic or international
5. Economic fundamentals are sound and domestic market is growing (albeit slowly) 5. High levels of regulatory burden; inconsistent regulation, and/or poor design of regulation/programs
6. Stable and open political system including intellectual property enforcement 6. Competition for skilled/unskilled workers is high, especially in low unemployment periods
7. R&D Tax Incentive encourages innovation and investment 7. Declining numbers of food scientists and food technologists
  8. Vast majority of food companies are small

4.2.1 Reputation for product safety and high-quality, including disease-free status

Australia has an international reputation for clean, green, safe and high-quality foods and that includes high-value-added products that are convenient, nutritious and targeted to consumer needs. Australia has responded to concerns about food safety which have emerged over the last decade as a result of a number of international crises by developing a comprehensive regulatory system covering domestic and international food trade.

The Asian market is increasingly looking for high-quality, safe and clean overseas branded products to purchase, which creates enormous opportunities for high-quality Australian exports. However, Asia is not a monoculture and strategies need to take this into consideration.

Two-thirds of the businesses participating in the PwC industry survey claimed that using the label 'Australian made, Australian grown' provided them with a competitive edge, particularly within the seafood, fruit/veg, and grain/cereal industries.

There have been a number of previous government supported campaigns to capitalise on Asian markets, such as Supermarket to Asia, and the current Australian Government initiative, Australia in the Asian Century, which is focused on identifying and exploiting opportunities associated with a growing middle-class in a number of Asian countries.

4.2.2 Large, high-quality public research institutions

Australia has high-quality researchers, research institutes and universities. Australia has a higher than the world average of citations in the research fields of geosciences, physics, plant and animal sciences, and agricultural sciences.183

We have significant R&D capacity and capability in food products in Australia including at the CSIRO, Rural Research and Development Corporations (RDCs), the Australian Wine Research Institute (AWRI), Cooperative Research Centres (CRCs) and through Australian Research Council (ARC) grants.

Apart from the CSIRO, none of the listed organisations specifically funds R&D in food processing. The focus of all RDCs, and all current relevant CRCs are pre-'farm gate'. This has the effect of distorting the R&D environment with strong public investment on farm, but very little in the post- 'farm gate' sector, which generates most of the final consumer value.

In the 2011 PwC survey, out of 92 respondents, 44 businesses had partnered with an organisation for research purposes within the last five years. Governments and universities were the most popular organisations to provide partners (27 businesses). Satisfaction with the outcome was relatively high overall (67 businesses) and 77 businesses felt they had an influence on the direction of the research undertaken. However, results from the recent Victorian survey on the need for pilot plant facilities suggested that many Victorian food processors were not engaging very much with public research organisations, but when they did, satisfaction levels were not high.184

New technologies and insights represent a major opportunity to build greater knowledge among consumers about the value of food in providing for short-term wellness and long-term health. According to the CSIRO, only a small percentage of Australia's thousands of food companies have the time and resources to take advantage of the new technologies crucial to advancing their business operations. For most small to medium enterprises, technical innovation can provide a unique and sustainable competitive advantage. Increasingly, it is seen as an investment in future market leadership as the capacity to manage technology uptake drives company growth.

Developing improved capability to take up new technology is vital to the survival of the food industry in Australia. The CSIRO is already tackling these issues on a number of fronts; for example, a process developed by Victorian-based Roma Foods, in collaboration with CSIRO, to make rice and maize gluten-free pasta spirals.185 But more can be done. Project partnerships with rural research and development corporations, universities and other research bodies can link outcomes championed by industry bodies and agri-food companies.186

4.2.3 Available energy, raw materials and land for large-scale productive farming

Food production is inextricably connected with resource allocation, land use, trade, employment, and energy and water usage. While Australia has a good environment for the production of raw materials, concerns have been expressed about the urban encroachment, mining and the use of crops for biofuels as issues to watch if this capacity is not to be diminished.187

Climatic differences within the Australian continent, from the tropical north to the cooler south, contribute to the extensive range of produce grown in the country. With its southern hemisphere location, Australia offers counter-seasonal produce to northern hemisphere produce seasons.188

'Productivity of Australian farms, measuring the efficiency of using inputs to produce a specific level of outputs, has risen strongly for cropping specialists and the mixed crop-livestock industry - averaging 2.1 per cent and 1.5 per cent a year respectively from 1977-78 to 2006-07. Beef specialists achieved the same average performance level as the mixed crop livestock industry over the past three decades. Their productivity growth coincided with high output growth and relatively marginal growth in input use.

'The sheep industry continues to lag behind the broad-acre sector in terms of long-term productivity growth. Between 1977-78 and 2006-07, the industry has experienced a decline in both output and input use. However, the Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry (DAFF) warns that broad-acre productivity has slowed since the turn of the century, after surging in the 1990s. This is partly attributable to droughts in 2002-03 and 2006-07.'189

Chapter Three discusses issues related to costs of inputs and the competitiveness of Australia with respect to raw material inputs. Historically high, recent increases in the Australian dollar have reduced that competitiveness.

A variety of energy sources are available in Australia to large-scale farming and manufacturing. These sources include gas, coal and renewable energies. Fifty-one per cent of businesses participating in the PwC industry survey said that their energy costs contributed 1 to 5 per cent of their overall costs. Sixty-one per cent of businesses said that water costs were between 1 and 5 per cent. According to the World Bank,190 Australia is placed 37th in the ranking of 183 economies on the ease of getting electricity and historically our electricity is low-cost by world standards.191 No doubt emissions pricing and adoption of low-emission technologies will cause comparatively small increases in the cost of electricity generation, but the more significant price pressures on electricity generation reside outside of the carbon price context, e.g. electricity infrastructure costs, and the declining availability of cheap alternatives to petroleum, the latter which may have reached its production peak.

There is an issue associated with the use of farmland for non-farming purposes such as mining. Conversion of farmland to mining has the potential to reduce agricultural production, whether the mining companies are Australian or foreign-owned. However, the investment in infrastructure, such as improved roads, that typically accompanies mining developments is likely to benefit agriculture.

'Australia's food security is likely to be further enhanced by ongoing foreign investment in agriculture. For the economy as a whole, the flow of foreign funds leads to higher aggregate production in the economy and thus to higher incomes, which improve consumers' capacity to purchase food'192.

4.2.4 Educated, innovative, motivated and multicultural workforce

Some 4.4 million people living in Australia, representing approximately a quarter of the country's population, were born overseas, with substantial representation from European, Asian and Middle Eastern countries.193 The 2006 Census of Population and Housing found that, in August 2006, 3.1 million people (16 per cent of the population) spoke a language other than English at home and that this cultural diversity within the population has resulted in over 200 different languages spoken in homes around the country.

Almost 1.2 million Australians are fluent in major European languages, apart from English, and nearly 1.5 million speak an Asian language at home (with more than 500 000 speaking a Chinese dialect).194 Mandarin is now the second-most common language spoken at home other than English, according to the 2011 Census, taking over the spot from Italian. Opportunities exist to capitalise on the richness of experience among Australian workers. Managers could increase productivity by greater use of employee insights and ideas, while understanding cultural sensitivities.

In addition, this diversity will continue to lead to a demand for a variety of foods. Many specialty products have European, Asian and Middle Eastern influences, and kosher, halal and organic foods have provided opportunities for food processing manufacturer particularly in the meat processing sector.

Australia's linguistic diversity, the proficiency with which the languages are spoken, and strong cultural links with Europe and Asia, provide an ideal mix for multinational organisations setting up or expanding their business in Australia.

The Australian workforce is also highly educated. In May 2009, of the 14.4 million people aged 15-64 years, 8 million (55 per cent) held at least one non-school qualification. These comprised 3.3 million whose highest non-school qualification was a Bachelor degree or above. A further 1.3 million reported an Advanced Diploma or Diploma, 2.3 million reported a Certificate III or IV and 0.7 million reported a Certificate I or II as their highest qualification.195 Australia is ranked sixth in the OECD education rankings, behind South Korea, Finland, Canada, New Zealand and Japan.

4.2.5 Economic fundamentals are sound and domestic market is growing (albeit slowly)

In a recent edition of its twice-yearly report about the global economy, the IMF noted that while economic recovery in major advanced economies will be "relatively sluggish", Australia and the newly industrialised Asian economies are off to a strong start and will likely stay in the lead.196

The IMF says Australia's economic growth is expected to be 3 per cent in 2012 and 3.5 per cent in 2013. According to the IMF, growth in Australia will be helped by strong demand for commodities, particularly from China. The report suggests that the economic outlook for Australia remains favorable despite the recent uncertainty on global financial markets. But risks remain. These include a stalling global recovery, and faltering Asian growth which would impact the demand for commodities.

According to the Heritage Foundation and the Wall Street Journal's Annual 2012 Index of Economic Freedom, Australia has an economic freedom score of 83.1, ranking third overall in the 2012 Index. Its overall score is 0.6 points higher than last year, reflecting better scores in trade freedom, government spending and fiscal freedom.

Australia, with a low population density and relatively low crime rates, continues to supply some of the world's most livable cities. In a ranking of 140 cities around the world, Melbourne was given a score of 97.5 per cent. Other Australian cities in the top 10 include Sydney, which ranked sixth-best - up from seventh in the previous survey, while Perth and Adelaide ranked unchanged with a joint eighth slot.197 Nearly 60 per cent of Australia's processed food businesses are located in Victoria and NSW.

Australia's modern and competitive economy benefits from the country's strong commitment to open-market policies that facilitate global trade and investment.

4.2.6 Stable and open political system including intellectual property enforcement

The foundations of economic freedom in Australia are strong and well supported by excellent protection of property rights and an independent judiciary that enforces anti-corruption measures effectively.

Australia is a safe destination for investment. The country's political and regulatory environment is stable, open and progressive providing investors with a high degree of confidence and certainty. Australia has been ranked equal first along with Canada, Norway and Switzerland out of 193 countries surveyed by credit ratings agency Dun & Bradstreet as a safe and secure destination for investment in its recently released report, Risk 2012 - The Economic and Risk Outlook.198

Australia's strength as an investment destination stems, in part, from a political system that has been assessed as being highly effective in responding to economic challenges and policy directions. According to the IMD 2011 World Competitiveness Rankings, Australia was ranked ninth out of 59 economies.199 The adaptability of Australian Government policy to changes in the economy, and the transparency and effectiveness of government were all rated highly.

Australia's stable political system and government ensures an open and progressive legal and regulatory environment, providing business with a high degree of certainty. Regulation of Australian financial markets is viewed internationally as a model of best practice - both responsible and prudent. This confidence has encouraged a steady flow of foreign investment, boosted by a strong and robust intellectual property framework and business-friendly policies.

4.2.7 New R&D Tax Incentive may encourage innovation and investment

Private sector investment in technology and product innovation is also crucial to success. Australia's expenditure on R&D as a per cent of GDP in 2008 was 2.24 per cent, 0.11 per cent below the OECD average of 2.35 per cent.200

According to a 2011 survey of Australian CEOs201 74 per cent of those in the food and beverage industry were investing more than 1 per cent of sales in new product development, and 11 per cent invested more than 5 per cent of their sales. Interestingly, the amount of product development expenditure did not vary in accordance with company size but did depend on the degree of senior management commitment.

In the 2011 PwC survey while most of the 92 businesses interviewed indicated the amount invested in innovation was relatively low (69 companies invested less than 5 per cent), the majority of businesses within the sector (84) claimed to invest in it to some degree. The survey indicated that industries more likely to invest in R&D (between 1 and 5 per cent) were dairy, fruit/vegetables, oil/fat, and grain/cereal. Businesses were more likely to spend money on R&D in order to gain a competitive edge - be that through new products or processes. A key unanswered question here is the extent to which the R&D spend by the industry is realising a full return on that investment.

The R&D Tax Concession was introduced in 1985 to encourage Australian industry to undertake more R&D activities. During the period from 26 November 2007 to 30 September 2010, 642 food-related companies registered for the R&D Tax Concession.

In the 2007 Deloitte study of the use of R&D in the Fast Moving Consumer Goods (FMCG) industry, a large component of which were food and beverage businesses, Deloitte found that 93 per cent of those surveyed had accessed the R&D Tax Concession.202

In the PwC industry survey only 48 companies of the 92 surveyed claimed to have accessed the R&D Tax Concession. Businesses within the meat, seafood, oil/fat industries were the most likely to have accessed it. This has now been replaced with the R&D Tax Incentive (see Chapter Three ) - the design of which should increase investment from multinationals and small businesses.

4.3 Weaknesses

4.3.1 Economies of scale

Australia's relatively small population and large geographical spread pose a significant impediment to business returns. The scale issue impacts everyone along the supply chain, from growers to packaging companies, freight companies and retail outlets.

With stable household expenditure on food (of around 16.45 per cent203) in Australia, growth of the food manufacturing sector is closely linked to domestic population growth and growth in overseas demand for Australian food products. Export from an island continent is comparatively expensive - sea or air are the only options (since air is costly, sea needs to be cheap and efficient).

The global food industry is dominated by large, multinational firms, and the Australian market reflects that trend. Almost half of the largest 50 firms are foreign-owned companies. Multinational corporations choose to invest where there is the greatest return on investment unless other strategic reasons prevail. Newly built plants overseas have huge scale and this makes them cost competitive. The strong Australian dollar adds to Australia's lack of competitiveness.

Australian manufacturers which are potentially able to achieve world scale (to be efficient, pass on cost savings, and to compete with imports that are being manufactured from world scale plants overseas) should, all other things being equal, be allowed to achieve that scale. Our small market size and costs to export markets (burdened by a sustained strong Australian dollar) mean that Australia has become a comparatively poor platform for manufacturing despite our natural resources and other advantages. Efficiencies can be attained with scale and scale needs significant capital investment.

Many businesses operate with surplus capacity. One major flour miller stated that the industry as a whole has surplus capacity of 46 per cent. Large millers compete for a declining per capita consumption through intense price competition. Smaller operators generally remain competitive because of a localised market in which larger operators cannot compete due to the cost of transporting flour. Small millers do not normally compete directly with the large companies because they have small niche local markets. However, one small miller indicated that the company lost 50 per cent of sales in 2000 due to a major competitor initiating price competition (although these sales have now recovered to 75 per cent of the previous level).

Similarly to flour mills, those oil processors which have a strong link with the farmers producing their canola are able to obtain an important competitive advantage. Ensuring the appropriate cultivar affects product quality (suitability for oil refinement) and quantity (oil content of seeds).

In Australia, processing plants crushing oilseeds are located close to oilseed-growing areas. Processing plants which refine vegetable oils are generally situated in capital cities. Locating plants close to metropolitan areas minimises transport costs for refrigerating the final product.

Without a significant increase in the domestic or export markets, further consolidation of the sector is the only means of getting scale. Current competition law and recent practice appears able to facilitate that consolidation, as well as preventing an unintended consequence of diminished competition as the result of that consolidation.

Several members of the Strategy Group have indicated that attempts by their companies to initiate vertical mergers (i.e. the sharing of assets in a couple of specific areas) were not progressed because independent legal advice suggested that these initiatives would be seen by ACCC as restricting industry capacity and hence would not be allowed. Strategy Group members also indicated that the consequences of having a proposed 'deal' rejected are devastating, so that a company would only take a proposal to the ACCC when it believed that it was 95 per cent likely to go through.

Notwithstanding that some proposals for the sharing of assets are never tested with the ACCC, only a few of the high-volume, low-value-add food oil and flour milling acquisitions tested with the ACCC since 2002 have been opposed (see Appendix D).

Moreover, industry feedback on these sectors from a report commissioned by DIISRTE was mixed, in regard to the benefits of increased consolidation. Stakeholders commented that consolidation keeps overheads such as head office costs down, and can allow for economies of a scale in sourcing and producing products; however, it adds to higher distribution costs, which somewhat dissipate the effects of the economies of scale.204

It should also be noted that the ACCC's Merger guidelines 2008 allow businesses that want to informally explore proposed mergers, acquisitions or asset-sharing to have a confidential discussion with the ACCC.205

Industry has also raised concerns that under section 50 (the merger provisions) of the Competition and Consumer Act 2010 (CCA), there is inadequate consideration of gains in economic efficiency that may result from a merger, or the wider public benefits a merger may bring to the economy when it applies the 'substantial lessening of competition' (SLC) test. Also with respect to manufactured foods, given international competition, a wide definition of the market is applicable

These issues have been raised by industry in the past with the Government regarding the Trade Practices Act (as the CCA was formerly known). A review was undertaken by a committee headed by Sir Daryl Dawson, a former Judge of the High Court of Australia in 2002 (the Dawson Committee). The Dawson Committee found that the merger provisions of the CCA were adequate. In relation to the third issue of industry being uncertain about the ACCC's approach to mergers and its reluctance to test the authorisation process, the Dawson Committee recommended:

  • That the ACCC should provide adequate reasons for its decisions (taking care to protect any confidentiality) in the informal clearance process when requested to do so by the parties and in cases where it has rejected a merger or accepted undertakings (Recommendation 2.1). The Dawson Committee considered that the provision of reasons in these instances would allow a better understanding of the ACCC's decisions and reduce uncertainty about the way in which the process operates.
  • The Committee also recommended the introduction of an optional formal clearance system that would operate alongside the informal system so that, should the parties so wish, the ACCC's decision with respect to a merger could be appealed to the Tribunal (Recommendation 2.2). Given the nature of the informal system, which focuses on expediency and does not require the ACCC to provide detailed reasons for its decision, there had been no course to appeal the ACCC's decision. However, parties did still have the option to seek a declaration from the Federal Court that a proposed merger would not breach section 50 (see AGL v ACCC, 2003).
  • To encourage the take-up of the authorisations process in relation to mergers, the application for authorisation of mergers should be made directly to the Tribunal.

The Government response accepted the Dawson Committee's recommendations in relation to mergers.

The Committee supported the preservation of the existing structure of the competition provisions such that the promotion of competition would continue to be the merger provisions' (i.e. section 50's) primary objective, while consideration of 'public benefit' considerations could be undertaken by virtue of the authorisation process. Further, the Committee recommended that the Competition Tribunal rather than the ACCC should undertake the broader efficiency assessment by virtue of authorisation. This has been put in place.

The Committee also considered the suggestion that a wider definition of market would enable the ACCC, in determining the likely effect of a merger on competition, to consider whether the merger would enable the merged entity to compete more effectively in a global market. The Committee noted that the issue raised there is whether the gains to the merged entity would warrant possible detriment to domestic consumers by the reduction of competition in the domestic market. The Committee considered that these matters can be considered on an application for authorisation and are best considered there. The benefits claimed for a proposed merger internationally can then be assessed alongside the impact of the proposal on domestic competition.

The ACCC has flagged a possible new process for acquisitions by Woolworths and Wesfarmers - designed to create a more streamlined system for the businesses and ensure the ACCC has relevant information up front.206

In return for advanced notice of a wider range of acquisitions, and for particular information being provided upfront with each transaction, the ACCC would establish a dedicated team to assess these transactions within specified times.

The ACCC has stated that those discussions have just begun and it remains to be seen whether or not the arrangements can be implemented. The ACCC has not indicated an intention to expand the proposed arrangements to any other businesses apart from Westfarmers and Woolworths.

Collective bargaining

Collective bargaining under the CCA is an arrangement under which two or more competitors in an industry come together to negotiate terms and conditions (which can include price) with a supplier or a customer. Ordinarily such arrangements can raise competition concerns but such arrangements can be allowed by the ACCC under the CCA where there is sufficient public interest from the arrangement.

Authorisation under the CCA allows businesses to obtain protection for arrangements that may breach the competition provisions of the Act, including collective bargaining arrangements. The assessment process for an authorisation application would ordinarily take about six months to complete but under a streamlined process - interim authorisation - for collective bargaining, the ACCC will seek to issue its final determination within three months of receiving the application for authorisation.

Since 2006, there have been six collective bargaining applications that relate to the food processing sector, mainly in relation to the dairy industry. The sectors that tend to utilise collective bargaining tend to have a fairly strong industry body representing them (such as the dairy industry).Given the potential financial benefits of collective bargaining, especially to SMEs, the comparative failure of the processed food industry to utilise this process may be explained by a lack of awareness of the collective bargaining process.

4.3.2 Labour costs are higher than in some developed and all developing countries

Australia is a country with a high cost of living which puts pressure on wage rates. Labour costs in Australia are relatively high compared with other developed countries.207

The high cost of labour makes decisions to replace labour with automation easy, despite redundancy costs. In the meat processing sector, for example, there has been significant consolidation and rationalisation in the supply chain with wholesale operations being integrated into processing activities and large-scale retailers bypassing wholesalers. This consolidation has resulted in greater efficiencies and initial job losses. However higher automation means that there is a greater need for trade and engineering skills (restructuring to a higher-paid workforce). The trend toward greater mechanisation of food and beverage processing also potentially diminishes the significance of labour costs in Australia's comparative competitiveness, but perhaps not materially so in the short term.

In the PwC industry survey, 38 businesses claimed labour costs made up 20 per cent or more of their overall costs, and 57 businesses indicated that employee numbers had increased over the past five years.

It should be noted that in many high-volume, low-value add sectors such as food oils and flour milling, employee numbers are small and capital costs high per factory, so the impact of high wages is not so great. Less than 4000 jobs exist in food oils and flour milling. While the percentage cost of Australian labour may be high, it is unclear whether this will significantly affect profit margins in the longer term, especially with greater capital investment.

Labour productivity is a key measure of economic performance. Globalisation has enabled corporations to relocate secondary operations to countries where the cost of labour and/or equipment is low. If the place of operation is also close to the market where the products will be sold, then the company benefits again from lower distributions costs.

Australia's manufacturing industry including the food processing sector, for example, has suffered because labour costs in some nearby developing countries are cheaper than the equivalent labour costs here and much of our export product is destined for the Asia-Pacific region.

Land at risk and factories expensive to set up

The area of productive land in Australia is diminishing due to increasing urbanisation and various degradation processes. For example, the National Land and Water Resources Audit's risk assessment (NLWRA, 2000) identified approximately 5.7 million hectares of land to be within regions at risk of, or affected by, dry land salinity, with this area increasing over time. Soil erosion is common in lands used for food production, with a tendency for erosion rates to be higher in the north and lower in the south-west of the continent. A further 23 million hectares of land is estimated to have acidification in subsurface layers.208 The use of emerging technologies such as GMO to facilitate the growing of grains, for example, in degraded land, represents an opportunity here.

The planning system is a crucial factor in achieving a sufficient supply of new housing and commercial developments; however, it also impacts on affordability. In Australia concerns have been expressed about the indirect and often unpredictable costs that arise from planned intervention in the land and housing market, direct costs associated with complying with building and design controls, time taken to secure approval, fees and charges for administration, infrastructure or other public services associated with development.209 The World Bank's Doing Business index ranked Australia 42 out of 183 economies for dealing with construction permits and 38 out of 183 economies for registering a property210.

Access to investment funds and new technologies are also two of the most critical inputs for the production of substantially and elaborately transformed foods as most manufacturing processes are capital-intensive. Access and costs of distribution within a region or country are also important. As a result, the location of manufactured food production facilities is not necessarily dependent on the availability of the natural resource endowments required to produce raw material inputs.211

The Prime Minister's Science, Engineering and Innovation Council (PMSEIC) commented on the fact of 'rising land prices, particularly in peri-urban areas' [i.e. those regions between urban and rural areas]212, the effect of which is, not only to cause a shift in arable land for food production, but also to increase costs to manufacturers when establishing factories and plants.

Freight costs to reach market are high

Australia is a large continent with a small population located on its coastal fringe. As a result, logistics are a major reason for preventing businesses from achieving greater sales and scale.

As noted in Chapter Three , 46 of 83 businesses that responded to the PwC industry survey identified transport as being more than 5 per cent of their overall costs. In a study of the food oil industry, transport costs in this sector were found to be 7 per cent of the overall costs.213

For a significant fraction of food freight there is no alternative to road transport, as the rail network is far less extensive than the road network. Furthermore, the flexibility and reliability of road transport is preferred for time-sensitive food freight, particularly perishable foods.

There is some concern in the industry that the consolidation of food and grocery retailers, that is, the retail 'duopoly', is affecting freight costs. In order to increase their market share, the major Australian retailers have followed European and US trends to retail liquor, fuel, newsagency products, and movement to control the food freight chain: both primary freight from the supplier to retailer's distribution centre and secondary freight from the distribution centre to the supermarket.

4.3.3 High levels of regulatory burden and inconsistent regulation

The Australia and New Zealand food regulatory system and some issues related to the system are described in Chapter Three .

Compliance with government and supplier regulation was regarded as a significant cost to businesses canvassed in the PwC industry survey. Fifty-nine businesses said that compliance with government regulations made up for 10 per cent of their costs and compliance with supplier regulations up to another 10 per cent. One Australian firm told the Strategy Group that in the past year it had conducted 51 food audits at a direct cost of $150 000 and an opportunity cost of $1 million.

In their submissions to the Senate Select Committee on Australia's Food Processing Sector, Mrs Mac's214 said that there was a need for audit and food safety consistency across the country; Summerfruit215 indicated that the regulatory environment was stifling food production; Campbell Arnott216 highlighted the complexity of the regulatory regime; Lion217 said that there were a number of regulatory regimes that pose a significant burden on the food industry, including those relating to container waste management, food labelling, preventative health and environmental requirements, as well as the proposed carbon price mechanism, and the Australian Meat Industry Council218 argued that red meat processing was under pressure in Australia due to regulatory compliance costs at a local, state and federal level, including a range of food standards, environmental regulations, occupational health and safety and export certification.

The Productivity Commission's Performance Benchmarking of Australian and New Zealand Business Regulation: Food Safety Research Report (the Report), released on 22 December 2009, benchmarked indicators of regulatory burden associated with food safety regimes across Australian jurisdictions and New Zealand. Findings of the Report included:

  • that the regulatory structure governing the food industry is complex and inconsistent
  • that little progress has been made in the area of harmonising primary production and processing regulatory regimes
  • for businesses operating in one jurisdiction, the burden of compliance varied, depending on the regulatory regime in place
  • that several regulatory differences between jurisdictions had the potential to increase costs for those businesses operating across jurisdictional borders and
  • that greater consistency could potentially increase competition and productivity, resulting in lower prices to consumers and decreased costs to the food industry.
Food labelling

Food labelling plays a critical role as a primary interface between suppliers and consumers. It is a finite space, subject to an increasing demand to contain more information.

Claims made on food labels must be accurate and verifiable. Many food labels are general in nature, based on average measurements, manufacturers' declarations (which may or may not be externally validated by laboratory testing); or - as is often the case with nutrition information panels - calculated or derived from technical literature.

Australia has two well-established, collaborative and rigorous regulatory frameworks (consumer law and food regulation) that monitor food standards and safety. Together they produce considerable net benefits to the community and aim to achieve minimum effective regulation. However, the operation of the current frameworks across a number of areas needs to be clarified.

The Australia New Zealand Food Standards Code (the Code) contains a standard requiring labelling and naming of ingredients in foods. A Memorandum of Understanding facilitates cooperation and coordination between FSANZ and the ACCC in relation to areas of overlap between the Code and the Competition and Consumer Act 2010, particularly in the area of false or misleading labels.

At the request of the Council of Australian Governments (COAG), the Australia and New Zealand Food Regulation Ministerial Council commissioned the Review of Food Labelling Law and Policy as part of the COAG National Seamless Economy reform agenda. In early 2011 an independent review panel, chaired by Dr Neal Blewett AC, completed Labelling Logic: Review of Food Labelling Law and Policy (2011) and presented 61 recommendations for consideration by the Ministerial Council. The 61 recommendations spanned a number of themes, including the policy drivers for food labelling; principles and criteria to guide government decision making on regulatory intervention, public health and safety, new technologies, consumer value issues, and compliance and enforcement.

The Legislative and Governance Forum on Food Regulation (convening as the Australia and New Zealand Food Regulation Ministerial Council, and hereafter referred to as 'the FoFR') responded to the review on 9 December 2011, accepting the majority of the recommendations.

The government's response proposed, among other things:

  • that work begin with the food industry and public health groups to develop a single front-of-pack labelling model that will assist Australian shoppers when they make choices about the food they buy
  • that standards for nutrition and health claims on food labels (such as 'low fat', 'high in fibre', etc) be improved to ensure the labels reflect public health goals and provide meaningful information to consumers
  • further advice be sought regarding labelling to provide consumers with better information about added sugars, fats and vegetable oils and
  • pregnancy warning labels on alcohol be mandated within two years noting the current industry initiatives.

The FoFR has considered the role and purpose of food labels now and into the future. In developing a response and proposed plan of action to the recommendations, the FoFR considered the terms of reference for the review, the COAG reform agenda and outcomes being sought by the recommendations.

The Australian Government has agreed to lead the development of a National Nutrition Policy that is anticipated to provide an overarching framework to identify, prioritise, drive and monitor nutrition initiatives within the context of the governments' preventive health agendas.

A National Nutrition Policy may consider a range of strategies such as defining how food labels should be used to contribute to preventive health objectives, and how issues such as vulnerable groups and Indigenous nutrition can be better addressed.

The Strategy Group values the importance of the system to maintain its clean, green and safe image but feels the label is being overused as a mechanism to achieve health outcomes with an increased regulatory cost to the industry.

Programs

While it has been noted, that some programs are best pitched at a local level (due to scale), there still remains a need for greater communication at the Commonwealth, State and Territory level to ensure programs are not duplicated unnecessarily. At present there are five programs at the Commonwealth and State Government level (Tasmania, Victoria, South Australia, Northern Territory) which aim to assist Australian SMEs to enter and develop export markets. Funding arrangements for the most part are almost identical for each program and reimburse up to 50 per cent of eligible promotion activities (including market research, trade show attendance costs, website development etc). While it cannot be argued that these programs do not provide benefit to industry, greater communication, coordination and integration, especially at the design stage of the programs, would ensure greater efficiency and potentially improved scale.

4.3.4 Competition for skilled/unskilled workers is high especially in low unemployment

A number of factors have combined to make it difficult for agricultural businesses to find and retain skilled labour. These include 'strong economic growth, the boom in well-paid jobs in the resources sector, and an ageing population'.219 Training is the responsibility of both government and companies. While it is difficult to quantify the amount that companies are investing in training their workforce, in challenging trading conditions some Strategy Group members have suggested that training budgets are seen by some firms as peripheral to core business.

According to the 92 respondents in the PwC industry survey, businesses are finding it extremely difficult to attract staff. Engineers appeared to be in particularly short demand, with 53 businesses claiming that they were difficult to attract. Seniority did not appear to play a strong role, however, as businesses claimed it was just as hard to attract plant maintenance workers (46 businesses) as managers (46 businesses).

Half of all businesses surveyed claimed that up to a quarter of their employees were engaged on a casual basis and this was more likely to be the case among businesses within the meat and fruit/vegetable industries. Twenty-nine businesses claimed that up to 25 per cent of their employees were shift workers, a further fifth of businesses claimed shift workers made up between 26-50 per cent of their workforce.

From 1978 until 2010, Australia's unemployment rate averaged 7.11 per cent reaching an historical high of 10.9 per cent in December of 1992 and a record low of 4.0 per cent in February of 2008. The unemployment rate in Australia was reported as 5.1 percent in January 2012.

The current low levels of unemployment and the competition for skilled and unskilled workers, described above, are exacerbated by the demands of the mining sector, which are impacting the food processing sector, particularly in rural and regional areas. It has also been alleged that for some time the food processing industry has suffered unfairly from an image problem, making it even harder to attract workers.

The difficulty in attracting employees appears to have driven some businesses in the food processing industry to recruit staff from overseas. Twenty-nine out of 92 businesses contacted as a result of the PwC industry survey said they were doing this to some degree although the numbers were small. Those most likely to sponsor employees were medium to large in size.

The Department of Immigration and Citizenship (DIAC) provides 457 visas for short-term workers. Industry has voiced concerns that the process for allocation of these visas is very slow. Some processors and manufacturers are now using foreign language students.

Recently, the Government announced a doubling of the period of time a skilled worker can stay in Australia to work for accredited businesses; there will also be priority processing for applications for certain employers under changes to the 457 visa system.

The changes, which came into effect on 7 November 2011, will allow the accredited businesses to bring in skilled workers for six years rather than three and also have priority processing for all future visa applications.

In order to become accredited, a business must have been an active 457 visa sponsor over the past three years, employing at least 30 overseas workers over the past 12 months, and guarantee that their local workforce is at least 75 per cent Australian.

Average processing time for 457 visa applications is 22 days, although DIAC aims to reduce processing time for 457 visas to within ten days.

4.3.5 Declining numbers of food scientists, food technologists and trained staff

Contemporary reports claim that 'formal training in agriculture and food production appears to be in decline',220 with falling enrolments in agriculture and related agricultural sciences. This observed decline raises concerns about the future availability of qualified agricultural scientists and skilled farm labour.

As outlined in Chapter Three , one of the tangible shifts in the sector in recent years has been a decline in the number of student enrolments and completions in the food sciences since 2004, while the number of student enrolments and completions in nutrition-related courses has been significantly increasing for the past few years.

It appears that fewer students enrolled to study the food sciences and that fewer completed their courses, while more students were enrolling in nutrition-related courses. As a consequence of reduced student numbers and the low level of R&D funding available for food science compared with other areas that universities operate in, there has been an erosion of food science capability within universities.

There appears to be a gap in meeting the food safety skills needs of food processing in SMEs. Suitable food safety training that can be delivered in ways that reach staff in their place of business, including regional locations, need to be developed. Unfortunately, some businesses are not aware that they have potential food safety problems. In these cases regulators can also provide valuable links to enable necessary skills development'.221

AgriFood Skills Australia222 believes that serious labour shortages in the agrifood industry pose fundamental food security challenges for Australia. In particular, the current very low number of agrifood students training in the VET system, relative to other industries, makes it unlikely that future industry labour needs will be able to be met.

In addition, the Strategy Group was told that food industry was not regarded as exciting enough to school leavers, who are unaware of the supply chain and how food is developed from 'paddock to plate'. Some Strategy Group members claimed that existing courses did not deliver the right balance of skills and the curricula did not reflect industry needs. Some courses were thought to be too academically focused with few practical subjects.

4.3.6 Vast majority of food companies are small

The vast majority of food businesses are small (less than 20 staff). When scale is small, competiveness is hard to achieve, particularly as the major retailers reduce the variety of products on their shelf in each category. 'Limited firm resources and international contacts as well as lack of requisite managerial knowledge about internationalisation have remained critical constraints to SME internationalisation. These resource limitations, especially of a financial kind, seem particularly prevalent among smaller, newly internationalising [SMEs]. SMEs from particular sectors and regions also appear to face particular international barriers.'223

Small businesses also have fewer funds to invest in innovation, and pilot equipment is expensive to access. Between 2005 and 2010 the R&D Expenditure on Food Product Manufacturing by businesses with 0-4 employees decreased significantly, by approximately 28.1 per cent, while businesses with 5-19, 20-199 and 200 or more employees increased by 107.3 per cent, 55.9 per cent and 36.8 per cent respectively.224

According to the 92 respondents to the PwC industry survey, while the majority of businesses surveyed were engaged in R&D, the investment was relatively low, with 69 businesses investing fewer than 5 per cent of their total costs on R&D.

Unfortunately, according to a CSIRO survey, there appears to be a lack of knowledge by industry about where pockets of research excellence are located and how to access them.225

Consequent to the food SME technology transfer pilot program (FoodTech), which was led by CSIRO and independently evaluated by a business called Clear Horizon in 2008, it was found that there was a need for technology transfer mechanisms to engage a broad set of stakeholders across sectors and industry segments in order to develop R&D and Technology Transfer priorities and capability plans for the food industry as a whole. Capacity and capability were found to be factors in small to medium companies' desire to innovate.

4.4 Opportunities

The Strategy Group's consideration of the opportunities and threats and these are outlined in the table below.

Table 4.2 - Environment - Opportunities and Threats

Opportunities Threats
32% increase in world population by 2050 Market structure - supermarket duopoly
Increasing middle class in Asian countries close to Australia will desire safe, quality convenient protein-based foods Sustained high Australian dollar
Lifestyle trend for convenient and tasty, healthy and sustainable foods Climate change
Reduction in post-harvest wastage Increasing energy costs
Barriers to export trade
Access to capital
Fear of emerging technologies

4.4.1 Thirty-two per cent increase in world population by 2050

Global demand for food is set to double by 2050. The Australian food sector is well placed to take advantage of this demand, especially in growing Asian markets where population growth is often accompanied by growth in incomes. Supporting Australia's food and grocery sector to develop new technologies, processes and investment was seen by the Strategy Group as an opportunity to help the industry grow.

'Global food security will demand the development and delivery of new technologies to increase food production on limited arable land and without relying on increased water and fertiliser use. In addition, the frequency and severity of climate 'shocks' are expected to increase due to the effects of climate change.

'Australia can make a significant contribution to addressing this challenge. We have extensive experience in dealing with difficult and low input food production systems. This technical and scientific expertise is valuable and well-regarded internationally. However, success in technology development and delivery requires community support. Although agriculture is one of our most productive and efficient industries it struggles to garner community support. The decline in knowledge and interest in food production has resulted in part from the urbanisation of the Australian population. This decline risks limiting industry's ability to deliver innovation to the Australian and international food industries'226.

4.4.2 Increasing middle class in countries close to Australia will desire safe, quality, convenient and protein-based foods

Emerging markets present opportunities for growth. The demand for high-value-added convenient foods will be one such opportunity, and supplying the demands of affluent consumers in Asia for more protein-rich diets is another. By 2020, the Asia-Pacific region could have more people in its middle class than the rest of the world combined. China could have a middle-class market that surpasses the United States in dollar terms. The continued rise of middle-class incomes in Asia presents Australia's food export industries with increased opportunities, as the demand for value-added products will rise.227

Australia's claim for a greater share of the Asian markets will depend on the food processing industry's capacity to increase its competitiveness and productivity. Australian companies have already proven their abilities in the past by gaining worldwide recognition for innovations in food processing. Successful businesses innovate. Innovation results in growth.

The strategy of some foreign-owned companies is to manufacture as close as possible to, or inside, the markets they supply. As a result Australia's proximity to Asia is both an opportunity and a threat. Australian firms are required to comply with tariffs and Free Trade Agreements (FTAs) whereas some of their competitors receive high levels of protection from their own governments (see Chapter Three ).

As outlined in Chapter Two, Japan remains Australia's largest food export market with approximately 17 per cent market value share in 2009-10. Indonesia has become an important export market with 8.5 per cent of the total value of food exports in 2009-10, which equates to a fourfold increase since 1990-91. Other Asian markets such as China, Thailand and the Philippines have also become more predominant importers. The share of exports going to the Republic of Korea, Singapore and Hong Kong has increased over the period.

The ASEAN group countries (Burma, Brunei Darussalam, Cambodia, Indonesia, Laos, Malaysia, Philippines, Singapore, Thailand and Vietnam) are rapidly growing in importance as destinations for Australian food exports, although a slight decline in the value share occurred in 2009-10 (down to 20 per cent).228

The food industry has been resilient throughout the economic slowdown compared to other industries due to strong growth fundamentals. Going forward, the industry is expected to continue growing, mainly driven by the Asian economies and their large populations and rising per capita income.

4.4.3 Lifestyle trends - convenient and tasty, healthy and sustainable options

Food choices are based on a complex range of drivers including convenience, health and wellness, pleasure and price. Add to this the desire for authenticity, longer shelf-life, sustainability and ethical production that have emerged as trends not only in Australia but with the rising middle classes in Asia.

Australian consumers are searching to define their personal eating styles through revising their thinking about food, and mixing and matching various food characteristics to find the ideal combinations for themselves and their families. The food value chain is strongly driven by consumer demands. Consumer trends and drivers that are likely to shape the future food value chain include:

  • consumers are dissatisfied with their current shopping environments
  • a rapid growth of online shopping
  • sustainability and other environmental issues increasingly influence shopping behaviour
  • consumers want personalised food product offerings and
  • health and wellness is a growing factor in consumer buying decisions.

In 2008, the Deloitte survey229 of 93 top businesses executives identified the following as the key consumer trends shaping the industry:

  • convenience - 66 per cent
  • 'premium-isation' - 48 per cent
  • more out-of-home consumption - 36 per cent
  • choice/selection - 23 per cent
  • freshness - 21 per cent
  • shopping experience - 20 per cent
  • prepared food - 14 per cent, and
  • regional pride/nationalism - 11 per cent.

In the PwC Australian food and beverage businesses industry survey, 11 of 92 surveyed businesses said that customer pressure on costs and the price expectations of customers were the biggest challenge facing their business. However, high levels of new product development (particularly for retail) and product renewal (particularly for health eating) with good return on investment, reasonable success rates and good levels of revenue provided for significant future opportunities.

Internationally, consumers are increasingly gaining influence and effectively getting their voices heard across the global supply chain. Food manufacturers agree consumers are becoming more empowered and commanding greater influence across the supply chain.

Manufacturers express a strong degree of confidence in consumers regarding the value of their endorsements. They consider claims made by consumers the single most valuable type of endorsement, which serves as another proof point of the growing influence of consumers. Demonstrating confidence in their own performance, food manufacturers rank their own endorsements as the next most valuable type of claim.230

4.4.4 Reduction in post-harvest wastage

As outlined in Chapter Three , waste occurs at different points in the food supply chain, although it is most readily defined at the retail and consumer stages, where outputs of the agricultural system are self-evidently 'food' for human consumption.

The post-harvest sector includes all points in the value chain from production in the field to the food being placed on a plate for consumption. Post-harvest activities include harvesting, handling, storage, processing, packaging, transport and marketing. Losses can be caused by a wide variety of factors, ranging from growing conditions to handling at retail level. In some cases everything harvested by a farmer may end up being sold to consumers. In others, losses or waste may be considerable. Occasionally, losses may be 100 per cent.

In the PwC industry survey 92 businesses were asked whether waste management was critical to the commercial, strategic or economic interests of the business. For 39 businesses in this survey, waste management was reported as critical for commercial, strategic and economic interests. The main contributing source factors to wastage included short shelf-life (15 businesses), poor process control (13 businesses) and poor quality of materials (12 businesses). A key observation was that businesses within the dairy, grain/cereal and bakery industries were more likely to claim short shelf-life as the source producing the highest level of waste within the business.

After identifying the sources of waste, businesses were then asked to demonstrate what measures had been taken to address the waste produced. Sixty-nine businesses had taken some form of action to reduce waste production. A significant proportion of those respondents (48) had changed their manufacturing or processing method to overcome issues with waste management. Another popular option for businesses to address waste production was to reuse or recycle waste products (33 businesses). Finally, approximately a quarter of respondents had sourced better-quality materials (25 businesses) - these respondents were more likely to be businesses within the meat, oil/fat, grain/cereal and beverage industries.

Considering that raw materials are the greatest input cost for manufacturers, consideration should be given to research on mechanisms to improve consistency of ingredients via improving process control, and improving shelf-life.

4.5 Threats

4.5.1 Market structure - supermarket duopoly

Australia's competition framework

The Australian Government's principal framework to promote competition in the Australian economy is the Competition and Consumer Act 2010 (CCA, formerly known as the Trade Practices Act 1974).

The CCA prohibits a number of anti-competitive practices. For example, section 46 of the CCA prohibits a corporation with a substantial degree of market power from taking advantage of that market power for an anti-competitive purpose. Broadly, that anti-competitive purpose is to either substantially damage or eliminate competitors or prevent others from engaging in competitive conduct. Other provisions of the CCA are designed to prohibit large businesses from engaging in unfair businesses practices when dealing with smaller businesses. In particular, the CCA prohibits corporations from engaging in unconscionable conduct in their dealings with other businesses. Relevant to whether a corporation has engaged in unconscionable conduct are considerations such as whether the corporation has acted in good faith in its dealings with another business, and the relative strengths of the bargaining positions of the supplier and the corporation.

In 2006 the Federal Government introduced a mandatory code of conduct for the horticulture sector, which came into effect in May 2007. This Code excludes a number of prominent agents involved in broader fruit and vegetable activities, like retailers and exporters. The implementation of this regulation has improved transparency in wholesale margins and allowed for more appropriate price setting.

The Australian Government also funds the Produce and Grocery Industry Ombudsman which can assist with disputes under the voluntary Produce and Grocery Industry Code of Conduct (PGICC).

Healthy competition

While competition is a complex issue, healthy competition provides the foundation for an internationally competitive economy and can be the catalyst to better business management, improved productivity and access to greater business opportunities. Australian consumers also benefit from lower prices and greater choice when there is strong, genuine competition between businesses.

In a perfectly competitive market, price pressures should flow through the supply chain to cause all participants (retailers, manufacturers, farmers and other suppliers) to seek productivity and quality improvements.

It is not unusual for countries like Australia (small population - large landmass) to have duopolies and oligopolies across a broad range of industries. As described in Chapter Three , there is concentration in the Australian retail food market. This can improve retailers' selling power to the consumer. This can also improve the buying power of the retailers, and the Strategy Group argues that such an outcome may disadvantage suppliers and consumers in the long run.

The numerous enquiries into the retail sector in recent years - ACCC and Senate - have all concluded that there is an acceptable level of competition within the sector. However, industry feedback suggests that those studies lacked sufficient input from manufacturers, the latter fearful of the potential consequences of raising their issues and concerns in a public or private context.

Real lower prices?

Ostensibly the main dividend of good competition is cheaper prices to consumers. The Australian consumer price index for food (excluding alcoholic beverages) increased by 3.7 per cent in 2010-11. This was close to its average of 3.9 per cent for the 10 years to 2009-10, although it should be noted that food prices in 2010-11 were affected by adverse weather conditions at that time.231 The conclusions of Choice in 2009 were that:

  • grocery prices have increased and Aldi is still cheapest
  • where you live and the intensity of retail competition in your area has a major impact on what you pay at your local supermarket
  • there is almost no difference in the average price of a standard basket of groceries at Coles and Woolworths, even when specials are included and
  • Australia had the tenth-highest food inflation of 30 OECD countries for the year to September 2009232.
Allegations brought about buying power

The Strategy Group identified the problems associated with dealing with the consequences of the duopoly in the retail world and the competition with home brand products (see Chapter Three ) as a major factor holding the development of the industry back.

In the PwC industry survey, the dominance of the two major retailers and global competition were by far the biggest challenges faced by business according to 25 respondents (32 per cent of non exporters and 27 per cent of exporters). Businesses within the meat, fruit and vegetable and sugar/confectionary industries were more likely to be affected by the actions of the large grocery retailers. Some respondents indicated that the relationship between them and the retailers had deteriorated significantly in the past few years, especially in the last nine months, and that it was now characterised by mutual distrust and a lack of partnership.

The issue of alleged abuse of market power by the major retailers was raised on numerous occasions during consultations conducted by DIISRTE and by DAFF around the National Food Plan. Fear of a possible loss of livelihood if producers and processors do not comply with the dictates of the duopoly was expressed by a number of producers.

In recent times concerns have been raised about retailers making retrospective adjustments to terms and conditions of supply, and suppliers being liable for losses due to shrinkage. The AFGC233 has claimed that the retailers' buyer power is threatening the long-term viability of suppliers by driving down prices, and that some of the weaker suppliers will be driven out of the market by the retailers. As discussed in Chapter Three , the growth of 'private labels' also appears to be adding to the problems. Some manufacturers believe that the behaviours of the retailers are impacting on their access to market, their margins and their ability to invest in capital improvements and innovation.

Some retailers have been accused of behaviours bordering on unconscionable conduct and misuse of market power. The new head of the ACCC has promised that he will look closely at the activities of the retail sector, particularly as to how it relates to suppliers.

Shifting profits

The distribution of profits along the food chain provides an insight into where productivity improvements can be made. The major retailers may be achieving increased profits while containing food prices (or only slightly increasing food prices above CPI) by extracting profits from suppliers rather than seeking internal productivity improvements. Table 4.3 below not only shows the rapid growth in net operating profit of Woolworths and Coles, but also their other operating revenue (e.g. rebates, discounts received and other) obtained from their suppliers. For those years with available data, rebates and discounts constituted a significant proportion of the retailers' profits. This compares to relative flat-lining of manufacturers profits over the past ten years (see Chapter Three ).
