Why aren't suppliers using the current legal mechanisms?

Fear of retribution may mean that even if a case for unconscionable conduct can be made, suppliers may be reluctant to bring forward complaints and be a party to any legal action. The cost, financial and time, especially for SME suppliers, is also a significant disincentive to explore this avenue. Notwithstanding the ACCC's capacity to take evidence in confidence, potential retributive action on the part of retailers, should there be a leak of confidential information, might include restricting access to market, cancellation of contracts, a reduction in the quality of trading terms, and failure to promote a supplier's product adequately, e.g. a non-optimum shelf position. Given the retailers' market share these issues can have a significant impact on any business. For this reason there may be a need for market intervention.

Possible ways forward

The long-term prognosis of continued, unmitigated retail concentration for local food and beverage manufacturing, jobs, product diversity, healthy competition and ultimately, prices paid by consumers, is unclear.

There have been developments in a number of overseas jurisdictions related to mandatory retail codes of conduct. A Groceries Supply Code of Practice (GSCOP) was recently introduced in the United Kingdom (UK). The UK's concentration of retailers is less than that of Australia. The GSCOP came into force in February 2010 and was introduced to address the UK Competition Commission's concerns that grocery retailers were adopting certain practices that transferred excessive risks and unexpected costs to suppliers. The British Government intends to introduce a bill in 2012 to create a Groceries Code Adjudicator. The European Union is considering a voluntary code of conduct that specifies unwanted behaviours.

The ACCC, once it has considered whether there has been any misuse of market power or unconscionable conduct, will be able to provide advice on the necessity of a mandatory code or any other changes to legislation to improve competition.

The Strategy Group believes it is important to have low barriers to entry in the retail environment to allow new entrants. While the Strategy Group has no particular suggestions to do this it notes the developments outlined in Chapter Three with respect to planning and zoning. This would create greater competition in the retail part of the chain providing more avenues, in addition to the export market, for manufacturers to sell their products.

4.5.2 Sustained high Australian dollar

In recent years, an increase in demand for Australian currency associated with trade and financial flows has resulted in an appreciation of the Australian dollar. This increase in demand for Australian dollars has resulted in a higher 'price' of the currency or an appreciation of the exchange rate (see Chapter Two).

To a large extent, the strength of the Australian dollar has resulted from the resources boom, high commodity prices and high interest rates due to a growing economy. This has contributed to competition from cheap imports, and confirms the view of Australian firms surveyed in 2000 that 'import penetration could increase'.

In the PwC industry survey, the strong Australian dollar was cited as a major reason why cheaper products were being sourced from overseas. As the Treasury235 noted in its submission to the Senate inquiry into the processed food industry, the value of Australia's processed food exports declined to $16.8 billion in 2009-10, $1.7 billion lower than in 2008-09. The appreciation of the Australian dollar was a key factor that would have accounted for much of this decline.236 However, growth in the value of processed food imports also appears to have flattened.

According to the Australian Food and Grocery Council (AFGC) 'the high Australian dollar is making imported food and grocery products cheaper making it harder for Australian-made products to compete on Australian supermarket shelves'.237

The cost of doing business is high in Australia and there is a lack of scale of facilities and output - so when exchange rates fluctuate or become unfavourable for a sustained period of time, the competitiveness of the sector is threatened.

4.5.3 Climate change

According to the World Economic Forum,238 one of the three clusters of risks to watch is the 'water-food-energy' nexus: 'A rapidly rising global population and growing prosperity are putting unsustainable pressures on resources. Demand for water, food and energy is expected to rise by 30 to 50 per cent in the next two decades, while economic disparities incentivize short-term responses in production and consumption that undermine long-term sustainability.

'Shortages could cause social and political instability, geopolitical conflict and irreparable environmental damage. Any strategy that focuses on one part of the water-food-energy nexus without considering its interconnections risks serious unintended consequences.

'Agriculture is the dominant water user, consuming more than 70 per cent of total global water demand. Industrially produced meat is especially water-intensive, requiring up to 20,000 litres of water to produce a kilogram, compared to approximately 1200 litres to produce a kilogram of grain. Both population growth and increasing meat consumption in emerging economies will therefore have a tremendous impact on resource needs'.

In Australia, the Garnaut Review report predicts that the impact of climate change will be severe if no effective mitigation occurs. It is likely to place stresses on water supplies and, together with changes in temperature, will have a major effect on agriculture.

'By mid-century, there would be major declines in agricultural production across much of the country. Irrigated agriculture in the Murray-Darling Basin would be likely to lose half of its annual output. This would lead to changes in our capacity to export food and a growing reliance on food imports, with associated shifts from export parity to import parity pricing ... By the close of the century, the impacts of a no-mitigation case can be expected to be profound. The increased frequency of drought, combined with decreased median rainfall and a nearly complete absence of runoff in the Murray-Darling Basin, is likely to have ended irrigated agriculture for this region. Depopulation will be under way ... Much coastal infrastructure along the early 21st century lines of settlement is likely to be at high risk of damage from storms and flooding'.239

Numerous submissions to the National Food Plan identified agricultural water and climate change as important matters likely to affect the food processing sector in Australia. Fonterra Australia Pty Ltd noted that climate change required the capacity of the food industry to be developed through climate awareness training in areas such as water efficiency, risk management tools and financial support.

Many large food manufacturers are working to improve their water efficiency. However, in most cases this is driven by rising energy costs which can be significantly reduced by improving water efficiency due to the large energy costs of water heating and waste-water treatment. Sara Lee's large bakery facility at Lisarow in NSW has implemented major water savings using biological waste-water treatment and improved water flow management, enabling the business to save approximately $1 million annually in trade waste-water costs.240

4.5.4 Increasing energy costs

The fact that food is a perishable item - there are long freight distances and cold chain practices in Australia - means that the energy costs associated with food processing can be high. Refrigeration represents the biggest energy use for most food and beverage industry businesses - from abattoirs to bakeries, cold stores, dairy processors and food, beverage and pet food manufacturers.

A number of submissions to the Senate Select Committee on the Australian Food Processing Sector, such as the Australian Meat Industry Council and Growcom (Queensland horticultural association) cited increasing energy costs as a factor likely to impact their businesses. In addition, the Tasmanian Farmers and Graziers Association noted that there seems little doubt that the cost of a price on carbon emissions will also overlay the cost of energy-intensive inputs - especially electricity at farm and in processing.

Some submissions to the National Food Plan linked food security and climate change to increasing energy costs. As Growcom pointed out, 'at some point in time the supply of oil will plateau and begin to fall (so called "peak oil"). The resulting decrease in energy availability and rapid increase in cost will have far-reaching effects on agricultural production and the wider economy'. 241

In the PwC industry survey, 57 businesses indicated that they had energy-efficiency plans in which they were investing, or were intending to invest. An example, the Tamburlaine Winery in the Hunter Valley, has slashed its power use by half by doing some simple things such as running time and thermostat controls, upgrading heat exchangers and making pipe-work modifications, reducing overall electricity use by 48 per cent and refrigeration energy use by 54 per cent. 242

The Clean Technology Food and Foundries Investment Program is a central element of the Australian Government's Clean Energy Future plan announced in July 2011. The Clean Energy Future plan provides transitional assistance for businesses and industry through the $8.6 billion Jobs and Competitiveness Program, the $1.2 billion Clean Technology Program, the $300 million Steel Transformation Plan and the $1.3 billion Coal Sector Jobs Package.

The Clean Technology Food and Foundries Investment Program is a $200 million competitive, merit-based grants program to support Australian food and foundry manufacturers to maintain competitiveness in a carbon-constrained economy. This program will provide grants for investments in energy efficient capital equipment and low-pollution technologies, processes and products.

The Strategy Group received some evidence that retailers are unlikely to pass the costs of the carbon tax on to the consumer. As a result the food processors may be required to bear the costs which they have incurred and those that have been passed onto them from the packaging and transport industries. In addition, increased fuel costs are likely to affect all areas of the supply chain from farm equipment to transport.

4.5.5 Barriers to export trade, including 'behind the border' barriers

Most processed foods are traded internationally by large multinational companies. The largest food manufacturing companies such as Nestle and Kraft operate in over 140 countries to take advantage of domestic market opportunities and to circumnavigate barriers to trade.243

Australia's processed food sector is characterised by a strong presence of such companies which are responsible for a significant proportion of the value generated in the sector. However, there are also approximately 12 500 small to medium enterprises operating in the Australian processed food sector (ABS).

Australia is an open economy, with the import and export sectors comprising 40 per cent of Gross Domestic Product244. Australia is one of the countries that have benefitted most from rising international trade, importing goods more cheaply than they could be made in Australia and selling exports that commanded rising prices because they were in international demand.

In the PwC industry survey, three in every four businesses of the 92 surveyed said they exported their products, although this was a small volume of their business (50 businesses claimed only 1 to 10 per cent of their sales). As mentioned in Chapter Three , a recurring comment made by food processors was that DAFF Biosecurity fees had significantly increased in recent years, and that this was placing pressure on exporters. Respondents said that meat export licences fees had increased from $2000 to $14 000 per annum in the last few years. Despite Australian products costing up to 40 per cent more than competitive products from Asia and Europe, sales growth had been good in Asia and the Indian Ocean Islands, especially from the food service industry.

The Australian Government is pursuing bilateral and regional trade agreements which are likely to afford significant net economic benefits and are more cost-effective than other options for reducing trade and investment barriers, including alternative forms of bilateral and regional action. In its trade negotiations the Government is seeking to reduce foreign tariff barriers and quantitative restrictions on Australian exports of primary commodities and manufactured goods.245

Nevertheless, other countries have been lowering their trade barriers at the same time as Australia, increasing the overall gains from trade liberalisation by improving market access for Australian exporters. Among Australia's major trading partners in the Asia-Pacific region, which buy 70 per cent of Australia's exports, average tariffs on all goods have been cut over the last quarter century from more than 25 per cent to around 5 per cent.246 However, agricultural trade remains distorted with average applied tariffs for agriculture being high in many countries including 13.5 per cent for the EU, 21 per cent for Japan, 10.7 per cent for Canada, 48.6 per cent for Korea, and there are 'behind the border' barriers in place due to differing regulatory systems.

Australia could provide policy assistance in neighbouring countries to help them develop appropriate regulatory systems including for GMO foods.

4.5.6 Access to capital

The availability of finance on reasonable terms allows businesses to expand their activities, fund new and innovative investments, smooth cash flows and maintain employment. Access to finance is critical for businesses to fund investment; however, capital is not easy to access from banks.

A number of studies using firm-level survey data has shown that small- and medium-sized businesses (employing less than 200 employees) not only perceive access to finance and the cost of credit to be greater obstacles than larger businesses, but these factors constrain small and medium businesses more than large businesses. This is particularly important in the processed food industry where almost 99 per cent of businesses are small- and medium-sized businesses.

In the report Exporting Australian Processed Foods: Are We Competitive?,247 45 per cent of survey respondents identified insufficient financial resources as a domestic cost inhibiting exports. This view is confirmed by more recent data provided by the Export Finance and Insurance Corporation (EFIC) Global Readiness index,248 which concludes that access to finance remains the key constraint to the offshore expansion plans of Australian businesses. It notes that in 2009, at the height of the global financial crisis, access to finance was nominated as a barrier to expansion by 58 per cent of respondents, with 34 per cent naming it their top barrier. In 2010, with global credit conditions easing, access to finance was still the top barrier overall with 43 per cent naming it as a barrier and 26 per cent naming it their number one barrier.

Similarly, in 2011, the PwC industry survey found that the cost of business expansion was one of the major inhibitors in increasing investment - 44 survey respondents nominated the cost of expansion as an inhibitor to increasing investment in their business.249

Most SMEs in the manufacturing sector fund investment through debt (borrowing) or internal finance (such as retained earnings and contributions of capital by the owner). This is in contrast to large firms which seek funding primarily through external equity markets. Only a small proportion of SMEs ever undertake external equity financing, and those that do rely on working owners for any equity contributions. Compared with large companies, small businesses tend to make greater use of debt funding and less use of equity funding; the latter is generally limited to the personal capital of the owners.250

External equity finance in the form of venture capital is generally unavailable to SMEs without strong growth prospects. This includes both venture capital funds and individual private investors such as business angels.251. As a result, SMEs face limited options to fund investment decisions compared to larger businesses.

About two-thirds of lending to small businesses is through commercial bills and other loans with variable interest rates. The remaining third of lending is at rates that are generally fixed for between one and five years.252

Lenders treat small businesses conservatively, expecting to secure loans against tangible assets, usually against residential property. This has been evident since the global financial crisis, where lending to small business has slowed, and interest margins have widened. According to the Australian Bankers' Association in its submission to the Economics Reference Committee's inquiry into access to finance by small business, this tightening of lending reflects the increase in non-performing business loans in recent years and needing to account for that increase in risk. Surveys of small businesses show that the availability and cost of finance remains a major concern.

According to the RBA, in the decade prior to the Global Financial Crisis, small business indicator rates generally moved in response to changes in the cash rate, which was the predominant driver of changes in funding costs. The average outstanding rate on small business loans declined, however, as there was a gradual fall in the perceived, and realised, risk of small business borrowers during this period (partly due to the increased use of residential property as security).

One common concern of small businesses is that interest rates on residentially secured small business loans are priced at a premium to residentially secured housing loan rates. However, according to the RBA,253 this pricing is a consequence of the higher expected losses on small business loans as well as the larger amount of capital that banks hold as a buffer against unexpected losses. In the first case, estimates based on past experience suggest that small business borrowers are more than twice as likely as standard mortgage customers to default and, in the event of default, a lender is likely to lose close to 30 per cent of the small business loan's value, compared to 20 per cent for housing loans.

As the financial crisis unfolded, the higher cost of deposits and long-term wholesale funding has seen banks' funding costs increase by more than the cash rate. There has also been an increase in the assessed riskiness of small business loans. This is consistent with the increase in non-performing small business loans, which have risen to be around 2.5 per cent of banks' total small business loan portfolios as of September 2010. This is up from a little under one per cent during 2005-07.

4.5.7 Fear of emerging technologies

Biotechnology is becoming an increasingly important part of product development in a wide variety of industries.254 It is transforming agriculture and food production, providing opportunities to produce new and improved crops, food ingredients and additives with increased value and functionality.

The expanded use of new technologies in food processing has enormous potential for the Australian processed food businesses. For example, the use of nanotechnology to extend the shelf life of foodstuffs will potentially assist Australian food manufacturers to reduce costs directly and also through saving the embedded energy and water lost in wasted food, the latter particularly important in a carbon-inhibited economy. The use of new technologies in food processing may also enhance the product differentiation of Australian processed foods, and therefore the attractiveness and competitiveness of our food exports through shelf-life enhancements and innovation, e.g. where GM food products deliver positive health outcomes.

While Australia is credited with good public research facilities, fear of new technologies and concern about customer perceptions has inhibited firms from capitalising on some of these quality research outputs. This is particularly true of GM foods.

The Centre for International Economics report, commissioned by DIISTRE, on the Australian Food Processing Industry listed the uptake and adoption of new technologies and new capital with embodied productivity improvements as a priority area for future opportunities.255 There is strong incentive for Australian biotech companies to develop new strains of GM crops due to strong global demand. Australia's biotechnology industry is currently considered to be the sixth-strongest in the world.256

On 13 May 2009, the Australian Government announced a four-year National Enabling Technologies Strategy (NETS) to provide a framework for the development of enabling technologies such as biotechnology, nanotechnology and other new technologies as they emerge in Australia. A key component of the NETS is a Public Awareness and Community Engagement Program (PACE) which includes activities such as free public forums, education materials and feedback mechanisms such as regular public attitude surveys and a free call information service. The IPSOS-Eureka Social Research Institute was commissioned as part of the PACE Program to undertake research on community attitudes to biotechnology. The project took place between December 2009 and June 2010, and involved the collection of both qualitative and quantitative data.257

In 2009-10, GM food was more highly supported than foods containing preservatives and foods grown with pesticides. While 67 per cent of the public say that GM foods are acceptable, half of those opposed would change that position if there was long-term evidence of no harm being caused. About 45 per cent of those opposed to GM foods would change their position if labelling explained what ingredients had been modified and why. This support varies, depending on the amount of GM in the food, why the modification was made and whether the food was a fruit or vegetable, or other crop.

Attitudinal statements relating to the use of biotechnology in food and agriculture revealed that Australians hold very mixed feelings on the issue, based perhaps on a limited understanding of the science. While the majority of participants (66 per cent) expressed agreement that we should use more natural ways of farming, the majority (53 per cent) disagreed with the statement 'research and development into the genetic modification of crops should be stopped', with more than a quarter (30 per cent) disagreeing strongly.

In Australia, GM foods are labelled under the Food Standards Code to help consumers make an informed choice about the food they buy. They are not labelled for safety reasons, as only those GM foods assessed by FSANZ as safe are approved for sale.

Other technologies, such as irradiation of food and nanotechnology, can also cause concern in consumers.

Food irradiation is a food safety, food preservation or quarantine measure in which food is exposed to a source of ionising energy. Standard 1.5.3 - Irradiation of Food, under the FSANZ code, prohibits the irradiation of food, or ingredients or components of food, unless a specific permission is given.258 The specific permission may impose conditions relating to matters such as dose, packaging materials, approved premises or facilities. To date, in Australia, only herbs and spices, herbal infusions and some tropical fruits have been approved to be irradiated for the purposes of pest disinfestation and decontamination. The approval process includes consideration of the technological need for the irradiation treatment and consideration of the effects on the food composition and the nutritional impact.

Community concerns about irradiation centre on vitamin and amino acid depletion in the food resulting in changes in its molecular structure forming toxic chemicals linked to cancer, organ damage, genetic mutations, etc.259 FSANZ notes that compounds generated during the irradiation of food such as 2-alkylcyclobutanones (2-ACBS) are not unique to irradiated foods, are naturally present at low levels in some foods (e.g. cashew nuts) and are formed through other conventional processing treatments (e.g. thermal processing).260 In addition, community groups are concerned about the lack of labelling requirements for irradiated products in pet food, animal feed, therapeutic goods and complementary medicines since they are not classified as 'food'. In food products, the label on a package of irradiated food must include a statement to the effect that the irradiated food has been treated with ionising radiation.261

4.6 Summary

Food processing is the biggest manufacturing industry in Australia in terms of employment and gross value add. It is a vital component of the economy. The industry makes a significant contribution to the economies of regional areas through employment, business and service opportunities. There are more than 12 800 small and medium enterprises in the sector and more than 222 000 people are employed in food and beverage manufacturing.

The Australian food processing industry faces a number of challenges and opportunities, including, the high Australian dollar; the need for productivity growth, higher input costs and the need to respond to climate change. Industry, unions, researches, academia and government need to work together to meet these challenges and harness the opportunities.

The recently released FOODmap262 analysis of the Australian food supply chain highlights some additional opportunities and challenges faced by Australia's highly competitive food market. Opportunities include emerging consumer preferences for convenience, alternative sales channels outside of the traditional retail and eating-out options, improved technologies, tailored marketing of local products and providers, and the increasing needs of the institutional food market serving the ageing, health-conscious population with an expanded range of convenient, nutritious and functional food.

Going forwards the impact of global economic forces on the exchange rate is likely to put further competitive pressure on the food supply chain. Competition between suppliers, food manufacturers, food retailers and foodservice operators is likely to compel all players along the food chain to streamline costs, improve the effectiveness of investment in innovation and optimise their performance by responding to consumer preferences for value, aspirations and convenience.

5. Recommendations

The industry transformation strategies and recommendations for the Australian processed food industry are informed by four main themes:

  • Innovation
  • New markets and the Australian brand
  • Regulatory reform and program efficiencies
  • Market place relations.

The Strategy Group has taken the strengths of the Food Processing Industry to maximise the opportunities identified in Chapter Four. It has also attempted to mitigate some of the threats and weaknesses with appropriate strategies.

This chapter will outline the key recommendations from the Strategy Group, including options, where appropriate, as well as the rationale underpinning the recommendations. Collectively these themes and recommendations provide a framework to deliver on the vision of the Food Processing Industry Strategy Group for the industry to 2020 and beyond: 'An innovative, growing, sustainable, responsive and globally competitive Australian food processing sector'.

5.1 Innovation

Recommendation 1

Industry, governments, research institutes and universities collaborate to create a Food Innovation Hub/Network, incorporating a strategic planning and intelligence function, and a virtual network

Rationale

As outlined in Chapter Four, collaboration between public research institutions, government support services and Australian processed food manufacturing business could be more strategic, commercially targeted and coordinated. There could be engagement with a greater percentage of the sector and the translation of its business problems into coherent, cost-effective R&D projects. The current state of low R&D engagement could be a key causal factor in the inconsistent and currently poor demand for government R&D pilot plants around the country, despite investment by state governments and the CSIRO in those plants.

Innovation also needs to be nurtured in the workplace through investment in training and education. Workers and businesses benefit from the 'right environment and culture, including: mutual respect; employee/employer engagement; management and leadership skills development.

While 98 per cent of Australian processed food businesses are SMEs, a large share of the employment and value derived from the sector is from multinationals. Most SMEs, while innovative by nature,263 do not necessarily have the capacity (e.g. funds) or capability (e.g. expertise) to undertake research, nutrition and market analysis.

With shrinking margins, large companies, the top 50 of which produce three-quarters of the domestic industry's revenue, cannot always afford the investment to increase their capability.264 It is perhaps unsurprising therefore that seven out of the top ten of Australia's processed food manufacturers have moved their R&D facilities to Singapore and Shanghai in recent years.

In addition, without heavy investment in developing insight into the needs of the Asian market, Australian businesses will miss the opportunity to properly target opportunities. Insight is a prerequisite for research and development and traditionally has been carried out independently of the product design and development process (see Diagram 1). The traditional focus of product design and development does not recognise that effective innovation involves a two way flow of information with new insights coming as much from each stage of innovation and brand growth. Integration of consumer insight and market understanding with prototype products and test marketing avoids this disconnection and increases the chances of market success. There are mutual benefits for SMEs and multinationals working together, but currently opportunities for collaboration are limited.

Diagram 1 - Process of Product Design and Development265


Text description for Process of Product Design and Development

The current comparative lack of SME/multinational collaborations is due to a number of factors including: geographical spread; lack of knowledge about how to collaborate, and who with; and lack of awareness of the potential benefits of R&D, reinforced in many firms by business plans that are focusing on the current state. This inhibits collaboration either between companies and researchers or between businesses. These issues are reflected in the Australia manufacturing sector's comparatively poor performance in innovation, with the proportion of large firms and SMEs with new 'to market' products well below the OECD average.266

Recommendation

The Strategy Group believes the Hub/Network concept is critical to driving competitiveness, by developing greater consumer insights (both domestic and targeted at Asian markets), collaboration in R&D and supply chain collaboration. There are a number of unique elements to a proposed Hub/Network including but not limited to:

  • ownership and oversight from industry, governments, researchers and universities - a 'quadripartite' partnership
  • central support systems, coordination and management (hence the term 'Hub')
  • linked infrastructure and programs (hence the term 'Network'), with physical and virtual focal points for innovation, information sharing and collaboration
  • firm-level information - geography, capability and capacity
  • improved deep insights into consumer needs and wants; the cultural context for potential export markets, global trends, and market and customer information
  • a business model that links consumer insights with product design, design development, manufacture, marketing and distribution and supply chain collaboration.

The Hub/Network could help link and expand the current web of capabilities spanning Australia, not all having the same range of assets, but assets applicable to the needs of the regions and targeted markets. This would include state organisations that supply innovation support to food companies (e.g. the South Australian Research and Development Institute (SARDI), Department of Agriculture and Food Western Australia (DAFWA) and Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry Queensland (DAFFQ) research organisations (CSIRO, ANSTO), universities and business support suppliers (Enterprise Connect and Business Enterprise Centres).

The Hub/Network would require skilled management, support systems and an efficient governance framework to improve awareness of and drive increased usage by SMEs and multinationals of national and state capabilities, and to deliver on key outcomes from the Hub/Network. Performance indicators would include improved productivity, margins and exports.

Enterprise Connect's Food Network business experts could have a key role here as account managers, helping to develop a new improved client experience between business and science; helping to translate business problems into R&D projects; and driving targeted and strategic engagement with industry, especially with SMEs that are currently not collaborating on R&D, and which do not necessarily see any value in doing so.

The Hub/Network could provide food companies with technical expertise in new innovative product and process development, including new nutritional functionality, packaging, shelf life evaluation and market testing/consumer insight, for the domestic and Asian markets. It could help small and medium enterprises create and test new products, from concept right up to market testing.

The Hub/Network could develop a database of consumers available for panels to undertake market testing including those recently arrived from Asian countries.

The Hub/Network could include dedicated pilot plants, laboratories, test kitchens and sensory suites coordinated by experienced professionals who work with clients to develop exciting new products, solve manufacturing issues and adopt new practices for an increasingly sophisticated food and beverage market. Services could also include consultants, including those advising on innovative ICT solutions, equipment for lease, training and advisory support, and meeting facilities. The Hub/Network could also be used to identify emerging research needs of the industry that the CSIRO and the universities could undertake. A range of open and closed innovation models may be successfully employed by industry and the research community.

Universities and TAFEs could support and utilise Hub/Network facilities for the teaching of three key disciplines (food technology, process engineering, and marketing), to develop closer linkages with industry, and for inspiring students. A benefit is that the close proximity of teaching and industry may foster additional industry-funded scholarships and attract more students into the industry.

Austrade and investors (such as business angels) could be made aware of the facilities and encouraged to help drive demand and provide capacity.

Retailers such as Coles and Woolworths may be involved, e.g. in thematic innovation on sustainability/packaging. Packaging (paper and plastic) companies may also be engaged, focusing on opportunities for innovation in extending shelf-life; reducing energy and water use.

A proactive Hub/Network would also drive demand for a self-sustaining industry collaboration, improving information exchange and knowledge between firms in the food sector, retail, packaging, transport sectors and the research/academic community.

The Hub/Network could help provide leadership in determining future research priorities. The CSIRO has begun this process and leads a National Food and Nutrition Leaders Forum reporting to the Primary Industries Standing Committee on prioritisation of food R&D.

More broadly, government may have a role in developing firm-level intelligence and mapping the complex and diverse processed food sector, contributing to better program design and more efficient delivery of services and supply chain collaboration.

The Hub/Network would produce a number of potential benefits:

  • increase the likelihood of increased overseas investment by multinationals in Australia
  • improve the level and quality of innovation by firms of all sizes, increasing both the scale and pace of national processed food innovation, and the commercialisation of that innovation
  • increase employment and competitiveness and
  • increase the supply of goods to meet the needs of the Australian market but also the growing needs of an increasing Asian middle class.

The Strategy Group has not yet developed options as how this may be funded, i.e. capital and operating costs. However membership could be subscription-based, through sponsorship or fees based on use. Without some government funding the engagement of research institutes and universities may be difficult to achieve.

Recommendation 2

Industry to develop proposals to utilise the Australian Research Council (ARC) Competitive Grants Program, particularly Linkage Funding Schemes, the proposed Food Innovation Hub/Network, and work with universities and the VET sector to promote current R&D and food industry courses.

Rationale

One of the issues that might potentially inhibit the performance of innovation in Australia is the decline of skills in the food sector. The industry needs not only to 'step change' the number and quality of graduates but also change the image of the food processing industry to help attract employees to the sector.

Key findings from Chapter Three regarding skills and training in the Australian food processing industry were that there has been a decline in domestic student demand for food science courses at university, despite most graduates finding employment in the food industry, albeit not necessarily in research but Q&A roles. There has also been a decline in the technical science and engineering component of domestic food science university courses, to the point where industry believes many graduates are not meeting its needs. There has been a downward trend in the number of students commencing VET food processing courses over the last decade, although the number finishing has been relatively stable.

The number of VET-trained food processing operators may be increased over the next five years as a result of the recent Australian Government announcement of a $7.2 billion investment to support the skills system and another $1.75 billion to support major reforms to Australia's national training system, as part of the COAG agreement to the revised National Agreement for Skills and Workforce Development (NASWD) and the National Partnership on Skills Reform (NP).267 The reforms to be delivered under the new agreement are: a national entitlement to training for working age Australians at a minimum of the first Certificate III; wider access to income-contingent loans; improved transparency through access to greater information; higher-quality training; and improved efficiency and responsiveness of the VET system.

As mentioned, industry needs technically capable R&D professionals trained in fundamental food science. Currently the deficit in suitably qualified food scientists is being met by some food manufacturers through the employment of foreign food scientists via the 457 visa mechanism. While this approach may fill a short term need, encouragement must also be given to employers to invest in the training and skills development of their current workforce and locally available labour.

The need to foster close partnerships between university-based researchers and industry members could be addressed through industry involvement in collaborative research projects supported through the Australian Research Council's (ARC) Competitive Grants Program.

Should the food industry be successful in securing funding from the program, it will still not necessarily address the generic food scientist deficit. Where possible, however, food industry members should apply for the program to support innovation and encourage skills development in their staff and graduates.

Universities, too, may be reluctant to utilise the ARC Competitive Grants Program in respect of food processing research. This is because food is usually too complex, variable and heterogeneous to be the topic of publications in top journals in comparison with work on non-food model systems, so food research rarely gets funded. In the era of Excellence in Research Australia (ERA), 268 research performance is the prime performance differentiator within and between universities, so the current limited opportunities for academics to access research funds in food processing can lead directly to a reluctance of universities to support the area of food R&D. Significant co-investment by industry in university R&D, however, would increase the competitiveness of applications to, for example, the ARC Linkage scheme and encourage universities to give a higher profile to food science and technology.

Nonetheless, Australian universities are beginning to address the related training issues. For example, the University of Queensland, in collaboration with the AFGC,269 has established a new chair of food science and technology which is a pilot program with the potential to extend to other universities. The University of Tasmania, in collaboration with DSTO and CSIRO, is also establishing a new centre for food innovation.270 These developments should assist the growth of food science research and training.

Universities are also exploring the re-engineering of their food science degree programs, to promote increased graduate numbers through offering more pathways for students enrolled in science and engineering to focus on food science and engineering during the latter part of the degree program. These changes not only reflect current student demand trends but will also assist in addressing industry concerns about the quality, currency and relevance of Australian food science graduates. Development of collaborative university/agency arrangements that provide for placement of some of these postgraduate students with CSIRO and State Government agencies will both enhance the training of the students and increase the effectiveness of a Hub/Network.

Recommendation

There needs to be a closer working relationship between the food industry and the higher education sector to promote career paths in food and food processing, and to ensure courses fulfil the training needs of industry employers.

One way of promoting food science research and industry career paths is by attracting university and TAFE participation in the Hub/Network. In addition to promoting employment in food manufacturing, this arrangement would cultivate collaborative training schemes with opportunities for students and industries in the form of industry internships for undergraduates and industry-supported Masters/PhD scholarships for postgraduates.

Many Asian food companies advertise 'food innovator' positions rather than 'food scientists', avoiding old (perhaps tired) language to describe careers in food science.

The Food Innovation Hub/Network could market ARC funded research and university programs related to food, to raise awareness of national food science R&D and development opportunities. If food technologists and scientists see improved, higher-profile career and development opportunities, they are more likely to be inspired to choose food science as a career and/or stay in Australia. The Food Innovation Hub/Network could be used to promote the VET training system.

Industry careers could be promoted to science and engineering students, by internships for mid-degree applicants and scholarships for final-year graduates. The key advantages of industry-supported research scholarships are that they fund industry-relevant research and raise awareness of potential career paths in the industry, by building close ties between public research institutions and industry.

These strategies would help to lift the profile of Australian food science careers by countering student perceptions of low R&D and low demand in the industry, and thereby assist reversal of the flow of Australian graduates into more nutrition-based and other fields or overseas.

The VET training system also needs to be more widely marketed and used within the food processing sector. Greater firm-level intelligence would assist the appropriate location and marketing of services. This could also facilitate the training system which is more responsive to the industry's needs. The Hub/Network could help facilitate this.

5.2 New Markets and the Australian Brand

Recommendation 3

Government to provide assistance and information that will facilitate exports and firm-level information be used to help match businesses to export opportunities

Rationale

As previous chapters have outlined, many Australian processed food businesses are suffering currently, with high input costs, the high Australian dollar, regulatory costs and declining margins. Driving demand in new markets helps to mitigate these impacts.

However, many Australian processed food businesses, especially SMEs, lack the capacity and capability to explore export markets. Consumer insight into Asian markets is critical to developing products that will be successful in these new markets. Access to information on Asian processed food buyers and distributors is also essential for Australian firms looking to export to those markets.

Austrade represents a considerable investment by the Commonwealth, e.g. employing up to 338 staff to represent the Asia network and 558 staff for the global network. However, currently those assets are underused by food manufacturers- e.g. leads on key Asian processed food buyers, current intelligence on Asian target export market regulatory regimes and scripts for businesses trying to navigate their way into those new export markets.

Recommendation

While Austrade provide market information/ intelligence following the introduction of their new trade business models in October 2011, more can still be done to increase awareness of the model with the Hub/Network used to facilitate this. Enterprise Connect could resurrect and upscale its 'export ready' initiative to provide firm-level information to Austrade on which companies and subsectors are ready to engage with new Asian markets. The Hub/Network could also provide information on the different regulatory requirements of target export countries.

The Austrade resource, in collaboration with other export facilitation resources nationally (state-based) could be used more effectively and strategically by industry. A more targeted promotion of market intelligence to match domestic manufacturers and export market buyers is likely to prove successful. The Austrade model since its introduction to 30 June 2012, has identified 118 opportunities in the Food and Beverages sector which could be leveraged within the Hub/Network. It is too early to assess the longer term business impact of these encouraging changes. Nonetheless, the sharing of firm-based knowledge, between Enterprise Connect and Austrade and industry, for example, remains both a long term challenge as well as significant opportunity. The Food Innovation Hub/Network could also provide businesses, especially SMEs, with opportunities to collaborate with and learn from businesses that are already successfully exporting.

Recommendation 4

Consideration be given to regulatory clarification to define substantial transformation in relation to food products, taking into consideration consumer perceptions of meaning of the term 'Australian Made' and Australia's international obligations. Industry and Government to test the reputation of Australian food in Asian markets and investigate the most appropriate brand mark for Australian products in the various Asian markets. An Asia Pacific marketing campaign to be developed by Industry to promote Australian processed foods.

Rationale

Evidence suggests that 65 per cent of Australian shoppers express a strong preference for buying Australian food.271 However, where price is a factor this preference does not always translate into consumer's purchasing behaviour.272 As Chapter Three outlined, many Australian food producers and their customers remain confused about the current rules for making country of origin claims, particularly in relation to the representation 'Made in (country)', and the interpretation of 'substantial transformation' for the food industry. This confusion is undermining the effectiveness of Australia's brand, which is based on Australia's excellent reputation for quality and food safety.

Country of origin labelling was considered by the review panel chaired by Dr Blewett AC and the response of the FOFR (both referred to in Chapter Three ). FOFR asked consumer agencies to review country of origin labelling material, and, if appropriate, develop an education campaign to clarify country of origin labelling. Since the Government's response to the report, the Treasury and DIISRTE have agreed to initiate a process to examine options for improving understanding of the meanings of country of origin claims.

Industry is arguably not sufficiently utilising the Australian brand enough, which is a quality hallmark in Australia and , based on informal feedback, internationally. There is currently a number of certification marks intended to convey the information that goods are Australian.

The Australian Made Australian Grown (AMAG) logo is a certification trade mark available to food manufacturers but it is currently used by only 212 food businesses. Alternative marks are AUSBUY (marketed by the Australian Companies Institute) and OZcompliance, also licensed from private sector providers. While some of these logos are well known by the Australian consumer, we do not know how the Asian market perceives or interprets any Australian origin claims, and whether they have any marketing power.

In 2010, the Australian Government committed $20 million over four years to the Building Brand Australia Program, to build a national branding platform that provides an overarching, strategic approach to positioning Australia in the global marketplace. While this brand is not suitable to be put on products such as food as it is more of a supplier branding tool, the label could be adapted for use in this context (international marketing tool) if working in close consultation with the sector.

Recommendation

Clarification for the term substantial transformation in relation to food products should be made in regulation. In developing the regulations, efforts should be made to understand what Australians think 'Made in Australia' means, as well as having regard to other international obligations. The regulations should help remove some of the confusion surrounding the term in relation to food.

Once regulations are in place an education campaign should be undertaken among manufacturers and then consumers.

Furthermore, country perceptions are becoming an increasingly important factor in competing successfully in an increasingly interconnected and global market. A strong, positive nation brand encourages people to choose Australia when they want to do business, buy products or invest.

Industry, including the AFGC and strategy group members, should survey Asian consumers to determine what is the most appropriate brand mark for them to relate the food to Australia, and how strong that the 'Australian brand' is in the various markets. Once completed, an Asian-Pacific marketing campaign could be developed and funded across industry and with the assistance of Austrade. The Innovation Hub/Network has a role here, both in promoting the Australian brand with Asian food processors and buyers who connect to the Hub/Network. 273

5.3 Regulatory Reform and Program Efficiencies

Recommendation 5

Taking into account the analysis and recommendations from the Productivity Commission study: Performance Benchmarking of Australian and New Zealand Business Regulation: Food Safety (December 2009), the Government review regulatory arrangements between the Commonwealth and the States, in particular undertake a cost-benefit analysis of the Commonwealth taking over regulation of food processing and safety.

Rationale

While Australia ranks highly on the world stage in the ease of setting up a business, there are a number of issues, related to government and industry food regulation, and the delivery of government food programs, which appear to be inefficient, and which add to industry costs or fail to enhance performance.

Despite efforts to encourage consistent food safety regulations across the States and Territories based on the national Food Regulation Agreement (including recommendations made by the 2008 Productivity Commission Report on Regulatory Burdens on Business: Manufacturing & Distributive Trades), each state maintains different regulations, institutes separate audit processes, and has differing levels of priority and resources dedicated to enforcement. These variations impose substantial competitive disadvantages on compliant businesses operating in multiple jurisdictions, in addition to larger compliance costs for those that trade across boundaries. The 2008 Productivity Commission report recommended that unnecessary regulatory burdens that are most costly to productivity be addressed first; however, it noted that businesses are largely unable to cost separately the unnecessary parts of the regulatory burden.

In addition, differences of policy opinion across the various jurisdictions mean that policy development process is often unnecessarily long. Current treaty arrangements also allow New Zealand potentially and problematically to opt out of outcomes from regulatory initiatives, e.g. the mandatory fortification of flour for bread making with folic acid under Standard 2.1.1. This can result in a lower regulatory burden in New Zealand, yet products are still able to be brought into Australia from New Zealand without complying with all of the Australian laws. This provides a further potential incentive (beyond lower costs and a more favourable exchange rate) for Australian companies to relocate to New Zealand.

Recommendation

There is no doubt that Australian processed food manufacturers are currently experiencing very tough trading conditions. There is evidence that poorly designed, disparate food safety regulations that do not necessarily deliver on their intended purpose and which take a significant amount of time to finalise add significantly to the costs of business. A cost-benefit analysis of current regulatory arrangements will help evaluate the current approach and any potential for efficiency gains through greater Commonwealth oversight and control of the regulation of food processing and safety.

Recommendation 6

Government to identify all programs at Commonwealth and state level and to identify opportunities to improve the business design of such programs to ensure that they complement each other and are delivered more efficiently.

Rationale

Each jurisdiction - commonwealth, states and territories, and sometimes discrete areas within a state, e.g. the Gippsland Food Plan - have their own programs and food plans which on their own may prove successful. However, often these programs lack sufficient scale. In addition, the lack of integration between these discrete programs means that there may be significant potential for duplication of effort, or conversely that good local initiatives aren't reproduced nationally.

Recommendation

Governments at all levels need to work collaboratively to develop a shared understanding of the purpose of delivering particular programs, and to design opportunities that help ensure that they are delivered in the most efficient manner possible, and at a sufficient scale to ensure that they produce the best economic impact.

Recommendation 7

AFGC convene a forum to undertake a cost-benefit analysis of the current level of quality assurance (QA) auditing of the processed food industry and to develop recommendations on alternative QA options that reduce the regulatory burden on business - retail and manufacturing - without compromising food standards.

Rationale

A number of retailing businesses and fast-food establishments have their own QA standards which they apply to suppliers e.g. BRC Global Standard, WQA Woolworths. The value of such standards is not disputed as they ensure that Australia's reputation for clean, safe food is maintained. However, given that the information sought by audits undertaken across the various standards appears to be identical by up to 80 percent, the number of audits appears to be excessive, as does the significant direct and indirect costs.

Food manufacturers supplying branded and private label products for the major supermarket and retail food service providers have a mandatory requirement to comply with the retailer's QA programs. These requirements involve different QA certification of state and territory food acts, but with substantial duplication (approximately 80 per cent), and also require periodic audits. Consultation with manufacturers has indicated that large manufacturers may face up to 50 QA audits per year, incurring direct costs of over $100 000 and imposing indirect costs several times higher.

Recommendation

Woolworths Ltd's Head of Trade relations recently acknowledged this problem in the company's evidence to the Senate Select Committee on the Australian Processed Food Industry, and tacitly supports the recommendation: 'If there is some agency out there that can bring the various schemes together to a level that allows us to be satisfied with the outcome - that it is no less robust than the process we have in place today. We do not seek to impose any additional costs on any of our suppliers because at the end of the day it gets passed on to the consumer. We would like to be as efficient and effective as possible. I am not sure what the mechanism is to reach that point, but we would support getting to a point that is more effective and efficient than today's'.274

Industry advice is that retailer endorsement of the British Retail Consortium (BRC) food safety governance model would be a good starting point. Almost all food quality specialists in Australia would be aware of BRC. There is a great degree of crossover between all QA/food safety systems because ultimately they all converge with global best practice.

Recommendation 8

Governments develop a statement explaining their approach to the use of emerging raw material and process technologies in food, which supports the safe development and application of emerging technologies.

Rationale

The safety of foods containing emerging technologies, such as Gene technology, irradiation and nanotechnology that have passed all current safety tests and regulations, is continually being questioned. This was most recently raised by recommendations from the Blewitt Food Labelling review, potentially jeopardising investment in one of the few technical options Australia and the world have currently to address some of the pressing future challenges and opportunities for the food sector, e.g. health problems, a burgeoning human population; peak phosphorous among others. Governments and opposition parties overseas have openly endorsed the application of emerging technologies, e.g. in the UK.275

Recommendation

To seriously address the role of emerging technologies in enabling continued food security, in terms of rapid global economic and environmental change, will require a confident whole-of-government approach. The framework for establishing such a consensus already exists. As part of the National Enabling Technologies Strategy mechanisms exist in Government to respond to issues relating to emerging technologies. The Health, Safety and Environment Working Group coordinate the Australian Government's work on Enabling Technologies. In 2008, the working group developed and released the statement 'Australian Government Approach to the Responsible Management of Nanotechnology'.

Emerging technologies that might improve the consistency and quality of raw material inputs, thereby reducing cost and wastage, should not be discounted because of the lack of public understanding of these technologies.

5.4 Marketplace Relations

Recommendation 9

The food processing industry, together with the retailers, to review the effectiveness of the Produce and Grocery Industry Code of Conduct (PGICC) to see if it is fit for purpose or if an enhanced Code and/or a stronger role for an Ombudsman is required to effect behavioural change in the retail sector.

Rationale

As discussed in Chapters Three and Four, the structure of the market means that retailers are able to drive a very hard bargain with their suppliers, possibly to the eventual demise of the Australian processed food sector. Manufacturers require enough profit to invest in innovation and productivity improvements. Smaller businesses currently need to seek other outlets for the sale of their goods as they find supplying to extremely large retailers challenging. While food manufacturers can consider seeking export opportunities as a relief from the difficult trading conditions, exporting too has its challenges, especially with a high Australian dollar.

States should continue to ensure there are low barriers to entry of retailers. Retailers should look to their own operations to gain productivity improvements, equally as hard as seeking price reductions from their suppliers. Retailers should also recognise that they need Australian manufacturers and, should economic conditions change all of a sudden, such as a large drop in the Australian dollar, imports will be more expensive, and they could turn to an Australian industry that may have disappeared.

The ACCC's current investigations need to play out to determine if there has been a misuse of market power and any unconscionable conduct. Australian consumers are likely to punish retailers with lower sales if retailers are found to be guilty. In the main Australians prefer Australian food - it is a brand they trust for quality and safety.

In the meantime, manufacturers need to deal with very powerful buyers.

Recommendation

There have been calls for the Government to review the effectiveness of the PGICC, most recently through the Senate Economics References Committee report on the impacts of supermarket prices decisions on the dairy industry (2011 Dairy Report).

The final Government response to the 2011 Dairy Report (3 February 2012) states 'It is appropriate that industry review the effectiveness of the Code. The Government is willing to continue to engage with industry about the operation and effectiveness of the Code and would consider facilitating an industry/Government partnership to review the Code.'

The Chair of the Code Administration Committee (CAC) has written to the Committee to determine if there is support among its members to initiate such a review. The Strategy Group agrees with the review and agrees that ex-CAC members such as the Australian Food and Grocery Council should participate in the review. The Government should help industry facilitate the review.

The ACCC investigations will determine if the laws have been broken. The ACCC may also make recommendations for changes in the law from its investigations.

Should an enhanced voluntary code be insufficient to change behaviours, a mandatory code of conduct could be introduced, with an Ombudsman's role that goes beyond dispute resolution as described in Chapter Four.

Recommendation 10

DIISRTE will work, in consultation with the ACCC and with industry bodies to increase industry awareness of the authorisations process for mergers and collective bargaining under the Competition and Consumer Act 2010 (CCA) as a means of achieving economies of scale and cost reductions through mergers, acquisitions, asset sharing, and collective bargaining.

Rationale

Processed food manufacturers, especially those in high-volume, low-value sectors that are experiencing difficult trading conditions, could seek larger economies of scale to balance power relationships or utilise collective bargaining provisions available under the Competition and Consumer Act 2010 (CCA).

As outlined in Chapter Four, industry feedback has suggested the sharing of assets has not progressed because independent legal advice suggests that these initiatives would be seen by ACCC as restricting industry capacity and hence would not be allowed. The key issue here is section 50 of the CCA which prevents any mergers and acquisitions that would significantly lessen competition.

Collective bargaining under the CCA is an arrangement under which two or more competitors in an industry come together to negotiate terms and conditions (which can include price) with a supplier or a customer.

Recommendation

It is possible that merger cases which potentially fail the 'substantial lessening of competition' test (in section 50) could be allowed to proceed on the grounds of public interest. That is, an authorisation application might have been made successfully under section 95AU. It should also be noted that the ACCC's Merger guidelines 2008 allows businesses that want to informally explore proposed mergers, acquisitions or asset sharing to have a confidential 'fire-side' chat with the ACCC:

'If merger parties believe their merger proposal will meet the notification threshold, they are encouraged to approach the ACCC on a confidential and informal basis as soon as there is a real likelihood that the merger may proceed to discuss possible competition issues and options for having the matter considered.'276

Currently, there are only isolated instances of food manufacturers using the collective bargaining provisions of the CCA to help reduce their input costs which, as Chapter Three has demonstrated, are significant. Manufacturers, especially SMEs, should be encouraged to explore this option as a potentially important means of reducing costs.

5.5 Summary

No doubt Australian food and beverage manufacturers will continue to struggle with the high value of the Australian dollar for the foreseeable future, as well as Australia's comparatively small market, and the relatively high cost of inputs. However, the Australian processed food sector also has the capacity, capability and opportunity to become a 'food superpower in Asia' in the coming decades.277

'Innovation is the key to making Australia more productive and more competitive. It is also fundamental to answering the challenge of climate change, the challenge of national security, the age-old challenges of disease and want.

The strategies recommended in this chapter are, in large part, aimed at building and supporting innovation. The other recommendations are focussed on making sure regulation is not stifling business productivity and that it is effective and efficient. The recommendations are also aimed at making sure businesses are well-informed and supported when looking to expand through export markets.

The strategies focussed on innovation support collaboration between public research institutions and Australian processed food manufacturing businesses as well as making sure government support services are more strategic and coordinated. Part of this is making sure businesses are fully informed and can more easily access Government programs and services.

When it comes to innovation and emerging technologies, it is recommended that there be greater engagement across Government and with the Australian public. Any further adoption of emerging technologies by businesses within the sector would be better supported by an educated and well-informed populace. Similarly, greater transparency and clarification of what Australian made means will mean that processing firms selling Australian products are able to fully capitalise on that brand and the Australian public can be more confident in their choice to buy Australian made.

The current design of government and industry food related regulation is often criticised for being inefficient and adding significant costs to industry, for instance, regulation of food processing and safety. Government programs designed to support the sector also often lack sufficient scale to provide long-lasting economic benefits. Some programs also appear to be designed in isolation, ignoring the potential benefits of greater design integration with existing programs and with little firm-level information to improve efficiency and effectiveness. There appears therefore to be potential to improve the design of both regulation and government programs, reducing costs to business and delivering improved, collaborative and more long lasting economic benefits.

Finally, in terms of marketplace relations, Australian food and beverage manufacturers operate in a highly competitive environment with large retailers seeking to drive a hard bargain. The strategies recommended in this chapter, among other things, seek to improve relations between retailers and processors, and seek to increase industry awareness of opportunities and processes that may be available to them to achieve economies of scale through, for instance, asset sharing.

Whether it be through working with public research institutions and universities to create greater linkages between industry and researchers or working closely with Government in identifying regulatory or program inefficiencies that are impeding industry efforts, industry has a pivotal role in all of the strategies.

Figure 2.1: Value Chain for Food in Australia, 2010-11

Is a value chain chart displaying the key components of the food supply chain in 2010-11:

  • Farm and fish food production worth 40.7 billion dollars comprised of Meat at 33 per cent, Grains and oil seeds at 30 per cent, Fruit and Vegetables at 18 per cent, Milk at 10 per cent, Seafood at 5 per cent and other at 4 per cent.
  • Food and beverage processing (2009-10 data) worth 82 billion dollars comprised of Meat at 24 per cent, Dairy products at 16 per cent, Flour, cereal and products at 8 per cent, Beverages at 13 per cent, Bakery products at 6 per cent, Fruit and Vegetables at 7 per cent and other foods at 26 per cent.
  • Retail food sales worth 130.4 billion dollars comprised of supermarkets and grocery stores at 62 per cent, takeaway outlets at 11 per cent, liquor retailing at 7 per cent, cafes and restaurants at 14 per cent and other food comprised 6 per cent.
  • Exports worth 27.1 billion dollars comprised of Meat at 26 per cent, Grains at 28 per cent, Dairy products at 9 per cent, Wine at 7 per cent, Seafood at 4 per cent and other at 26 per cent.
  • Imports worth 10.6 billion dollars comprised of Beverages at 19 per cent, Seafood at 12 per cent, Horticulture at 17 per cent and other at 52 per cent.

  • For the non-employing group category there were 192 entries and 245 exits in 2007-08, 213 entries and 281 exits in 2008-09, 230 entries and 173 exits in 2009-10 and 193 entries and 210 exits in 2010-11.

  • For the 20-199 employee group category there were 9 entries and 21 exits in 2007-08, 3 entries and 15 exits in 2008-09, 6 entries and 9 exits in 2009-10 and 6 entries and 12 exits in 2010-11.
  • For the 200+ employee group category there were no entries and no exits in 2007-08, no entries and no exits in 2008-09, 3 entries and 3 exits in 2009-10 and no entries and no exits in 2010-11.

Figure 2.8: Growth in Gross Value Added by Category

Is a line graph displaying the growth in gross value added by category from 2000 to 2011. The following categories are described:

  • Food, beverage and tobacco products
  • Manufacturing
  • Total gross value add

From 2000 to 2011, 12 overall GVA grew 37.6 per cent, while foodand beverage and total manufacturing GVA grew by 8.4 and 10.5 per cent respectively. As a result ofthis divergence, food and beverage GVA as a share of total GDP has decreased from 2.3 per cent in2000 to 1.9 per cent in 2011.

Return to Growth in Gross Value Added by Category

Figure 2.9: Food, Beverage and Tobacco Gross Value Added and Gross Domestic Product

Is a line graph displaying the gross value added and gross domestic product for the food, beverage and tobacco industry for the following markets:

  • Japan
  • United States
  • South Korea and
  • Australia

From 1980 to 2009, the Japanese food and processing sector went from over 3.0 per centof the economy to around 2.5 per cent, while in Australia it went from 3.0 per cent to 1.8 per cent.The United States (US) has also seen a similar decline, with the food and beverage processing sectordecreasing from over 2.0 per cent to just below 1.5 per cent. Of these countries, however, South Koreahas by far seen the most dramatic shift with food and beverage processing decreasing from3.5 per cent to just over 1.0 per cent of GDP.

Figure 2.28: Industry Value Added by Employee by Selected Industry Groups, 2008-2009

Figure 2.31: Annual Change in Exports

Is a bar chart which reports the annual percentage change in exports at an aggregated level. Change in exports of food products is highly sensitive to prices. The chart shows annual growth in exports ofprocessed foods increases, from -10.5 per cent in the pessimistic scenario (low industry productivity)to -1 per cent in the business-as-usual scenario (average industry productivity) to 9 per cent in theoptimistic scenario (high industry productivity), indicating that growth in exportsis highly influenced by productivity growth in the food processing industry, as it leads to falls in pricesand hence enables manufacturers and their products to be more price-competitive internationally.

Figure 3.5: Regions with Strong Comparative Advantage (2007)

Is a series of bar charts showing the countries with strong revealed comparative advantage.It should be noted that the scales are different for each graph. The charts reveal that Australia has a strong comparative advantage in beef (represented as bovine meat), sheep meat (represented as cattle/sheep), dairy, wine (represented as beverage or tobacco) and wheat. In comparison, New Zealand has a very strong comparative advantage in most food categories, especially in dairy and beef. The countries represented in the bar graphs include New Zealand, Latin America, Australia, Canada, United States and Eastern Europe.

Return to Regions with Strong Comparative Advantage (2007)

Figure 3.6: Regions with moderate to little advantage (2007)

Is a series of bar charts showing the regions with moderate to little comparative advantage.It should be noted that the scales are different for each graph. The countries represented in the bar graphs include India, South Africa, European Union, Middle Eastern Africa, South East Asia and China.

Notably, China had no comparative advantage in any processed and unprocessed food products listed, except for livestock.

Return to Regions with moderate to little advantage (2007)

Figure 3.7: Regions with no Comparative Advantage (2007)

Shows two bar charts for the Rest of Asia and Japan that has no comparative advantage.It should be noted that the scales are different for each graph.

The graphs show that South-East Asian region has no comparative advantage in processed foodproducts where Australia had a comparative advantage, such as dairy, beef, or other processed meats.Japan had no comparativeadvantage in any food products and is a major importer ofprocessed and unprocessed food from Australia.

Return to Regions with no Comparative Advantage (2007)

Figure 3.17: Global Consumer Megatrends
  • HEALTH & WELLNESS
    People of all ages are more proactively addressing their health in more holistic and personalized manner.
  • SENSORY & INDULGENCE
    The driving force behind continually higher expectations of quality, variety, and immersive ‘consumption experiences’.
  • COMFORT & UNCERTAINTY
    Consumers value safety, tradition, simplicity, and reassurance due to rising stress and uncertainty in their lives and the world around them.
  • SUSTAINABILITY & ETHICS
    Mounting awareness and concern surrounds the scale, complexity, and interdependence of shared social and environmental challenges globally.
  • INDIVIDUALISM & EXPRESSION
    Values emphasizing individuality and self-expression guide confident and empowered modern consumers.
  • EVOLVING LANDSCAPES
    The underlying and mostly dependable socio-demographic and economic trends impacting consumer societies.
  • SMART & CONNECTED
    In a hyper-connected, technology-enabled society, consumers seamlessly integrate the use of multiple technologies in their lives and buying behaviour.
  • EASY & AFFORDABLE
    Time and money are perceived to be the great scarcities of modern life as consumers search for ways to maximize these limited personal resources.

Return to Global Consumer Megatrends
